The Red Page
“The Bulletin’s” £1700 Prize Story Competition.
This country, rich in short stories, is suffering and always has suffered from
a great dearth of full-length Australian novels. The book-stores and -stalls
are in consequence overflowing with novels written and published elsewhere,
and too often advertising some other country.
“The Bulletin,” which has been responsible for popularising the short
Story in Australia, and has created a school of short-story writers, realises that,
the time has come when something should be done for the long story and
the long-story writer. It therefore announces a competition with prizes
so substantial that it believes the very best of which Australia is capable will
be forthcoming.
A MINIMUM OF £1700 WILL BE DISTRIBUTED.
The First Competition will close on June JO, 1928, at The Bulletin
Office, Sydney.
PRIZES.
First .. .. .. ... .... .. £500
Second .. .. .. .. ., 125
Third .. 75
Further Competitions will close in 1929 and 1930, the prizes for which will
be not less than £500 each competition.
The prize stories are to be available for publication serially in The
Bulletin and or the Woman's Mirror; but apart from'that the copyright of
every story will be reserved to the writer, to whom all profits on the publication
of the story in book-form will belong. It is expected that, as the result of
winning the competition, the prize stories will have ‘a very large sale in
Australia and Great Britain.
Picture rights also will belong absolutely to the writer.
Competitors must be Australian-born or bona fide resident in Australia.
(Australia, for the purposes of this paragraph, includes New Zealand and the
Southern Pacific.)
Details with regard to the judges will be announced later. There will
probably be five, including the Editors of The Bulletin and the Woman's •
Mirror; and the A.J.A. (Australian Journalists’ Association) will be invited-*
to nominate one.
Membcis of Jhe Blllktin and Woman s Mirror staffs will not be eligible
to compete.
Every story for competition .must be submitted under a nom de plume;
and the name and address ot the writer, with the nom de plume, must
accompany it in a sealed envelope, which will not be opened until after the
stories have been judged.
Stories submitted for competition must not have been previously published;'
The Nature-poetry of Elsie Cole
Lord Balfour once said, “I would rather
be, known as having added to the , sum of
our knowledge of the truth of Nature than
anything else I can imagine.” I suppose he
would have liked to be a nature-poet. The
Cromagnard who drew the picture of a
bison on the wall of his pleistocene cave
was a nature-artist. You can make some
sorts of poets and some sorts of artists, but
not the “nature” variety; they must be
born, with a sixth sense inherited from the
great god Pan. It is this gift that qualifies
much of the poetry of Elsie Cole (Mrs.
A. 11. Angel). And that is the reason that
it is poetry, and not merely excellent verse.
The nature-poet is not a person who
simply catalogues what he sees in a museum,
or out of it. Elsie Cole does not cata-
logue. If she mentions an animal or a plant
or a geographical feature she wastes no
time in telling how' many legs or leaves or
sandhills it possesses. She tells you, or
causes you to imagine for yourself, what it
feels and thinks and does. It may seem
far-fetched, or even absurd, to talk in the
firfft person of the joys and sorrows of an
Actinia rosea; but you will hardly feel that
it is so when you have read “Anemone”:—
All these I see,
T, the Anemone, the furled Anemone.
Red as live blood, pursed like the lips of
pride.
I alow on the dim edge, and, water free,
Wait in a trance of patience the returning
tide,
Small, smooth and motionless, fettered,
supine;
Yet not the. ship-spilled rubies that may
shine
Among the rose and pearl of unknown
shells
In the pellucid depths where quiet-dwells
Hold richer fire than mine!
And it is only one possessing the sixth sense
who could give us such an impression of
the bush as is conveyed in “May Night”:—
The ebon moments, cold of wing,
For multitudinous blossoming,
A dust of finest water-seed bestrew.
That dawn may draw from trembling buds
of crystal
Miracle flowers of dew.
So late! So black! Yet all the bush
With chirp and dart and blunt-nosed push.
With stir of busy to-and-fro is rife;.:
The forest folk play in a sweet absorption
The rigorous game of life.
And even as terrific day
In flames of golden disarray
Brightens to glory of a heaven-wide birth,
The little pink and white soft-fisted mush-
rooms
Reach up, and break the earth!
Who has said that better before? Or as
well? And it is all true, though none but.
a nature-poet would have noticed it.
“The Children of Joy” appeared in The
Bulletin in 1916, but before that Elsie
Cole had produced a volume of verse called
“Holiday Songs,” from which Professor
Murdoch - chose- “The Song of the Foot-
track” to put in his Anthology. Neither of
these is amongst her very .best, but 1 they
show an effortless capacity for getting in-
side nature and taking the reader there, too.
It was “The Children of Jov” that first
drew my own attention to “E.C.C.’s” verse,
and that not so much on account of the
good workmanship as because one could see
that whoever wrote it \vas a friend of
; . the riotous poppies,
Tim little wild poppies,- ■ - .
The unafraid poppies that dance on the
hill,
And one saw. also, in the whole poem, an
underlying knowledge of the individuality
oi the flowers of which the poet'sang and
of their kindly offices to man.
Elsie Cole’s almost pantheistic love of
Is ature crops up all through her writings.
At times it would almost seem as if she her-
self were unconscious of the fact. In order
to be a natme-poet it is not enough to have
profound appreciations; one must be able
to express them in language worthy of the
beauty of the subjects. “E.C.C.”' is fully
equipped for this duty; and, although "she
is no phrase-maker, some of her expressions,
once read, are not likely to be forgotten by
any friend of Pan.
One of her longest nature-poems is
Sand,” and.,here again she uses the first
person singular:—
1 am the Sand, the Had of Things ; in many
places many lined.
\et through all guises still the same. I
weave the death-clothes of the World!
And in lighter vein, in another poem:—
Too rich am I to care ; l saw a thing to-dav
Whose perfect artistry would sorrow's self
make gay—
A wheel of cobweb, fairly wrought as
Heaven s wheel of stars ;
In a sweet fern were hung its vvise, fine
bars.
Each thread had a thousand drops, each drop
had the light
And majesty of all the suns that color day
and night.
Fly, .griefs, one singing hour, until vour
sly return.
I found a lovely Toy of God, between two
fronds of fern!
And this is the way she ends one verse of
: “If ?J,.Were Queen”:-- .
One windy day
1 would slip away
lo the rough bark of the great tree-trunks,
my palms against their boles.
To the pleasant crunch of slipping sand
y beneath my bare foot-soles!
Here is a whole poem that perhaps expresses
what she wants to say when she is feeling
a bit dismal for once:—
THE COMFORTER.
The sunshine speaks a. heating word .
To those who walk in grief,
And tenderer sound hath no man heard
Than lisp of leaf On leaf.
But take your sorrow to the ground—
Face downward on the grass
- Ten thousand tiny threads of sound
Through your soothed soul shall pass.
Ten thousand tiny scented sighs
ln a vague vast lament
Shall charm the tears from your hot eyes
Till their dry fires be spent.
Go seek the teeming breast of Earth,
Beat it with childish hands ;
Old bleeding Earth, torn Mother Earth,
Alas! she understands!
There is seldom a word in Elsie Cole’s
poems that does not fit snugly in its place,
and not a sentence nor a simile that cannot
be understood by that much-enduring
person the Ordinary Individual.
C. H. SOUTER

“ The Little Aristocrat ” evokes
both admiration for itself and
respect for its owner.
'T'HE Erskine Six is a newcomer, but it is built
1 and backed by Studebaker, a name that has
stood for satisfaction and honest value for seventy-
five years.
Perfected after two years of experimenting and ex-
treme road tests, Studebaker confidently presents
this sturdy little Aristocrat, knowing that economy
has not impaired its quality. From its lustrous
finish to die heart of its chassis —every part has
been designed for stamina in all emergencies;
rugged endurance and thorough-going depend-
ability for long, lasting service.
A little gem—of pronounced distinction, sparkling
performance and exceptional economy; but only
as you sit behind its wheel, with the miles slipping
silently behind, can you realise how much charm
this little thoroughbred holds for you.

Riviera at the most attractive time of the
year. They may also leave the Orient

England for the Spring.

Marcus Clarke
Marcus Clarke’s misfortune in having been
forced by a lack of cash to write “For the
Term of His Natural Life” may not be as
great as “Hamer” seems to think (R.P.
28/7/’27). Admittedly he exposed in Hugo-
esque nrose a diabolical page of history;
but the fact that it was created by the
notorious mishandling of English convicts
by English prison officers, with the com-
placent. connivance of the purely English
society then at the head of this country,
does not seem to leave “Hamer” and others
like him at ease; as it should, considering
that it leaves clear the conscience of Aus-
tralia. Especially should this fact be borne
in mind, now that Australia’s first “big
motion picture is to be launched upon the
world with the stamp of its birthplace fresh
upon it.
There is no need for this uneasiness. At
the time the book was published—when
Australia was still a “colony” in the minds
of English gentry, and consequently even
less a recipient of respect than she is now,
and when discredit cast upon English meth-
ods and men was considerably more un-
popular and hotly resented than to-day—
even at that time the London book reviews
were full of the most gratifying of all forms
of praise, the startled, reluctant praise of
men surprised and compelled to eulogy.
“This—from Australia! Who the devil is
Marcus Clarke, and where is the country
that produced such a man?” is the key-
note of the notices of that time—too long
to reproduce here. And amongst men whose
positions and reputations are too great for
them to be affected by any light shed upon
themselves or their country, so long as its
purpose be laudable, Marcus Clarke’s stand-
ing is immovable and eternal. .Even if it
were written for cash, and even its theme
reflects about as little credit upon anybody
as ever a novel theme did, its writing re-
sulted in no small kudos both to author
and to the country he adopted.
Probably the public taste for the morbid
and gruesome is responsible for the promi-
nence given to “His Natural Life.” And
of course this interest has been revived
by the unfortunate choice of theme for
Australia’s first big picture. But amongst
Clarke’s short stories, now almost forgotten
by his adopted country, are works com-
parable to those of Dickens. In “Pretty
Dick,” for instance, and “Poor Joe,”
“Gentleman George’s Bride” and his “Bul-
locktown” stories he is as realistic as Victor
Hugo, as penetratingly imaginative as Cos-
mo Hamilton; and he gives us as true and
vivid a picture of the Australian Bush as
any Australian-born bush-writer has ever
penned. Beside the crudity, too, of style
and the slapstick humor of, say, Steele
Rudd, or the bewildering, if clever, “psycho-
logical ’ bush-writing of Lawrence, his prose
is clear, arresting, and polished to a degree.
“He was not an Australian by birth or
sympathy,” laments “Hamer,” seeming to
make that the basis for an unsympathetic
appreciation of Clarke’s work and life.
Michael Arlen is not an Englishman, and
Karel Capek, the Czecho-Slovakian, writes
in English, and many another “foreigner”
shines unmolested in the English literary
firmament. Moreover, Pamela Travers, an
Australian girl; Dale Collins, a product of
the same portion of the earth’s surface;
Alfred O’Shea, the singer, equally a com-
patriot of ours, are all accepted and
acclaimed in England. Accidents of birth
carry little weight amongst the big men.
For that matter, it is difficult to see why,
if he had not been very strongly imbued
Avith Australian sentiments and held by
strong Australian ties, Clarke did not accept
the offer to go to London, to start on a
high rung of the journalistic ladder in that
city, made to him by the proprietor of the
then brilliantly written Daily Telegraph.
Clarke refused that offer, and remained in
Australia to the day of his death, writing
very beautifulty and kindly of Australians
and the Bush; thereby giving his adopted
land a position in the literary world which
she seems to have done little enough to
deserve, judging by accounts of Clarke’s
not too happy life and poor circumstances.
Marcus Clarke apparently belonged to a
type common enough to psychologists—the
restless and intelligent adolescent, bitten
by the writing bug. Happiness is rarely
the lot of this sort; but greatness is always
possible and in many cases probable. The
incidents of his life that “Hamer” treats
with a complete lack of sympathy or under-
standing are perfectly comprehensible when
one considers Clarke’s personality, his ex-
periences in London before he came to
Australia, and the, to him, irritating and
futile environment in which he found him-
self when he did land. In fact, in reading
of the “Cave of Adullam,” that intriguing
Bohemian cafe of Melbourne in those days,
his unpractical generosity (he was often
cuff-linkless, to the advantage of some im-
pudent “toucher”!), his tiff with the bishop,
via articles in the papers, and his own
delightful account of his bookish and argu-
mentative three years on the cattle-station,
when “his conversion of the station-hands
to a taste for books and reading and their
subsequent neglect of their work, led to
him and his employer parting company,”
one obtains an extraordinary impression of
vitality and intellectual energy that could
not be suppressed.
The first Tuesday in August was his anni-
versary day. His daughter is in Sydney
now in the thick of theatrical work. She
has received many letters from .men in
South Australia gnd New South Wales who
both liked young Marcus when, as a good-
looking English youth of 17, he made
his entrance into our land, and who were
with him when he passed out, leaving us
one of our few literary glories and tradi-
tions.
PHILIP BENN.
An Australian National Theatre
“Columba” (R.P. 21/4/’27) thinks a back-
ground of legend and tradition is necessary
for a National Theatre. I don’t agree,
though I think his idea of exploring the
abo. mind for legends and lore is admirable,
and it should be done before it is too late
to make anything but a mess of it. The
turning to Irish folk-lore at the dawn of
the Abbey Theatre was somewhat political:
at that time in Ireland the present and
the future held nothing; what glory there
was shone out from the past, and to .it
naturally the intellectuals turned for in-
spiration. But whatever its beginnings, the
type of play produced now by the Irish
Theatre does not savor of legend at all.
The plays are reflections of the tragedy
and comedy of the life around. Therein
lies their interest for the audience; the
Abbey Theatre is a national mirror in which
the Irish may view themselves in all their
garbs—a decided advantage, though natu-
rally not always appreciated.
My plea is for something similar in Aus-
fralia. Edward McNulty’s farce “The Lord
Mayor” is great fun, but its equivalent is
in not one, but a hundred places in Aus-
tralia; our Australian politics and poli-
ticians, when not tragic, are mostly scream-
ingly funny; what comedies of injured
dignity is a royal visit not responsible for!
The vast extent of our continent provides,
too, for a growing variety of dramatic treat-
ment —North Queensland, with its sugar-
cane Kanakas first and now its Italians;
Tasmania, in its chastened English setting;
the pearl fisheries, with their polyglot popu-
lation; the Chinese, in their varied walks
of life; the slums of our great cities; their
Tooraks; the country towns, the mines, the
small farms, the sheep-stations and, more
colorful than all, the vast cattle-runs of the
Never Never, with their wonderful type
of bush men and women; the Afghans and
camel teams; the half-castes and abos.—-
what tragedy and comedy must be in it
all and how little our city-bred folk are
brought in contact with its variety and
problems! Neither does our past lack the
dramatic: blacks, bushrangers, explorers,
convicts and pioneers jostle each other in
a crowd all calling for something more than
a passing word. Most of it is so tragic
indeed that some of the world’s deepest
sorrows lie hidden in our historical past.
Even nature, with its background of
drought and flood, tornado and bush-fire,
provides a thrill of the uncertain and un-
expected lacking in more sober climes. In
a land, too, free from tradition like ours,
nothing is made to pattern; individuality
becomes more marked; there is in conse-
quence a greater freedom in the develop-
ment of personality among the masses of
the population, and dramatic situations tend
to multiply accordingly.
We have the writers. When Gregan Mc-
Mahon, some years ago, called for an
Australian play, he was snowed under with
efforts from Sydney alone. They were
probably mostly bad, but it is a marvel
that with such little opportunity and en-
couragement. so many should show an urge
to write.
If money is not forthcoming to commence
in lavish style there are many ways of
making a humble beginning. Not long ago
I saw performed in the open air the
“Medea” of Euripides by members of the
Dublin Drama League; the beautiful sur-
roundings reminded me of Sydney, but a
certain chilliness in the air was in .contrast
to our balmy days and nights. Australia
is a land admirably suited for out-door
theatricals, but with an utter lack of origi-
nality we cling to the stuffy atmosphere of
Old World theatres built as protection from
rain and cold. Could we not take classic
Greece for our model and give, when suit-
able, Australian plays in the open? Then,
too, the dramatic world is on the lookout
for new ideas. We may be able to work
out in time a type of play somewhat suited
to new needs. W T e cannot copy, blindly,
other countries; in a matter such as this
each one must work out its own salvation.
Our universities, growing in vigor each
year, should take some interest in the fos-
tering of a national culture and prove there-
by that they are more than training-grounds
for the professions. By ’ showing interest
in movements such as this, they would raise
themselves to the cultural level of the best
Old W’orld universities and do an eminent
service for Australia.
DUBLIN.
K. McE. R.
Art and the Movies
Mr. Richard Meredith (R.P. 7/7/’27) is
very eager to disprove my case, and in
his hurry has sometimes trod where the
seraphim might pause. In outlining my
thesis I argued that the interposition of
the machine in the process of film-making
was fatal to “high art”—a term forced Upon
one by the poverty of the English vocabu-
lary. Mr. Meredith urges that my point
applies equally to etching. His analogy
between the “machinery” of etching and
the camera is, however, a false one, the
proper parallel being, of course, that of
the film and the photograph of an etching;
and the whole point of my previous article
may be summed up in the affirmation that
an etching is a better thing, artistically,
than a photograph of it.
Mr. Meredith confesses that he thinks
the screen actor is as well able to convey
meaning by gesture and expression as the
actor on the stage. It suggests that he is
in danger of mistaking his grandmother’s
photograph for his grandmother herself;
for he is regarding the shadow as equal
in the capacities of life to the breathing-
being.
Mr. Meredith, by some fantastic method
of his own, argues that if the films cannot
tell a story, then peither can the novel,
“let alone the redoubtable etching.” I am
perfectly willing to admit that the films
can tell a story, but I insist that they do
not approach the novel in narrative power.
Children can tell stories, but they do not
tell them as well as Victor Hugo. A child,
or a moving picture, might give us a ver-
sion of “Hamlet,” but that version could
never be as adequate as Shakspeare’s. How,
for instance, would a movie actor give us
in sign and gesture the full significance of
Hamlet’s changing moods? Could Barry-
more convey, without the use of words,
the deepest meaning of the soliloquies?
And would Mr. Meredith suggest that we
would understand Hamlet and Hamlet’s
story better if we reduced the theme to
the skeleton that can be represented on the
films, the mere physical incidents of the
affair?
The fact that the films have to use
words to explain the simplest developments
destroys Mr. Meredith’s contention. His
“art” is dependent from the outset on the
art of the literary man who writes the
titles, just as it is dependent on the material
of literature for its motives. It is as if
the etcher had to explain himself in a
musical form; as if the only way a poet
could write a lyric was to design it as a
color scheme.
In short, the movies are not independent
and self-substantial; and if Mr. Meredith’s
reading in aesthetics has carried him as far
as Lessing, it is strange that he misses the
significance of such a fact.
D. P. McGUIRE.
A Satchel of Books
The Man Behind the Curtain, by J. M.
Walsh (Cornstalk Publishing CoQ. An Aus-
tralian edition of a novel by the author of
“The Key Above the Door.” A detective
story of the better class.
The Divine Lady, by E. Barrington
(Harrap; Robertson and Mullens). A cheap
reprint of the blend of history and fiction
for which this lady has a reputation, here
applied to the love story of Lord Nelson
and the housemaid who, developing into a
society beauty, became Lady Hamilton.
These Things, by A. M. P. Smithson
(Fisher Unwin). The Emerald Isle is very
dear to the heart of Annie Smithson, and in
this romance of a dancer she returns to her
old love. A pleasant homely Dook with deft
sketches of character and a sentimental love
theme.
Emily's Quest, by L. M. Montgomery
.(Cornstalk Publishing Co.). An Australian
edition of a story that will be welcomed by
readers of “Emily of New Moon” and “Emily
Climbs.” A homely story of a side ot
American life that, seemingly, still persists
beyond the roar of New York and Chicago.
The Invocation of the Holy Spirit in the
Prayer of Consecration, by ,T. W. Hunkin
(Ileffer). The author, having given the
New Testament accounts of the institution
of tin' Lord’s supper, traces its history in
relation to the English Church since the
Reformation. Of interest at the present
time in view of the revision of the Book of
Common Prayer, in which it is proposed to
provide for an alternative Communion office.
The Creator Spirit, by Charles E. Raven
(Martin Hopkinson). A survey of Christian
doctrine in the light of biology, psychology
and mysticism, being the substance of lec-
tures delivered at Cambridge and Harvard.
The book is presented as an “effort to for-
mulate and defend a Christ-centred view of
the universe in such wise as to heal the
breach between science and religion.” The
importance of such an effort, he considers,
must be evident “to all who realise the
disastrous effects of the divorce between the
old religion and the new learning.” But
not all admit that divorce: very many scien-
tists see science and religion on parallel
planes so that they do not collide. The book is,
nevertheless, a useful contribution to modern
thought in theology.
Cape to Cairo, by Stella Court Treaty
(Harrap). The record of a motor journey
made by the author and her husband. The
journey had been attempted before but not
accomplished. The pioneer, Captain Kelsey,
made the attempt in 1013-14, but had a
series of mishaps. Everything seemed to go
against him, and finally, when hunting a
leopard in long grass in Rhodesia and wound-
ing it, the infuriated beast turned on and
fatally mauled him. The present adven-
turers, a party of six in all, were more fortu-
nate, a fact largely due to good organisation
in providing for food dumps and dumps of
spare parts, petrol and oil along the trail.
The book is richly illustrated and the letter-
press written in a vivacious style, convey-
ing a body of interesting information.
Canberra's First Hundred Years and
After, by F. W. Robinson (Penfold). A
second and enlarged edition with eight new
illustrations. Originally intended as a sketch
of the local Anglican church and its growth,
this account of the Federal Capital has
developed into a social history of Canberra
and its neighborhood for the last 100 years.
The author has a few words to say on the
question of the disputed pronunciation of
the Capital’s name. “Nor is there any
doubt,” he says, “that in pronunciation
Canberra should be accented on the first
syllable, and almost omit the e in the
second. A happily illiterate witness to a
marriage in 1840 wrote the name phonetic-
ally as he knew it, and spelt it ‘Canbrey.’
This is good evidence,”

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Skylarks.
In the glory of the morning,
When the dew is on the grass.
And the mists from hill and hollow
vv reatliing, rolling, rise and pass,
H you care to go a-strolling
Through Centennial Park you’ll hear,
above you, skylarks singing.
Singing, singing sweet and clear.
Flowers about you will invite you
I o behold their radiant hues,
But you 11 turn from their allurement,
from their purples, reds and blues,
lor the rich down-thrown bewitchment
Of each upward-circling lark.
As it mounts to greet the morning.
Singing, singing o'er the park.
Trees and lakes, the while you listen
To those minstrels for a space,
Will in vain assert their beauty '
And their rare, compelling grace;
Spelled you'll be, enthralled by rapture,
Sense and spirit upward borne,
As you listen to the skylarks.
Singing, singing in tlie morn.
When you leave the park behind you,
Seeking what the day's round sends—
M hat of failure, what of triumph,
What of fate that mars or mends—
Ever, ever, like an echo
Borne from regions far apart.
Ton will hear the skylarks singing,
Singing, singing in your heart.
N.S.W.
RODERIG QUINN.
From a Train.
Suddenly, after wastes of wild
(irey and sullen brown,
We came upon a quiet field
Where the sheep lay down.
Snow-white sheep on a w r et, dark field,
With a still tree beyond,
And the fat bodies of four ducks
Ruffling a golden pond.
All suddenly, out of the hushed
Thick darknesses of night,
A carillon of bells we heard
In a gleaming flight.
Shaking their rhythm down the sky
In a bright cloud of sound,
Like the soft beat of breasting doves
Over the muffled ground.
And suddenly all else was gone
Save Beauty aching on and on.
MAORILAND.
UNA CURRIE.
The Little Track at Kuringai.
Ine little track goes up the hill,
Following always its own will—
Between tall rocks in cloaks of grey,
Tike Quakers kneeling down to pray;
under the she-oak’s dusky hair
That folds it from the still, blue air
And falls so thickly on the ground
That feet upon it make no sound;
Beneath the wings of wild sea-hawks
I hat hover where the lone moon Walks
the little track goes up the hill,
following always its own will:
W hiding and winding out of sight
Into the Silence and the Light.
N.S.W.
PETER PIPER.
Brisbane River.
Blight Prussian-blue the winding' river lies -
And here some milk-white seagulls. lik<’
a reef
Found in warm seas beneath pale, tropic
skies,
.Rest in mid-stream. Dusk caves, of bough
and leaf
Seem sunk in On'c,Tree Hill, for all the air
is clear. Some mage has made a crystal
ball
Of the round world. Would you the Future
dare,
Read Fate in that fair crystal! Life mav
call
You to a high emprise on morns like this,
O l ' woo.you to the following of a face,’
ihat leans its golden; beauty for a kiss,
Or draws you blindfold to some secret
place.
Are the gods weaving visions from a tower
Ot rainbow vapors? Does the blue June sky
\ eil some proud goddess in a star-strung
bower,
A crystal-gazer where our earth-liills lie,
W hile we make moving shadows in the ball?
So lull of godhead do we feel to-day.
She may have mortal longings, after all.
To wash her white feet in our sunlit bay.
Scarlet poinsettias on their supple rods
T W rite her a message with flamboyant pen
Fntil she wonders, wistfully, do gods
Of high Olympus love as*well as men!
Maybe, with chin cupped in a rosy hand;
She dreams to buy a lover with a star,
And aches to be a daughter of the land,
For earth must seem so lovely, being far.
Fvon to those who walk the shining ways
And have the great twin-rivers of the sky,
Is anything more regal than our bays—
Or Rrisbane River where the white gulls
lie?
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
Margaret.
A window set with little squares
At which the swaying rose-sprays tap;
A cool, pink room, where gentle airs,
That ail the garden odors trap,
Have layered sweetness ages old
That on each thing within is set:
Ilow all these stir the thoughts of gold,
01 days when I loved Margaret!
Hers was the sweetness of the flowers,
The fresh young beauty of the rose;
Her mirth the light of sunny hours
That on the apple blossom glows;
Her home was Lden ere the sin
Brought thoughts to hide and to forget;
And I find sweetness still within
The days when I loved Margaret.
She loved me with a love that laughed,
Not passion-touched, but soft and kind;
And in those gentle hours I quaffed
A cup whose nectar now I find
Is than the hectic wines
That all my veins on lire set —
Ah, still a clean white sunlight shines
On days when I loved Margaret!
From roads of passion and its pain,
i The orgies of life’s restless riot,
Coo! rooms like this may draw again
My soul, and steep it in its quiet,
Its charm of scented; unseen flowers
And happiness without regret;
And memory lingers in those hours
When youngly 1 loved Margaret.
N.S.W.
THE FREQUENT LOVER.
Smoke.
Strolling out at sundown,
It is pleasant to espy
Threads of smoke that upward curl
Against the evening sky;
Smoke that rises softly from
The little chimneys brown
Of homely little house? at
The outskirts of the towm.
Just across the way, the smoke,
So blue against the trees,
Dances in a blithesome way
With every passing breeze: ■
Such a cheery house it is— \ ■
Or so it seems to me—
As children with a merry shout
Come trooping home to tea.
I wonder if in yon .poor place
One breaks his bread alone?
The smoke’s so low upon the roof,
And so forlornly blown.
But there’s the little red-roofed house
Where friends watch out for me!
Can t you see the steady smoke
Of glad expectancy?
victoria.
LUCY HILL.
The Mermaid.
Her hair is as green as the weed that grows
And sways in the wash of the tide that flows
by her island lone and bare,
A\'here the north-west wind for ever blows,
\\ here the forge of the lee King brightly
glows,
An island that only the sea-bird knows
As he wheels and circles there.
1 here she sits through' the lonely day
Combing her hair mid the flying spray
On that island lone and bare;
\\ ith a comb of gold and a glass, they say,
As crystal clear as the morning ray
Of the sun as he leaves his midnight way
And rides through the eastern air.
She combs her hair, and still she sings
A sweet, unending tune that rings
Along the whitened wave.
Its story is that the seagull brings,
The salt breeze made the tune, and flings
It upward, onward, till it wings
Into an airy grave.
She sits and sings through the summer night.
When the south is. ablaze, with a cross of
light
Above the whitened wave,
A lullaby then, but in winter’s height
When curling seas and tempests fight,
She sings a paean of' wild delight
To the organ winds that rflve.
QUEENSLAND.
W. T. MILLER.
The Impenitent.
Ilis sins-were scarlet; that I knew,
And, trembling at the Judgment Throne,
I deemed my errors small and few
Reside the faults that he must own.
For T have led a careful life,
Eschewing all that’s mad and wild;
V faithful husband to my wife.
To parents an obedient child.
Rut he was wilful, rash and blind,
Knowing no guidance, so ’twonhi seem;
No laws to keen, no vows to bind,
Riving a fevered nightmare dream.
Yet on that Ray, to my surprise,
lie grovelled not. nor looted low.
ITe looked his Lord straight in the eyes
And spoke out proudly: “God, Yon know!”

CRICKET.
"If they did not voluntarily observe a decision given by a proper tribunal they must be compelled to observe it. Governments could not legalise those strikes, and
if there was a defiance of the law of the land the one thing they had to do was to see that the law was observed.” — Prime Minister Bruce at Innisfail, Queensland.
CAPTAIN AUSTRALIA: “But you must'obey the umpire, man!"
PLAYER SOUTH JOHNSTONE: “What! Obey the umpire when the blankard gives a man out!"

Special Notice.
All correspondence to be addressed to Box 2521 8.8.,
G.P.0., Sydney.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
gart of the contents of this number is hereby expressly
reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned or preserved unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive

THURSDAY, AUGUST 18, 1927. ‘
OUTLAW THE PRE-SELECTION
SWINDLE!
So many crimes are committed in the name
of pre-selection that the putrid thing should
be put down by law. There is not a shred
of excuse for it in communities with adult
suffrage and preferential voting; when such
a community, called upon to elect its repre-
sentatives, permits insignificant, and perhaps
corrupt, minorities to restrict it to a choice
between their particular pets, it stultifies
and injures itself. Reputable citizens who
are prepared to stand, at their own expense,
as Nationalists, Laborites or Agrarians and
undertake to play the game—on the hust-
ings by asking the electors to vote in the
order of their preference for the party’s
other candidates, if any, and in Parlia-
ment by supnorting its programme and
leader and resigning when they can no
longer do so—should be entitled to have
their names placed on the ticket. Where
a name is excluded, the excluding body
should be obliged, on demand, to give its
reasons and take the consequences if the
reasons are false. This may seem a des-
perate remedy, but the disease is desperate,
in N. S. Wales especially. Able and cour-
ageous men are sorely needed in Sydney’s
saddening Leg. Assembly, but these are
the very candidates whom pre-selection, as
the Ma State knows it, excludes.
* * * *
There is nothing to choose in this matter
between the National Federation and the
A.L.P. Nationalists sneer at the other
side’s selection ballots, but their own bob-
a-nob system is just as bad in its way,
although there may be no stoushing of
scrutineers and the like. In some districts
men of parts have been rejected at a
mockery of a plebiscite in favor of dismal
duds whom the party could well spare. In
Bondi, 10 members of “an electorate
council” ordered Jaques, M.L.A., a man
with four years’ service in the A.I.F. and
seven years in Parliament, to stand aside
for Miss Preston Stanley, who has been in
Parliament for two years and had no very
creditable attendance record during her first
year. In Willoughby, Sanders, M.L.A., was
selected at one meeting of an electorate
council by 14 votes to 12, and rejected at
another, also by 14 votes to 12. In Dulwich
Hill, Hoskins, M.L.A., asserts that he was
defrauded of the nomination, since his
supporters in the leagues were not allowed
to vote; and the Mayor of Waverley has
withdrawn from the ballot in his district,
alleging “chicanery” and other nasty things.
Of the Labor proceedings in Sydney, let
the Worker speak:—
“Never were selection ballots so rottenly
conducted. The whole scheme was so flag-
rantly faked that most of the candidates
who entered the contests in known antago-
nism to the dominant faction had no chance
whatever; they were doomed from the start.
Ballot papers suddenly became unobtainable.
Perfectly valid votes were challenged and
disallowed. Intimidation was resorted to.
Even the Red gang’s own rules were vio-
lated. It was stipulated that before mem-
bers of the A.W.U. could participate they
must have signed the roll-books of the
leagues operating in the districts where they
live, though Rule 119 specifically exempts
them from this procedure. The Seale execu-
tive fixed a date limit which rendered com-
pliance with this regulation impossible, and
thus robbed thousands of A.W.U. men of
their votes.”
Loughi.in, Mutch, McTiernan and Evatt
were the brains of the Parliamentary Labor
party, which, incidentally, got thousands of
votes two years ago because it had brains.
But for the selection-ballot system, as con-
trolled by the gentry who have “cleaned up
the ‘movement,’ ” these four men and their
Sydney followers could have gone before their
constituents as Labor men and appealed
with confidence for support against the
Reds. As it is, they have all, or nearly all,
been driven out of their party, most of
them by methods . such as the Worker
describes, leaving it so poor in material, so
barren of ability, that it has become a public
danger on that account alone, Thus the
selection-ballot system produces and sus-
tains tricksters and dummies; it sacrifices
men with the capacity to think and the
courage to speak out.
* * * ' *
The record of the Agrarians is compara-
tively honorable. In the Commonwealth
and three States they have stood firmly
against pre-selection, and to this fact they
owe no small part of their success. Under
the mistaken impression that optional pre-
ferential voting would render pre-selection
necessary, some of their N.S.W. branches
recently took ballots of their members, but
they did it reluctantly, nor was their example
universally followed—one branch, indeed,
endorsed three nominations for a single seat.
Their politicians will now strive to get pre-
ferential voting made obligatory, as beyond
question it should be. Let them also strive
to get pre-selection outlawed. Gratitude
and an honorable place in history await
the party which can say that through its
exertions chained and gagged representa-
tives were set free to serve the people and
constituencies were re-endowed with rights
of which they had been robbed by the
machines.
THE SHIP WITH A CRAZY CREW.
Another year’s trade returns have beep
compiled—to June 30—and they are the
most remarkable of a remarkable series. It
is necessary to make an adjustment in
order to arrive at their true value. In
1924-25 Australia, or the Commonwealth
Bank, borrowed from foreign banks and
imported 101 million sovereigns to meet a
crisis which threatened. They were ex-
ported again last year, the threat having
passed. The transactions thus balanced one
another and have nothing to do with the
national productiveness or solvency or any-
thing else, and if they are left out each
year appears at its true value. The result
is that 1926-27 stands forth as the year with
the greatest mass of imports in this coun-
try’s record; as the worst save one for the
failure of exports to pay for imports (the
shortage was over £30.000,000); and as the
very worst in the way of failure to make
exports pay for imports and cover the ever-
growing ' account for interest and other
annual liabilities which fall due abroad
(when everything is added up the 12
months’ production failed to meet the 12
months’ liabilities by over £61.000.000). As
the inevitable result it was a period of
much unemployment, for a nation can t
import goods and earn wages by making
them at the same time; and it was a period
of the most wholesale borrowing (apart
from the war loan days) that Australia has
ever known. Added to all else, it was a
time of deficits, loud Communistic talk,
job control and political corruption.
S: * * *
The average Australian evidently fails
to realise that his finance is in a category
by itself, and is the kind of thing at which
all the world would wonder if the world had
time and a desire to go into the matter.
This can be illustrated when Common-
wealth trade figures are compared with
those of some other English-speaking
countries which also have external debts
to provide for. As regards the other
Dominions the statistics are up to the latest
year for which the year-books furnish
details—no nearer than. 1925 in South
Africa’s case:—

family, these figures being Australia’s also

There has of late been magnificent borrow-
ing to fill the gap, and the bill which the
Federal Statistician describes as “approxi-
mate annual obligations abroad for interest
and services”—the one which surplus exports
should cover but don’t—grows more and
more unmanageable. This is the amount
as nearly as possible:—

It is a bill that never stops growing.
* t- ; i= #
In Canada, which is considerably more
populous than Australia, the nine Provinces
got along at recent advices with a com-
bined expenditure of £28,000,000, and South
Africa’s provincial Governments managed
on £9,200,000, of which more than half was
a subsidy from the Central Government.
The six Australian States spent £103,600,000
in 1926-27, or about £6-1,500,000 if railway
and tramway expenses were deducted, or
£36,200,000 if railway and tramway expenses
and interest and charges on the public debt
were all left out. That is to say, they spent
that sum and £33,258,000 of loan money to
boot, only about two-sevenths of this being
for railway construction. The South African
provinces have no separate debts, whatever
they borrow being from the paternal
Administration at Pretoria. Canada works
on the Australian plan, in evidence of which
Quebec, which is more populous than N. S.
Wales and over twice the size, had an
accumulated debt of £11,580,000 by last
accounts. N. S. Wales spent almost that
amount of loan money in the year 1925-26
alone, and its entire debt was £223,000.000,
of which railways and tramways represented
a little more than half. Yet the Ma State’s
accumulated lOU at that date (June, 1926),
was a little less than the Australian average
in proportion to population. The Federal
debt in Canada is somewhat greater than in
Australia, though there are 20,581 miles of
Federal railway as a set-off against 1733
miles here, but the outstanding feature is
that there is something apart from new loan
funds to pay interest with.
* * * *
The small and to some extent brown and
tattooed neighbor across the Tasman Sea
is far from being a model of early financial
piety, yet he has been content with a miti-
gated jag during the past few stormy years.
In the absence of later complete statistics,
which won’t be available for a long while
yet, the following are some figures to go on
with:—
Increase of Gross Debt per Head, Six Years,
1920-26.

Victoria .. 24 8 0
The position would be nothing to boast of
even if it showed some slight signs of
improvement, but any such signs are want-
ing, though the strenuous efforts of the
Bruce-Page Government to regulate the
trade in lOU’s may lead to something. Thus
far everything else merely leads to inevit-
able smash; for six years to 1925-26 the
deficit in exports to meet outside liabilities
averaged about £32,000.000 per annum, and
the rise to £61,000.000 for the 12 months
just ended is staggering.
ALL THE WAY FROM ALMA TO
ALASKA.
Premier McCormack, of Queensland, was
lately heard of from Canada, where he was
busy being interviewed. There he is
credited with the statement that the Domi-
nion’s fate is to be swallowed up by its
big and aggressive neighbor, the United
States. It might not be a very tactful
observation, yet it might certainly be pro-
phetic. Canada has U.S. to the south, a
projecting bit of U.S. to the south-east, U.S.
on the north-west, and a very long strip of
Alaska, the severed dependency of the U.S..
on the west, cutting off the upper half of
British Columbia from the sea. So absorp-
tion in the U.S., which is an absorbent re-
public, would have, on the face of things,
some commercial advantages.
The story of Alaska is one which deals
with the misfortune of having the Free-
trade craze badly, and also of backing the
wrong horse. Nearly 120 years ago a son
was born to Louis Bonaparte, brother of
Napoleon 1., and to his generally absent
queen, Hortense. At least a son was bom
to her. In his declining years, and he de-
clined very much, it was alleged that the
offspring must be a true Bonaparte because
he suffered from cancer of the stomach, the
disease which carried off his illustrious
uncle. As his complaint proved to be stone
in the bladder, the identification fell
through.
The son spent a great, part of his early
life in exile, and he spoke French with a
thick German accent to the end of his days.
After a series of complicated adventures,
in and out of gaol, he became President of
the French Republic, and later, by one
supreme and bloodstained adventure, he
became a dictator and next an einpero'.
and called himself Napoleon 111. Great
Britain was then in the first enthusiasm of
the Freetrade movement, and Nap. 111. had
courted her assiduously by professing to be
the man who yearned more than all other
men put together to throw open the French
markets to everything British. The rackety
Foreign Minister Palmerston hastened to
offer friendship, recognition and congratula-
tion. Napoleon 111. was the first monarch
to be habitually photographed, and his
photographs give a general impression that
something was wrong with the hang of his
pants.
In the course of 19 stormy years, this
fresh and dubious Bonaparte, who was no
general, made war in China, Russia, Mexico
and places in between. He was generally
reputed to be “warring for an idea,” but
the idea was hard to find. His first effort,
however, was to some extent an effort of
damaged vanity, and the results were far-
reaching.
Nicholas, Czar of Russia and recognised
in a general way as political head and
guardian of the Orthodox Greek faith, gave
the new adventurer the glassy eye and the
supercilious observation. The new adven-
turer, whose whole life was a pose, struck
a noble attitude as a devout son of the
Church, demanded that the custody of the
Holy Places at and around Jerusalem should
be transferred from Greek to Roman
Catholic priests, and made a fuss. Con-
sidering that the Turkish Empire covered
the original home of the Greek Church, and
that the R.C. population was a small min-
ority, the claim seemed ridiculous; but the
Emperor was insistent. Possibly he wanted,
in his unaccountable way, to get square for
his reputed uncle’s retreat from Moscow.
Wherefore the Sultan, who was alleged
to be weak in the head and addicted to
spirituous and malt liquors, being roared
at from both sides took what he thought
to be the line of least resistance, and the
purple-clad new arrival had his way. Beimi
roared at something extra after this by (In'
infuriated Russian, the Sultan had an extra
drink and declared war. The French joined
him because, after making the war, they
could hardly leave a -weak-minded acquaint-
ance in the lurch. The British did the
same thing, partly because they had begun
to acquire the strange impression that it
would be fatal if Russia secured Constanti-
nople or any other ice-free port on open
water, and partly because they hated to
leave an alleged Freetrade friend in the
soup. The various parties grew so wild that
they weren’t content with chasing the
rather harmless Russian off Abdul Medjid's
cabbage patch, but pursued him into the
Crimea, where the troops of five nations
froze horribly on a horrid peninsula for
the sake of nothing in particular. It wasn’t
the kind of war which could have been
averted by a disarmament policy, for the
five nations were hardly armed at all accord-
ing to twentieth-century ideas. They
mobbed each other and froze, and, like the
British in the Peninsular War or the
Yankees in their Civil Commotion, they
learned the art as they went along.
One result was the uplift of Prussia, whose
tail was exalted on finding that her eastern
neighbor couldn’t even defend his own terri-
tory. Another was that the Unspeakable
Turk secured a new lease of life and ot
Western friendship, which helped him to
prove a rather unpleasant enemy in 1914
and after. Still another was that Russia,
being apparently bowled out in Europe,
went to look more strenuously for an ice-
free port in Asia, and bumped into Japan,
with consequences from which it never re-
covered. And a not able result was that
when Russia decided to sell its North
American estate, which looked of small
value until railroads began to be built, it
made its offer to the United States instead
of to Great Britain, whose Canadian pro-
perty adjoined, because it didn't feel in-
clined. after the Crimean episode, to have
any dealings with J. Bull at all.
So it comes to pass that Canada is to a
very- considerable extent surrounded by
United States, and, as McCormack ol
Queensland stated with his usual penetra-
tion, the position is precarious.
SOLOMON MALAPROF.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
Uncle Sam’s Brand of Justice.
There is no country in the world where
the criminal is given more license than in
the U.S.A. and none where the criminal law
operates with greater laxity. The republic
holds the unenviable record of having on the
basis of population the world’s highest
criminal rate, and its administration of
criminal law has been declared by one of
its own most eminent jurists to be “a
disgrace to civilisation.” In the cases of
the two condemned Italians, Sacco and
Vanzetti, the fact that they were Com-
munists was used for Bolshevik purposes;
but had no such side-issue been raised the
same demonstration on a smaller scale and
within a local area would in all probability
have taken place. It is only a few years
since in Chicago, which is a notorious centre
of crime, two students, Nathan Leopold
and Richard Loeb, sons of wealthy business
men, murdered in cold blood a 14-year-old
schoolfellow, Robert Franks. The atti-
tude of the accused was callous. Leopold
offered to plead guilty “before a friendly
Judge” provided he got a sentence of im-
prisonment only, and remarked to a police-
sergeant: “Why couldn’t a man with the
millions of my father behind me before a
friendly Judge beat this case?” That ex-
pressed the attitude of the young American
under the influence of the materialistic
idealism of the States.
That justice can be bought and sold
is a conception very largely the result of
what was by its founders formulated as a
noble principle. In a democracy where all
men had an equality of right to elect their
own rulers, it followed that they had a
right equally to elect their own Judges.
The consequence is that, with the excep-
tion of the Supreme Judiciary, the Judges
hold office for a term of years only and
then have to submit themselves for re-
election by the people, and that re-election
depends on popularity. The goodwill of
men of influence, expressed in dollars,
means much to an elected Judge as the
close of his term of office approaches.
This explains Nathan Leopold’s reference
to “a friendly Judge.” On the occasion of
this trial, much as in the case of Sacco
and Vanzetti, though on a far smaller
scale, demonstrations in their favor were
held throughout the State. On. the day
set down for the delivery of judgment
nearly a hundred police were stationed
round the court building, and its founda-
tions were closely scanned and examined,
as threats to blow it and the Judge to
pieces had been freely circulated. The
Judge himself, on the way to court, was
guarded in spectacular fashion. “The
chief of police sat beside him; two ‘dead-
shot’ detectives in plain clothes accom-
panied him; another detective sat by the
driver, while two cars with detective guards
drove one in front and the other behind
the Judge’s car.” That, as in the Italians’
case, seems to be the popular attitude of
the American public towards the Judge
and the criminal. As Chief-Justice Taft
remarked some years ago, “The trial of a
criminal seems like a game of chance, with
all the chances in favor of the criminal.
And if he escapes he seems to have the
sympathy of a sporting public.”
Apart, then, from its Communistic signifi-
cance, the attitude of the American public
with regard to the execution of these two
malefactors appears to be its normal atti-
tude towards crime. One American autho-
rity attributes it largely to sentimentalism
wrought upon by the sensationalism of the
press. Others trace it to the contempt for
the judicial processes and the long
delay that frequently occurs between
the date of sentence and its fulfil-
ment-running sometimes into years.
The American system of delay upon
delay naturally arouses aversion against the
final execution of sentence of death after
months or years of hope and despair. In
the cases of Sacco and Vanzetti, seven
years elapsed. There was a quality of
mercy in the old practice of British law
which made the interval between sentence
and its execution as brief as possible—
it was often measured in hours. Molern
criminal law in British communities does
not favor such speedy despatch; it allows
time for appeal and a reasonable interval
for preparation. But it does not prolong the
prisoner’s agony, nor does it hold <>ut to
him hope of escape by legal processes
stimulated by the influences of money.
If in some matters the States may teach
us, we have good reason to rejoice in
our firmly-established administration of
justice by Judges who are neither friendly
nor unfriendly, and behind whose office no
shadow of the dollar can be cast.
» —-
Is It Cheaper to Keen £lO,OOO or Give
Away £6000?
The Freetrader, who is an ever-present
nuisance, states vociferously that it is more
profitable to give away the £6OOO. The
question came up for discussion recently at
Geelong. The Government, in a rare
moment of intuition, placed an order for
locomotives in Australia at £lO,OOO per
loco., when they could have been procured
from England for £6OOO apiece, with, of
course, the Federal duty added. An English
Chamber of Commerce circularised its Aus-
tralian brethren on the subject, saying
that “unemployment is rife” in the engi-
neering trades of Britain, and that the
transaction was in flagrant disregard of
Cobden and Adam Smith. The Geelong
Chamber of Commerce, being sympathetic,
held a corroboree over the matter, and the
Acting-Minister of Customs, being wholly
unsympathetic, said that unemployment
is rife in the engineering trades here also,
and that save for Protection Geelong would
be only a village or less than that, and
(in effect) that he had no time for Cobden
or Smith either.
Whether Freetrade and the passion for
cheap imports are of any use to any sort
of country is doubtful, but they are cer-
tainly rotten policies for a land with a load
of external debt and an excess of imports
which is always adding to the burden. If
Victoria had bought English locomotives
at £6OOO it would have assuredly borrowed
the money in England, and almost as cer-
tainly it would have gone on owing it
indefinitely. So with compound interest at
5 per cent, the price, at the end of 50 years,
would be £68,804 in gold for each machine,
and that is altogether too much to pay
for an engine that will be dead by then.
On the other hand, if locomotives are
obtained here at £lO,OOO, the money will
come back in taxes. When State and
Federal taxation stands at about £4O annu-
ally for a family of three (a bread-winner
and two dependants), the money which a
Government distributes for local products
comes back pretty fast, while the money it
gives for foreign goods doesn’t come back
at all.
And even if the native-born locomotive
is paid for with locally-borrowed money,
the interest thereon is payable in paper
and not in gold, as is the case with outside
liabilities, which introduces another
element. It is easier to meet the interest
on £lO,OOO or on £50,000 in notes which
are printed here when wanted than to meet
the interest on £6OOO abroad, seeing that
that liability has to be paid with surplus
exports which can be turned into gold, or,
as the surplus exports are never forthcom-
ing, with borrowed gold. There are more
problems in national finance than came
within the experience of Cobden or Adam
Smith or Henry George, for none of the
three lived in a country with a massive
and ever-growing foreign debt. And there
are more problems at its very door than
the Geelong Chamber of Commerce evi-
dently dreams of. If it doesn’t get a better
hold on Australia’s position, Geelong would
be as well without it.
«
Anti-Arbitration.
Before William Pember Reeves drafted
the Act that gave Maoriland a reputation
as a pioneer of industrial arbitration, he
spent months dredging among the rejected
bills of other Anglo-Saxon legislatures. The
fact that he was building on ruins did not
daunt him, and he finally settled on the
rejected proposals of Charles Kingston of
S.A. as the best groundwork for his struc-
ture. The essence of Kingston’s bill was
compulsion; and arbitration embodying that
principle has operated satisfactorily in Maori-
land for 33 years. Australia has had it for
very nearly as long; but here the industrial
courts, both State and Federal, apologise
whenever they have to stand up to their
decrees. The opposition nowadays comes
from the unions. In Maoriland, on the
other hand, most of the unions regard the
compulsory law as a sort of Magna Charta,
and it is the farmers who are clamoring
for the repeal of the Act. Farmhands
have never had an award, but the cocky
takes the view that everything he has to
buy in town is dearer because the Court
will persist in giving wages increases to the
city “parasites.”
Both the Maoriland cocky and the Aus-
tralian anti-arbitration union boss are Free-
traders by instinct. The idea that there
is such a thing as a fair wage, and that it
can be arrived at by a weighing of evidence,
revolts them. The cocky would like the
empty belly to operate as a prime factor.
The union boss wants an arena in which
he will have unfettered opportunities to
foster and exploit class-consciousness. Both
in Australia and in Maoriland the big,
inert, middle strata of society—it comprises
probably about 80 per cent, of both em-
ployers and wage-earners —prays, whenever
it thinks about such things, that the little
10 per cent, of militant extremists at each
end of the social gamut will wear them-
selves out with frothing and hysteria and
die peaceably. But the pious hope seems
vain. If the Arbitration Court is a strong
and fair one, there will always be em-
ployers who will seek to destroy it. If it
is weak and vacillating and a prey to special
pleadings, the workers will rise up against
it themselves, no matter how it may pander
to them.
The best thing that can be done in any
modern State is to settle the matter of
the Court’s authority by a referendum. If
the nation doesn’t want its labor disputes
settled by a judicial tribunal, let it say so,
and Rafferty’s Rules can be made a Statute.
But if the nation wants a fairer arbiter
than Rafferty, and says so, then the thing
to do is to put the Arbitration Court up
beside the Supreme Court, and back its
decisions with all the nation’s forces—from
police to warships.
It may be that an Arbitration Court,
whether a State or Federal affair, is a faulty
machine for rectifying labor troubles. Mani-
festly it can’t be familiar with all the ins
and outs of every occupation, and a cunning
and diplomatic advocate on either side may
plunge it into absurdities and injustices. A
Wages Board for each industry or broad
division of industry, composed of men from
the industry, would have a better technical
grasp and would be less likely to be bull-
dozed; but to split authority in industrial
affairs into several fragments isn’t expedient.
Perhaps an organisation of Wages Boards
to advise and an Arbitration Court to legis-
late and control might meet the require-
ments of the case. But the Court’s authority
ought to be absolute and unquestionable
and its mandate, ought to be written into
the Constitution in such a way that no
minority, whatever its political influence,
could hope to upset it.

The Commonwealth and Its Railways.
Dear Bulletin, —“Plain Bill” asks (B.
11/8/’27) “Should Millions of Sheep Die?”
and proposes that the Federal Government
build a railway from Bourke (N.S.W.)
northward through Western Queensland. He
wants 450 miles of line, but a more common
demand, which was repeated to Prime
Minister Bruce the other day, is for 950
miles right up to Camooweal. The plea
is that such a railway would save a repeti-
tion of the recent calamity (said to have
cost £10,000,000, but probably less) because
even in the worst drought there is always
relief country somewhere, if it can only be
reached. “Plain Bill” suggests that the
Queensland State Government should pay
“10 to 25 per cent, of the rents collected
on properties benefited by the railway into
a sinking fund” to clear off the inevitable
debt for construction.
As to this scheme there are these things
to be said:—
(1) Queensland isn’t the only State
which has drought losses. N.S.W. has seen
its flocks go down from nearly 62 millions
to 331 millions; Victoria, well railwayed as
it is, has had a drop from 153 millions to
11 millions; and S.A. lost half its jumbucks
in a series of inferior years.
(2) There isn’t always enough relief
country or nearly enough.
(3) A long railway from a small town
to no hamlet in particular, built chiefly to
carry starving stock for impoverished owners
at long intervals, looks about the poorest
investment imaginable. When the State
railways, with all the big coastal towns to
supply business, can lose £1,500,000 in a
boom year, there is no prospect for an out-
back line which would probably be held up
by a strike when most wanted.
(4) The interest would be heavy, and the
loss on working would probably be as much
as the interest. A grant by the State of
“10 to 25 per cent, of the rents collected on
properties benefited” would probably pay a
penny or two in the £ on the annual losses
and make no sinking fund at all. In Queens-
land the rentals of all the public lands in
the State often fail to cover railway losses.
(5) Even the penny or tw r o in the £ might
prove hard to collect.
(6) The line is really a State duty if it
is a duty. The Queensland Government has
built over 1000 miles of new railway in the
past 10 years, but it paid mighty little atten-
tion to its own West because of the scarcity
of votes there.
I have as much sympathy with a dying
sheep or even a dead sheep as the next
man. I can even go further and feel for
the unborn sheep which might be flourishing
in Central Australia if the Federal Govern-
ment had attended to its own business a
little better. But there is a point beyond
which posterity shouldn’t be pawned even
for the sake of the deadest sheep. All
the same, I believe that the Federal Bourke-
Camooweal railway job will be success-
fully put through one of these days, just
by reason of its unreason and its total fool-
ishness, combined with the effrontery of
its advocates. To some minds there is a
fascination in being a greater ass than the
average.—Yours, etc., J.E.

The Rights of “the Nigger.”
A recent announcement of Minister for
Home and Territories Makr illustrates the
danger of amateur interference in the
routine of highly technical administration.
The Minister, apropos of indentured labor
in Mandated New Guinea, says that he pro-
poses to “reconsider” the terms of that
Ordinance which makes compulsory the
return of native laborers to their villages at
the end of their three years’ period of em-
ployment. “The planters,” he says, “point
out that in many cases the natives do not
wish to return.” Though Marr had a good
look over Papua he has apparently learned
nothing from the experience gained in native
administration in that territory. Papua,
after half a century of successful experiment
in the always intricate problem of fairly
adjusting the requirements of white em-
ployer and native laborer, has come to the
definite conclusion that, however capable
the indentured native worker may become,
he must be sent back to his village life for
a period before being permitted to take on
another term of indentured service.
The Papuan in his home life is the most
communal person in existence. His village
carries him from birth to death; he has
rights of tenure in the village lands and
groves and hunting lands. So long as he
goes back to the community for a period
at least, says the Papuan Government, he
keeps in touch with his legal rights, and is
sure of maintenance in his old age. To see
that he does it is a wise and just precaution,
and one that applies equally in New. Guinea
as in Papua. Everyone familiar with that
strange little world knows the “boy” who
desires to cleave to his white master or
mistress, and forget the communal life
which is the essence of his people’s exist-
ence. But to encourage this spirit is to.
strike at the very roots of a primitive
civilisation which it is our duty to preserve.
Qnce more, the Papuan and New Guinea
native is essentially a man of the land. Any
movement to encourage him to forsake it
by extending terms of service in extraneous
jobs amounts to nothing more than giving
him a shove down the slithery slide which
has engulfed so many primitive victims of
well-meaning ignorance.
Give Us a Man!
Dear Bulletin, —I, too, a Labor supporter
for years, am up against a problem. Being
Labor, I automatically cannot vote Lang;
and I cannot vote Bavin. Given every
chance, with majorities in both Houses, the
Nationalists have consistently failed to
justify their existence. Their financial
methods are Micawber’s— they failed to
live within their income and knew no way
of balancing the ledgef without going cap
in hand to John Bull. They have side-
tracked railway lines connecting coast and
hinterland, so that one city may grow fat,
and lines connecting pastoral countries are
also verboten. They cannot make up their
minds what to do about the Leg. Council,
either, not being sure whether the election
of members is democratic or autocratic. In
short, they are not a party at all; they are
an agglomeration vainly struggling to satisfy
both the likes of me and the most reaction-
ary elements in N.S.W. political and finan-
cial life. Buttenshaw is only Bavin
hyphenated. So I am compelled to pick
the man irrespective of party till a suitable
body presents itself to the electors. That
body will mean the abolition of pre-
selection by legislative enactment, the in-
stitution of the Initiative and Recall, the
introduction of elective Ministries and the
death-knell of borrowing except for con-
version purposes—planks that will give
needed security to both employer and em-
ployee. Lang is hopeless, Bavin-Butten-
siiaw helpless; so any Independent of
decent calibre can count on my vote until
the Labor party becomes Australian in deed
as well as name.—Yours, in distress, Keg.

A Clumsy and Flat-footed Scheme. .
Seeing that the N.S.W. employer has to
pay three per cent, on his wages sheet into
the Child Endowment fund, it is plain that
the law offers him an extra inducement to
employ low-paid men instead of high-paid,
and half-pay women instead of men, and
flappers rather than full-grown women.
Already there are tales of processions of
males passing out of occupations in
which beef and brawn are not essentials, and
of processions of females going in. If a
time comes when there are too many
women who can’t afford to support husbands
and who are too busy to have children, it
will make the social edifice totter on its
basis, or possibly totter right off its basis.
A remedy might be found if some Beeby
or Piddington declared an equal wage for
male and female. The female would be
very short of a job then, no doubt; on the
other hand, the male could afford to marry
her oftener. If he neglected to do so, a
bachelor tax might wake him up.
A simple way would be to make the boss
pay so much per employee regardless of
wages. That would incite him to sack in-
efficient “hands”; but he would gain no-
thing by discharging highly-paid ones, who
would naturally be the best workers.
It is apparently impossible to work the
Lang trick of charging the Child Endow-
ment to the employer alone without giving
him reason for being less of an employer,
or a meaner employer, or a missing em-
ployer who has skipped over the border.
The whole thing is clumsy and flat-footed
to the last degree.

First Things First.
Stimulation of America’s export trade is
the idea behind the visit to Australia of
the steamer Roxen, which is a floating exhi-
bition of goods from Pacific Coast ports.
Some local minds threatened with bright-
ness consider that Australia should retort
in the same nautical manner. But the last
of the army of Freetrade ghosts will have
to be laid before the suggestion will be
worth a prize. America is the world’s
greatest manufacturer. Australia is only
now giving more or less serious attention to
its manufacturing _ industries. America in-
sured its manufacturers against competition
by raising a sky-high tariff wall. Australia
has fiddled with a tariff that, in most cases,
isn’t a picket fence by comparison.
The Federal Tariff Board has done a lot
of good, but it fails, as the Parliament
which created it failed, to realise that a
protective tariff that doesn’t protect is no
better than a ship that doesn’t float or a
gun that won’t shoot. Idle mills and fac-
tories on half-time, coupled with bulging
imports, proclaim in terms which a half-
wit can understand that more tariff is neces-
sary. Australia’s needs in aluminium
hollow-ware, for instance, will be fed by an
Australian company as soon as the tariff
tortoise moves in the right direction. The
plant is standing. Another Australian co.
is in a position to supply 80 per cent, of
the Commonwealth’s consumption of flint-
glassware, but a recent six months’ stoppage
of the works owing to a deluge of cheap
imports makes it yearn for some guarantee
against a recurrence of the flood. More }
illustrations are available —in textiles and
heavy industry notably.

POLITICAL
POINTS
By a happy inadvertence the makers of
the present N.S.W. Electoral Act restored an
old proviso, cut out by the Storey Ministry
in 1920, by which every voter lias to sign
a declaration that he is the right individual
and no personation fiend, and that he hasn’t
already voted. If he is bogus he thus makes
a false declaration, does a dangerous forgery,
and leaves behind a sample of his hand-
writing. Premier Lang expressed regret at
this “objectionable” necessity, and it is
certainly objectionable if one happens to be
found out. So is a dead body or a faked
cheque.
Representatives of IS unions, all prepared
to seize on that part of the Beeby award
which suited them and reject the rest, met
the other day in Melbourne and in Peck-
sniffian mood declared “uncomprising hos-
tility to any system of payment by results,
which tends to develop the worst character-
istics by appealing to the avaricious side of
character.” Possibly the real objection is to
the development of the industrious side of
character. The great national occupation of
backing horses from sordid motives is an
appeal to the avaricious side of character,
likewise to the profane and street-corner-
loafing side, but it has the murky advantage
that there is no productive industry- about
it. Yet if the racehorse doesn’t pay by
results, what does it pay by?
,T. ,T. Daly, who finished fourth two years
ago, will be the Labor nominee when the
S. Aus. Leg. Assembly and Leg. Council
meet to choose a successor to the Lite
Senator McHugh. It is not incumbent on

That will mean reducing S. Aus. Labor to
one Senatorsliip, whereas the electors
awarded it three.
James Dooley, Speaker of the N.S.W. Leg.
Assembly and ex-Premier, is the latest victim
of the new Red bosses of “the movement” ;
he was beaten in the selection ballot for
Hartley. Tom Mutch has decided to let the
electors judge his case in Botany, and so
has George Cann in Lakemba; but with the
Lithgow miners against him there is no
hope for Dooley. In other country ballots
the Reds were routed. Clark won hands
down in Castlereagh. Kelly in Bathurst,
Flannery in Murrumbidgee, Connell in Kahi-
bah and Hoad in Cootamundra: and they
were Louglilinites all. The “industrialists”
(as they call themselves) may be strong in
the mining districts and parts of Sydney,
but they are weak everywhere else.
Not only did candidates for A.L.P. nomi-
nations advertise themselves, in the language
of patent-medicine vendors, in the Labor
Daily (they generally described themselyos
as “fearless fighters”), but the executive
of the Government Railwaymen’s Union
“urged” its members, at space rates, to sup-
port three particular country aspirants. Pre-
sumably union funds were drawn upon for
this insolent departure from the old Austra-
lian ways. The recommendations or orders
were signed by Chapman, the English shop
steward, who was appointed secretary of the
organisation over the heads of numerous Aus-
tralian applicants, apparently because be
had been to Moscow. It is significant that
Chapman’s proteges were all licked; one,
indeed, got 20 votes out of GOB in Bathurst,
and another 18 out of SSI in Murrumbidgee.
Lang’s Labor Daily went into mourning
wnen two Italians convicted of murder were
supposed to have been executed in America,
and a flag was flown half-mast over a union
office. Other noisy friends of the noisy
Fremier suggested a boycott of American
goods, just as they suggested a boycott of
the American warships that visited Aus-
tralia two years ago: and with hearts bleed-
ing for Sacco and Vanzetti they ordered the
Commonwealth Government to exclude
Italian immigrants henceforth. A mob
demonstration against the Commonwealth
Arbitration Court was also arranged for.
The new leaders of the White Australian
worker wound up a hectic week by “affiliat-
ing’’ with a crowd of yellow Communists at
Hankow.
The Maoriland Budget, which deals with
the financial year that ended in March last
—the Fernlanders copy Mother- —isn’t a very
exciting document. Revenue was £677,000
over the estimates, departmental expenditure
increased by £107.000, despite or because
of a visitation of efficiency experts, and
there’s a visible surplus of a bit over half a
million. During the year the Shivery Islands
borrowed close on ten millions and paid back
£1,700,000. They make it a point of honor
to pay back something, and it’s a laudable
ambition not shared to any extent on this
side of the Tasman.
