A frilly idea of very dubious quality is
that a great exhibition shall be held at
Sydney in 1931. It seemingly appeals to
the Prime Minister, but then it naturally
would. More than anything else Australia
needs to put its nose to the grindstone and
get on with its everyday job. An exhibition
seems little more than an excitement and an
excuse for knocking oft' the everyday job.
Perhaps because he talks too much, the
chairman of the Film Commission suffers
from an imperfect memory. Melbourne Age
reports him as saying that “the whole of the
evidence so far is that an increased tariff
would serve no good purpose.” As a matter
of fact, Frank Hurley suggested a “heavy”
duty on U.S. positives when the Commission
examined him in Sydney. Professor Wallace,
the Chief Censor, is also in favor of increas-
ing the duty on Hollywood stuff—“double it”
was his recommendation. And the Professor
knows his subject from A to Z, and has no
axe to grind.
The Lang Government, which is supposed
to loathe overtime and such devices of the
employer, is pushing the rural work of the
Family Endowment business on to State-
school teachers. Now the S.-s teacher is
wondering who is going to suffer during the
long Christmas vacation —will he have to
grill at his post or will the business be hung
up for six Aveeks in midsummer?
Industrial Commissioner Piddo’s fixment,
without hearing evidence, of a basic wage
for the N.S.W. “rural worker” struck an
early snag. The problem is whether the
term means a person engaged in rural work
(the usual dictionary describes this as agri-
cultural) or one who does any kind of work
in rural surroundings, whatever they may
be. Or both. In other words, is the man who
tidies up town gardens rural, or is the
handy-man at a wayside pub or the ill-paid
bush parson?
Funny little echoes of the Communist
thunder over the death sentence passed on two
Italians in U.S.A. are already heard. The
other day a popular local pug. was fined at
Broken Hill for offensive language and re-
sisting the police. Whereupon the Barrier
Industrial Council screeched its demand, not
for a new trial, but for the removal of the
S.M. and the sacking of the constable who
fired the pug. in.
Labor aldermen at Sydney Town Haul be-
comp more unaccountable with age. In their
electricity department the wages bill was
at the rate of *43.751 per month at the
beginning of 1925. and rose to £91.044 per
month P'wly in 1927. and a half-year’s profit
of £34.200 changed into a half-year's loss of
£4O 270. Certainly it is possible, to buy
a lot of popularity by these lavish conces-
sions to municipal employees, but what is the
use of popularity if it merely leads to con-
tinuance of an unsalaried job which takes
up valuable time, leads nowhere and is re-
warded, especially when the finances are
rocky, with the breath of suspicion and the
sniff of opprobrium? It is difficult to imagine
a sadder spectacle than an aged alderman in
the Benevolent Asylum, having sacrificed a
life’s opportunities on the civic altar.
Miscamble’s term as manager of Tas-
mania’s railways has nearly run out, and
Lyons has weakly decided on a board of
three. As a teacher Lyons would know a lot
better than to appoint a trinity of head-
masters to run a school. But these poli-
ticians are pretty well all alike in one
respect; they do like lumps of putty to play
with.
While permitting Melbourne War Memorial
Committee to erect the Shrine of Remem-
brance on the Grange site in the Domain,
Vic. Labor Government will not endorse the
promise made by the Lawson Ministry to
contribute £50,000. To raise £250,000 by
private subscription is now regarded as hope-
less, and many people urge that a cenotaph
on the same lines as that in Martin-place,
Sydney, would serve. Canberra, they sug-
gest, is the right place for any memorial
claiming to be national.
Victoria’s new Hogan Ministry has to
deal with a State which went maddest on
“settlement” because it had the most insig-
nificant percentage of unsettled soil avail-
able. It made sure of a loss of £9,000,000
by advancing its new farmers money at less
intefest than it was paying itself, and it
proceeded to make other losses galore. Last
year it took £178.055 worth of clerk, inspec-
tor, Barnacle, stationery, travelling, hotel and
other expenses to keep 9000 soldier-agricul-
turists on the land, if they are still there.
And with all this breathless agitation lec-
tori an acres under cultivation were fewer
for the year to June, 1926, than in 1916,
or 1926, or 1924, or 1925.
The magician showed the empty box.
A tap of his wand and out came ribbons,
handkerchiefs, flags and a pair of pigeons.
“Wot a bloke to handle a selection ballot,’’
murmured a good Red in the gallery.
Unless Lang’s followers secure a few,
hundred per cent, more votes at the N.S.W.
election than at the selection his crowd will
be hardly large enough to make a Christmas
party for an infant. So many people were
ruled out, or disqualified, or had their ballots
mislaid or something that a few dozen votes
sufficed in some cases to land a reliable
Red at the head of the poll.
The Premier lately promised that N.S.W.
will put up the money for a flight of three
Australians across the Pacific, and eager
speculations were indulged in as to whether
the name of one among the three might be
John Thomas Lang, he being a man of high
ambition who is always ready to make a
splash. But hope was ever a bolting phan-
tom and a skedaddling myth.
W. L. Ross, discoursing on early times
to the Manly, Warringah and Pittwater
(N.S.W.) Historical Society, unintentionally
drew a striking picture of our present-day
defences in the North. For many years
the Customs House was at Barrenjoey, and
to scare off smugglers an officer had a
wooden soldier erected in front of the caves
thereabouts, resplendently rigged in white
trousers, scarlet coat, fin helmet and plume,
and with drawn sword and swinging scab-
bard. Dummy remained on duty till a few
years ago, when white ants ate his legs from
under him, and he collapsed. But he still
lives on as a symbol. Our vast_ northern
coastline offers nothing more formidable
against smugglers than Dummy, plus tower-
ing cathedrals of wffiite ants.
Lang’s Child Undowmont wasn’t going to
cost the “worker” a penny. But when the
worker’s missus drops into a shop for a
cup of tea and finds that it's fourpence
instead of threepence she probably begins
to wonder who is the liar of the family.
It was a big importing year in Maori-
land, and import and excise duties provided
a good third of revenue, but only a small
proportion of the tariff charges can be
reckoned as Protective. The Maorilander
pays more per head through the Customs
than anybody else in the world, _ but the
manufacturer is still regarded by him as an
eccentric individual suffering from delusions.
For the current year it is reckoned that
Customs revenue will decrease by half a
million but that departmental exes, will con-
tinue to soar. Debt charges, of course, will
swell, and unless the luck of the Reformers
works overtime there will be a deficiency next
year.
Two Melbourne detectives performed a
creditable deed the other day; they found
a man in Collins-street with a land subdivi-
sion plan in his possession and straightway
locked him up on a charge of vagrancy. It
is true that he was an undesirable who had
been let off a year or so previously on his
promise to keep out of the State, but still
the promptitude with which the Force made
the land subdivision plan a casus belli was
refreshing.

/ says: “Your Honor, think of what?”
“Think of the nation,” says ’is Nibs.
E says: “Of course, you all belong
To unions ’ere and unions there,
And each of ’em is flamin’ strong,
And strongest of ’em yours, you’ll swear.
1 tell you now, and tell you flat,
There’s one much bigger union, see!”
/ says: “Go on; and which is that?”
“The nation, nothin’ less,” says ’e.
"This nation push must seem to you,”
I tells ’im then, “to ’ear you preach,
The largest oyster in the stew.
The biggest pebble on the beach.
1 know me Trades ’All better, though.
It isn't listed, low nor ’igh;
It ain’t affiliated, so—
It cuts no ice with me,” says I.

“Aitchsee”: Not all the biscuits issued
in the Great War were too fragile to go
through the post (B. 4/8/’27) : I delivered
several, also lots of other unusual “letters.”
The most popular from Anzac were cigarette
boxes. I once saw a mere visiting-card come
through with' a long message microscopically
penned thereon. Some of the soldiers made
a welter of the free postage, one sending
to his ladylove 97 postcards in one mail.
On another occasion I delivered a bundle
of postcards to a love-lorn maiden with the
remark “Only 28 this mail.” She turned
them over and disappointedly remarked:
“And only seven of them have written.”
“P.W.A.”: I have been
assisting an old Irish-
woman of my acquain-
tance to lodge an appli-
cation for the old - age
pension. She has always
answered to the Christian
name of Mary, but upon
investigation of the pas-
senger list upon which
she voyaged from her
native land to Australia
nearly 40 years ago it
was found to be Mary
Anna. I pointed this out,
and she explained that
upon arrival in this coun-
try she had dropped the
second name, Mary ap-
pealing to her as being
“more commoner” than
Mary Anna. I said I
t hong h t most people
would prefer the double.
“An’ me worrkin’ for me
livin’!” she exclaimed.
‘Thwat at all would I be
doin’ wid a title like
that?”

after Wilhelm had finally skied the towel,
there was a compound of German prisoners
under Freneh control, the occupants being
employed by daytime in filling in trenches
and shell-holes and exploding duds. One day
they took it into their square skulls to re-
fuse duty. A percentage of the striking

ot authority was a short, spiteful whip. The

“Bullwhanger” : May I round off the dis-
cussion on tattooing with a reference to
the celebrated “Tattooed Lady” of Folkes-
tone, who was a feature of that English
seaport in 1919? Tipping the beam at about
20 stone, she was a walking picture-
gallery, all of her anatomy that was
visible, as well as presumably what
wasn’t, being covered with weird, won-
derful designs, the work of her “profes-
sor.” His “studio” was on the first floor
of a back-street building, the ground
floor being fitted up as a fish-
shop and run by the lady her-
self. For the absurdly-small
sum of a bob you could enjoy
a fine Dover sole while getting
an eyeful of the tattooing art.
The professor’s price-lists occu-
pied a place on the table next
the menu-cards, and the walls
were covered with framed de-
signs which patrons could have
transferred to their hides if they
so desired after discussing the
more material fare. Hundreds
of men from the near-by camps
and naval vessels in the port
found their way into the studio
upstairs. And I’ll bet they’re
now cursing their folly, like
everybody else who takes on the
damfool stunt.
“Pfella”: How’s this for a really sympa-
thetic way of breaking bad news? A W.A.
railwayman lived some little distance from
the country station where he was employed,
and walked to his job every morning. One
day, while his wife was engaged in her house-
work, an old gin, well known in the district,
drifted into the back yard, picked up the
axe and commenced slogging into the wood-
heap. When she had cut about a week’s
supply she accosted the puzzled wife.
“Missus,” she exclaimed, “boss no bin able
cuttem wood long time; he bin
leg crushed longa trucks.” A few minutes
later the news was confirmed officially.
“A.8.W.”: He was sinking one with the
flies in a Bourke-street (Melbourne) pub
when a glad-eyed female entered and asked
him to shout. He obliged, and she returned
his kindness by going out and coming back
with another of her kind. After several
more refreshers the three roysterers de-
parted ; on their way up Russell-street the
cavalier dropped in his tracks. There was
a constable dangerously handy, so the ladies
dragged the gent to his feet and round the
corner, regained Bourke-street by a back lane
and re-entered the pub parlor with their port-
able exchequer. After more drinks, one of
the ladies “brushed him down” and departed.
The other remained with her companion till he
slumbered, then she completed the work of
depredation. Hours later, waking suddenly
(and soberly), the injudicious x-eveller took
stock; in addition to all his loose change, his
gold watch, chain, sovereign-case and a £lO-
- had vamoosed. Too common an occur-
rence to be worth a par., you think, and
youth must have its fling, anyway. But this
youth w r as 94!
“Bulantun” : Many of the Chinese who met
their fate when the ship St. Paul went ashore
near Rossel Island in 1858 (B. 14/7/’27)
must have had English sovereigns. Some
years after the tragedy, when the islanders
had become more civilised, a trader dropped in
on them. Hearing talk of “red-fella pennies,”
which some of the natives claimed to have, he
induced an old man to show him some, and
secured gold coins in exchange for some trade
goods. Further supplies were unearthed by
other natives, and the lucky trader is alleged
to have obtained in the vicinity of £lOOO in
gold. He was always reticent concerning his
transactions, but many old residents of Papua
bought sovereigns from him as souvenirs;
these were filed on one side and inscribed
“Rossel Island. 1858.” An excellent speci-
men may be seen on Duncan Campbell's
watch-chain at Samarai.

“Drag Link” : Speaking of free tram-rides
(B. 28/7/’27). I was taking a light engine
from Townsville to Ayr (N.Q.) early one
morning, with the mercury slipping down
below 40. At Giru we stopped to replenish
the tender, and a voice out of the darkness
asked me what time the next train was going
Ayrwards. I could dimly discern a figure a
few r yards away. “None till daylight,” I
replied. Outside Ayr the signal was against
me and I stopped. Poking around the engine,
I discovered my Giru questioner sitting on the
cowcatcher. I gazed at him dumbfounded,
and he remarked quietly, “Gee, Dig, she’s
cold out here!” I reckon he deserved that
ride.
WHEN I WAS EIGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Oh. Granny Tate! when I was eight
1 thought your house was line,
For didn’t ivy climb the walls,
And round the windows twine;
And such a dandy almond-tree
Grew at the garden gate!
How good those almonds used to taste
When I was only eight!
And when the lamps were lit indoors.
And flames leapt up apace
Around the kettle, swinging in
The fine old fireplace,
I’d sit upon the big wide hob,
And think it simply great
To watch the gum-leaves crackle high,
When I was only eight.
Oh, Granny Tate! when I was eight.
I loved with you to sit.
For you would tell me lots of things
As you would knit and knit.
About the many beaux you had—-
All left disconsolate —
And I would gravely look at you
When I was only eight.
AD. 1927.
“I mp-pose you are afraid that your mother might come?”
“Yes, I am. But I want you to promise that you won’t let her vamp you off me,
Freddy.”
You told me I must always strive
To be both brave and good,
And always heed my parents dear.
As all good children should ;
But Grandpa Tate would wink at me,
And rub his snovry pate—
He seemed a droll old gentleman
When I was only eight.
Oh. Gramiy Tate! when I was eight,
You said your curls were gold
The time a dashing Grandpapa
Came wooing, brave and bold ;
And then you’d sit and dream awhile,
And I’d watch Grandpa Tate
As. chuckling soft, he’d scrape his pipe,
When 1 was only eight.
Then he and I would quiet sit,
And watch you as you’d doze,
And laugh to see the spectacles
Go sliding down your nose:
And I would try to picture you
A bride, and Grandpa Tate
A voung and dashing cavalier.
When I was only eight.
Oh, Granny Tate! when I was eight,
I was so very sure
That you had been a prim young thing,
And always so demure,
Till Grandpa, chuckling, said that you’d
Eloped to be his mate —
He was a droll old gentleman
When I was only eight.

LUCY HILI.


“Golden Gate”: As to town
criers (B. 11/8/’27), the most
interesting the writer ever met
was an institution at Croydon
(N.Q.) in the long ago. A well-
educated, refine d, slightly-
touched remittance man, he
drew £4OO a year in half-yearly
instalments; and when his ship
came in it was his custom to
expend most of the money on a
big free-for-all picnic to the
population of the town. No
invitations were necessary; on
the occasion when the writer
attended he was driven out to
the function, as a matter of
course, by the landlord of his
hotel. The landscape was
strewn with hams, fowls, bottles,
barrels and other aids to
alfresco enjoyment, upon which
the bellman’s guests were busily
engaged. Through the scattered
banquet wandered the host, like
Banquo’s ghost, utterly un-
heeded by the recipients of his
bounty, to whom he occasionally
extended a tremulous hope
that they were enjoying them-
selves. The repeated question
so irritated a large, red-faced
lady near the writer that, with-
out reply, she seized the shat-
tered corpse of a turkey in front
of her, and “landed” her host
with it behind the ear. Shak-
ing fragments of stuffing out of
his long, grey locks, the old
gentleman smiled deprecatingly,
and passed on. “It’s always
like that,” said the publican
sententiously, “but still old
B 1 an k seems to enjoy it
thoroughly.”

“Pourri Pourri”: The Irish
agriculturist is reputed to be
the hardest man in the world to
separate from his money. Yet
a cable told, the other day, of
one who parted up £5OO to a
“wise woman” who undertook to
cure his ailments by her magi-
cal powers. She explained that
she could do this because she
was the last-born of triplets, a
piece of reasoning which seems
to have dispelled all doubt. Most
people raise deprecating eye-
brows at the thought of such
credulity and superstition. Yet
the horde of fortune-tellers
which flourishes in every Aus-
tralian city is clear proof that
many of us are every bit as
gullible. I myself knew a well-
educated woman who was told
by a fortune-teller in Sydney
that her husband, who had been
twice wounded in South Africa, would receive
his third and final issue on Gallipoli, where
he then was, and that she herself would marry
again. So convinced was the lady, though a
dutiful wife, that she sold her husband’s
entire civilian wardrobe, the fairly extensive
accumulation of years. When hubby re-
turned to confound the soothsayer, there
wasn’t even a collar-stud left, and the new
outfit made quite a hole in his deferred pay.

“Wanchai”: A few years ago I was teller
at a bank in Gippsland. Resuming duty on
a Saturday after holidays, my reliever,
Blank, advised me that a cash surplus had
accumulated, and we decided that he should
take it to the Melbourne office with him on
the Monday morning. I overslept myself and
when the car called at our hotel to take us
to the bank for the cash I had hardly time
to put slippers on my feet and an overcoat
above my pyjamas. We lifted the cash from
the bank strongroom, and I had installed my
companion in the train with it when he dis-
covered that he had left a suitcase behind
and insisted on returning to the pub for it
while I remained on guard. He cut it so fine
that the platform-porter had rung the bell
and slammed the gate before he returned,
leaving me with the horrifying prospect of a
trip to Melbourne in my pyjamas, without
ticket and money, yet with a fortune locked
away in a black leather bag under the seat.
To make things worse, the head office had
been instructed to provide a police escort
when the man with the cash arrived in Mel-
bourne. Blank, however, just managed to
burst through the gate as the train was
starting _ and I was so relieved when he
hopped into the compartment and resumed
charge of the black bag that the bruises I
got as I jumped out, to sprawl full length
on the platform, and the spirited lecture from
the stationmaster seemed trivial things.

the .Malay, who is no mean angler, has been
brought to Singapore by Japanese. A motor-
boat tows a dozen smaller craft to the deep
water off a reef. A large net is stretched
along the reef, and the fishermen,, stripped
to a pair of goggles, form a crescent of
swimmers, each swimmer trailing a line
<a- little streamers attached to a sinker.
Every now and then the crescent dives to

“Oxmead”: Australia’s forthright High
Commissioner, the breeziness of whose
vocabulary has always been well advertised,
got to work early in London, by warning
some Dreadnought lads against the perils
of “booze” in Australia. Cook certainly
wouldn’t have employed such a term, nor
Andy Fisher either: Reid might have in his
lighter moments; coming from Ryrie it
seems entirely natural. Yet many people
will consider it inelegant; two or thr'vj
years.ago an Australian archbishop publicly
deplored the prevalence and the vulgarity of
the exnressive word. Which rather suggests
that the prelate was a poor philologist, for
so far from being modern slang “booze” is
good Anglo-Saxon and almost certainly the
oldest term in the language for strong drink
and lots of it. Among authors who didn’t
scruple to use it to convey what they meant
W. S. Landor, 'Sir Walter Scott and H. R.
Haweis are mentioned by Webster. So that
Ryrie, even if no more a philologist than tlie
archbishop, is really in excellent company.
“Nakhoda”: Immense surprise is being
shown, mainly by people who have forgotten
their own youth, at the result of a film test
carried out at a suburban theatre in Mel-
bourne to discover wliat kids get out of
pictures. The sub-titles were cut out of
a typical reel of sex appeal and 3000 child-
ren asked to describe the tale. All but
2 p.c. nicked out the most sultry por-
tions, and several located improprieties
that the adult mind had missed. In a scene
showing a husband saying good-bye to his
wife, the bushes in the background stir;
one boy wrote: “I knew there was a man
behind those bushes and that he wanted to
seduce the woman.” Of course, all this will
be trotted out as evidence that the modern
child is going to Hell faster than his dad
did when a boy. Perhaps his dad was fortu-
nate in not having the pictures to awaken
his curiosity about life, but it was awakened
all the same. As far as I can trace it back,
mine was aroused by an antiquated Family
Bible, with the Old Testament illustrated in
the uncompromising fashion of 200 years
ago.
“Landa”: Mrs. Soapsuds wanted more
office-cleaning to fill in time and asked me
for a reference. Being busy I called the
presiding genius of the typewriter and hastily
outlined what was required. The document
returned to me for signature ran: “This
is to certify that Mrs. Soapsuds has been
cleaner since March 1. 1026. at which date
she took up duties with this office.”

“Roka”: Maoriland is to have something
unusual in the botanic-garden line. This
is the A'pine Garden it is proposed to estab-
lish in Tongariro National Park, where the
Dominion’s flora will be collected from the
very high altitudes to which ordinary folk
cannot aspire to climb. Maoriland possesses
something really unique in mountain flowers,
as the enormous Egmonc buttercup, the
largest in the world, with its huge leaves
and three-inch blossoms; also the beautiful
Mount Cook daisy from the slopes of the
snowy giant (the Maori Aorangi) of the
Southern Alps, and an assortment of smaller
flowers and mosses that delight the botanists
who come to study the alpine plant life.

“Panchaz” : While one of the “Tre” boats
(Hain Line) was in Sydney last year, two
of the nigger firemen deserted, and the local
agents had to pay the usual £IOO bond in
each case. The skipper had difficulty in
replacing them, but finally got away. After
the voyage to London he cleared for the
Persian Gulf, touching at Marseilles on the
run out. Following a visit to his agents he
w T as strolling back to the ship when he un-
expectedly ran into the two men who had
cost his employers that little matter of £2OO.
Recognition was mutual, but the skipper only
had time to clout one defaulter and confer
a vicious kick on the other before the pair
made their escape. He still deplores that
he got so little for the vanished'money.
“lan C.” : Death has claimed
Terence Callaghan, of Colling-
wood (Vic.), who obtained wide
publicity two years ago when he
caused proceedings to be taken
agin four ’tecs for conspiring
to relieve him of £6OOO-odd.
Three were sent to quod for
varying terms, and one, after
disappearing for months, sur-
rendered at last and was
acquitted. Callaghan was 63
when he died of heart disease.
He boasted that he was worth
£I,OOO 000 at the time the
sleuths were putting the screw
on him with a charge that he
had “received” from persons of
doubtful honesty. He combined
the occupations of milk retailer,
pub owner and speculator, and
possessed an abundance of
native shrewdness to compen-
sate for his lack of education.
He didn’t bother much about
hating. Once, after being fined
for selling indifferent milk,
which he averred he hadn’t
watered, lie invited the Health
Department witnesses who had
helped to convict him to come
and have a taste. They didn’t
accept, but they refused with a
smile and, not with a snarl;
Callaghan had a “way with
him.”

“C. Coral” : Restoration Rock,
f >w~„ s ide the island where
Bligh of the Bounty broke his
long journey in an open boat,
now has an unattended light
erected on it, linking up with
the long chain already flashing
along the Great Barrier. One
of the earliest warnings to
mariners along the route was a
beacon erected at Raine Island,
where a number of fine ships
had crashed in 1844. The Bri-
tish Admiralty had instructed
Captain Blackwood of the Fly
to put up a tower 70 feet high,
and he applied to the N. S.
Wales Government for assist-
ance ; but all he could get was
the loan of 20 convicts and a
small revenue cutter. The Fly,
Bramble and the said cutter,
Prince George, did the job.
Lime was burnt from clam and
other shells, coral-rock blocks
were quarried and a circular
structure erected with Avails five
feet thick and a canvas-covered
dome at the top. Timber and
other materials from the Avreck
of the Martha RidgAvay Avere
used in the construction of the
beacon. The beacon is still
there, its inner Avails scored
with the names of countless sea-
farers. It was Avliile the Fly
Avas on this Avork that she was
able to succor the passengers of
the brig Archibald Campbell,
which had got piled up near
there.

“X.D.”: An extraordinary
case cropped up in a Melbourne
court the other day. A man
with a foreign name pleaded
guilty to a charge of robbery
and put in a statement that for
seven years he had been accepted
as a member of a respectable
local family, returned from the
war. Actually he Avas an im-
postor, the soldier he imper-
sonated having been killed in a
gambling saloon in New York.
He made the confession to saA r e
from shame the people who had
accepted him. Then the sister,
whom he alleged wasn’t his
sister, Avas put in the witness-
box. Asked if the story was
a true bill or humbug, she said
she didn’t knoAV. The curious
part about it was that tlie man who claims
to be an impostor has had previous convic-
tions, but this is the first time he was ever
audibly conscience-stricken.
BALLADE OF CLERICS AND KISSES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
“ ‘Don’t expect the clergyman to kiss the bride, even
in the vestry,’ said the Vicar of Leyland, England,
in some advice to bridegrooms. ‘lt isn’t done
except by “soppy” bachelor parsons.’ ” — Cable.
Do not expect, O palpitating groom,
The kiss ecclesiastic for your bride.
Though paradise upon your vision loom
At sight of lips these clergymen deride.
In celibate sky-pilots may abide
The habit, and to them seem not amiss;
But why—assuming they are not belied—
Why do the wedded clergy dodge the kiss?
Is it the rose, Romance, has lost her bloom
Since close upon her mystery they have
spied ?
Is it in clerics’ wives there may be room
For some improvement on the amorous
side ?
It seems the fact can hardly bo denied,
Though loath am I to harbor thoughts like
this:
The fox defamed the grapes he had not
tried—
Why do the wedded clergy dodge the kiss?
Nor sly approach nor rope gives
hint of doom
To beast that never has been
thrown nor tied;
Your foot-free curate finds no
future gloom
In present pouting lips; no
cunning guide
Is there to lead him from the
snare set wide,
No hand to hold him back from
the abyss.
Unless he halt, as I do, to
decide
Just why the wedded clergy
dodge the kiss.
Envoy.
Intruding doubts I have before
defied
A-many times to dream of
nuptial bliss;
But now—a hole I seek where-
in to hide!
Why do the wedded clergy
dodge the kiss?
VICTOBIA.
HASEN.
WILDFLOWER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As sweet and fair was she
As the hawtncrn-buds above her,
Or dew in a dewy rose—
How could I choose but love her?
Should not one joy in the sun.
Or in draughts of cooling water?
Does not one loose a dove
After one’s hands have caught her?
What use the golden cup
If the wine spill untasted?
The honey in the flower,
If sipped not, is but wasted!
And yet—l cannot tell—
Reason so wayward seeming!
should I have passed her by
And left her to her dreaming?
VICTOJRIA.
HELEN E. WALLACE.

“Kayeff” : “R.F.lv.F.’s version (8.4/S/’27)
of the discovery, of phosphate
deposits at Nauru and Ocean
islands isn’t the only one.
Here’s another. A. F. Ellis,
in Sydney on leave, spotted a
piece of rock phosphate being
used as a door-stop in a Dar-
linghurst “residential.” He
satisfied himself of its value
by testing a chip, and pro-
ceeded to investigate the door-
stop’s antecedents. The pre-
vious owner had been a bar-
ber, who had received it from
a sailor. The latter proved
hard to locate. Ellis presently
ran into his man on the Syd-
ney waterfront. The shellback
was no navigator and had
only a vague idea of the
whereabouts of the island
where he had picked up the
rock whilst on a water-carry-
ing party. But.his approxi-
mate directions were enough
to set Ellis quartering the
Pacific between the Caroline,
Marshall and Gilbert groups.
Ocean Island and Nauru were
finally located, and parties
sent ashore, ostensibly for
water and provisions. Test-
holes, secretly and hurriedly
dug, settled all doubts. Eng-
lish capital was secured, and
the rest is history. I wenl
through the relevant portion
of Ellis’s diary in the Com-
mission’s Ocean Island Office
not many years ago. The door-
stop came from Ocean Island,
not Nauru; hard and beauti-
fully marked, there is nothing
quite like it on other plios-
phate fields. Ellis’s discovery helped to make
him a very wealthy man.

“Ex-Dig.”: Mentioned at the R.S.L. con-
gress the other day that some ex 7 army
doctors had had tlmir professional feelings
outraged by being hailed as “Comrade” by
a fish-hawker. There’s a yarn the other way

an affluent person in civil life who was never
more than a private in the A.1.F., gave his
orders for some appetising-looking prawns
before he looked at the vendor; then he gave
a yelp of recognition. “Why, Dig.!” he said:
“aren’t you Captain Sluggs, of the Fmp-
teenth Battalion?” There was something
grudging in the way the other admitted the
correctness of the, identification, but
Jinks thumped him cheerily on the back,

“The Rumpus”: I cured a man of sea-
sickness before I was out of my ’teens. At
that time I was stewarding along the Ban-
analand coast and was frequently appealed
to by green-faced passengers for something
to relieve their suffering. I had suggested
my usual specific, a stiff brandy, to an
elderly gent who had been prostrated for
days, only to hear him declare, between
spasms, that he had never tasted alcohol in
his life and wouldn’t start at his age. So I
tried an old sea joke on him —a helping of
mashed potato with as much pepper in it as
it would hold. To my surprise he swallowed
the appalling mixture and his mal de mcr
vanished with it. I was slapped on the back
and my grateful patient was presently im-
pressing on the captain that “the young
steward knew more than a doctor.” Arriving
at Brisbane he handed me a fiver as a tip.

“Med.” : The “plant alonga bush” used by
Papuan natives for contraceptive purposes
(B. 11/8/’27) is undoubtedly the pink-
flowered Cinchona succirubra. From this the
alkaloids quinine, quinidiue, cinchonine and
cinchonidine are obtained. The first is used
in many communities for the same purpose
as the Papuan one, but serious poisoning and
sometimes death is the penalty for taking too
much of it.

“Nakhoda” : Whether or not Papuan women
are expert contraceptionists (B. 11/S/\27)
the ladies of the more or less neighboring
island of Borneo have little to learn on the
subject, according to my planter friend Blank.
Seven years ago he engaged a native house-
keeper, as is the fairly frequent custom of the
country. “Mind, there are to be no children,”
he warned her. “.Tangan takue, Tuan.” she
replied with superb confidence. “There
won't be.” There weren’t —-and many another
planter has had a similar experience.

“Eureka”: Tattooing may have its useful
side; some people think it has. An old seaman
told me that Indian ink thus applied w T as a
sure cure for rheumatism, and as proof of
his belief in it showed me a design round
every joint in his body. In his old age he
was employed as a useful at a Sydney hos-
pital where there was an inmate under
treatment for rheumatoid arthritis and
unable to walk. Medical remedies having
proved of no avail the patient left the
hospital and got the old sailor to tattoo him.
The prescription was a snake winding round
each leg. hips to his toes, and in a month
the subject was able to walk without dis
comfort. Many old sailors 1 have known
have a big percentage of their ornamenta-
tion round the joints; some I have seei
v/ith a ring tattooed round the finger on
which is usually worn the familiar rheu-
matic ring, which suggests that the belief
is a widely-spread one.
* * * S
“Sebrof” : I don’t think the tattooing art
is dropping into oblivion (B. 4/8/’27) ; it
is more likely that artists and their clientele
have moved up in the social scale since the
former used to inhabit dens along the water-
front and the latter were sailors before the
mast. During the war the most expert
tattooist in London had a suite of rooms
in Jermyn-street as imposing as any
specialist’s; it was necessary to book
appointments. One day I was in the wait-
ing-room when a tine-looking, bearded old
man, accompanied by a lady, came out of
the “studio.” The needle-wielder informed
me later that the man was the [late]
Duke of Orleans. I was left to infer that
professional etiquette forbade him telling me
who the lady was, or on which of the pair
he had been operating.

“Coot” : Melbourne Public Library’s latest
acquisition is a set of the exceedingly rare
Mercubitjs Politictts, a weekly paper pub-
lished during the whole period of the Eng-
lish Commonwealth, to which the poet Mil-
ton was a regular contributor and for two
years editor. The set acquired lacks one
number only; a copy, however, of the miss-
ing issue has been procured. Masson, in
his “Life of Milton,” describes the paper as
“indubitably the most effective organ of the
Commonwealth.” The Library Trustees paid
£4OO for the set.
* * * *
“Windsor” : Which is the world’s gaudiest
flag? China boasts most colors, viz., red,
yellow, blue, white and black; but Abys-
sinia’s vivid green, red and yellow arrange-
ment is rather more garish. Persia owns a
florid concoction —white, green-bordered on
top and red-bordered on bottom, with a sun
and a lion slapped together in the middle.
Nicaragua avoids simplicity with five vol-
canoes, a cap of liberty and a rainbow super-
imposed on a blue, white and blue ground.
Salvador, on the other hand, strikes a re-
freshingly subdued note with light-blue, white
and light-blue ; but the simplest banner of the
whole lot belongs surely to the tiny 38-
square-mile principality of San Marino on
the Adriatic—just plain white and blue.
Crosses are popular in the cold North. Fin-
land, Norway, Sweden, Denmark and Ice-
land all favoring different colored varieties.
Incidentally, it’s high time some form of
copyright in the national flag business was
recognised. Already Jugo-Slavia has pinched
France’s colors, while Ecuador has for years
fluttered a flag which is a replica of neigh-
boring Colombia’s —horizontal yellow, bine
and red, with the yellow stripe twice the
width of the others.
COMRADES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The Returned Soldiers’ Congress rejected a
motion that in all minutes and correspondence,
members should be designated ‘comrade,’ whatever
their rank. One delegate declared that the practice
had brought disaster on his own organisation, two
colonels having resigned under the stress of being
so addressed and several doctors being seriously
annoyed because a man who sold fish in the street
had spoken to them in this way.’’
Though Peace may have her victories more
renowned
Than War, she also keeps in stock, ’tis
clear,
Humiliations equally profound
And catastrophic blows no less severe.
The most fraternal greeting’s apt to sound
Unpleasant to the regimental ear
For the mere reason that it is fraternal—
When the recipient has been a colonel.
Comrades-in-arms they’d with their soldiers
shared
All that campaigning offers. None the less
They found themselves entirely unprepared
For this familiar method of address.
Now and again perhaps they mightn’t have
cared
But it occasioned them acute distress,
Being be-comraded, times uncomputed,
By chaps who once stood silent and saluted.
They would have faced undaunted shell and
shot;
Their nerves had not been frayed where
bullets sing;
But this incessant “comrade” made them
hot —
The word’s so levelling and leavening:
Also the Bolshie flavor that it's got
Makes it emphatically not the thing.
In ink it jarred and orally it jolted.
What wonder in the end the colonels bolted?
Picture the brisk, important medico.
Mow can he keep the dignity he’d wish,
The pose demanded, by punctilio
When a hoarse person who is vending fish
Flings at the passing car a glad “What ho.
Comrade !” One almost hears the “pshaw !’’
and “pish !”
And sees the angry frown at the concoctors
Of such outrageous ways of hailing doctors.
Brothers-in-arms, no doubt, for many a day,
From Private Snowy up to Marshal Haig,
But only in a large, loose-iointed way—
An acceptation infinitely vague;
Comrades in the objective none gainsay,
But: in the vocative the term’s a plague
To some who’ve changed into civilian clobber.
They couldn’t look more wry at “Dig” or
“Cobber.”
N.S.W.
T. THE R.

The Commercial Banking Co. of Syd-
ney :
Dividends
June, Profits. (10 p.c.) Written Off. Reserves.
Bank of Victoria absorbed.
Reserves taken over, £850,000 . . £3,905,297
1927. . £556,705 £401,818 £50,000 4,010,184
The C.B.S. has entered a new era in its
development. For close on a century its
activities had been confined to N. S. Wales
and Queensland —incidentally it is now the
biggest deposit-holder amongst the associ-
ated banks in its home State. With the
absorption of its old friend the Bank of
Victoria, provisionally agreed to as from
Dec. 31, but not completed till March 22,
the C.B.S. at once gained a substantial Vic-
torian business and a small but growing
connection in South Australia. The price
paid for the business was £2,217,022
£1,478,010 in cash for return of capital, the
balance in shares for surplus assets. Net
profit for the 12 months to June 30 was
equal to 13.8 p.c. on average capital, and
after handing out the usual 10 p.c. div. and
striking £50,000 off premises, already in at
a notoriously conservative figure, not quite 19
p.c. of the year’s net earnings were visibly
sent to reserves, making them equal to
£lO 12s. lOd. per share £l2 10s. paid. Apart
from the 59,121 shares issued at par
(£l2 10s. paid) to the Bank of Victoria
shareholders, only half a million of new
capital was required to finance the big deal.
The balance was paid out of ordinary
trading funds. Indeed, as soon as it was
paid out no doubt a good deal of it was
paid back as deposit money—to be avail-
able for paying out to the next shareholder
who dropped in. The business taken over is
expanding, the chairman says.
Here are balance-sheet figures at June 30
together with the combined figures of the
two banks 12 months earlier:—-
Combined Figures.
1926. 1927.
Notes £14,506 £14,377
Bills 2,892,251 2,520,186
Deposits . . .. 50,780,057 51,239,268
Liquids £26,001,521 £24,262,059
Advances 35,472,176 37,084,511
Premises, etc. . . . . 1,384,380 1,424,670
The position is a very strong one. Deposit
ors could see 23.4 p.c. of their deposits in
cash and money at short call. For each £i
of its own the bank was holding £6 3s. 4d.
of the public’s money, and 45 p.c. (9s. in
the £) of outside liabilities was liquid. The
advances to deposits ratio was 72.3 p.c.—
a conservative margin. When the books
closed there was still £20,100 of new share
money to come in, which will advance paid
capital to £4,730,512. The ratio of outside
money to shareholders’ funds is pretty heavy,
and it can’t go much further without causing
the directors some anxiety. If the deposits
continue to grow and especially if the legiti-
mate demand for accommodation grows too,
we shall not be long before we see a further
issue of capital. The recent growth in ad-
vances may, of course, not continue. It cer-
tainly has been causing anxiety in conserva-
tive banking circles, chiefly because it has
encouraged overspending, through the T.P.
habit and otherwise.
To the investor bank scrip is essentially
for holding, and benefits are looked for by
way of new share issues. As to that, it is
interesting to look back over a few years’
history. A purchaser of five C.B.S. £l2 10s.
paid shares in Oct., 1907 —the price was then
£27 10s. —who has taken up his full propor-
tion of the seven subsequent new issues, all
of them at par, would have exactly doubled
his original capital outlay at an average cost
of £l7 3s. 9d. per -share. At that figure
the return is £7 ss. sd. p.c., and at the
present market price, £23 12s. 6d. per share,
his holding (16 shares) would be worth
£378, thus showing a capital profit of £lO3.
A purchaser at £23 per share in 1918 who
has acted similarly can show results almost
as good. The return to the investor at the
current price, stated above, is £5 ss. lOd.
p.c. on a 10 p.c. basis. The figures once
more suggest that the man who plays for
safety scores highest in the long run.
* $ * *
Australian Securities, Ltd. —Brown
decides to buy a motor-car, or somebody
decides it for him. Unfortunately he hasn’t
the money to pay for it; and the idea ot
waiting to buy until you can pay is repug-
nant to the present generation. There are
plenty of people who are prepared to accom-
modate Brown. So he makes his round of
the motor-houses, and decides on his ’bus.
He pays a deposit, signs a hire-purchase
agreement and a few promissory notes, and
drives the thing away. Then the seller
gets busy. He can’t afford to be out
of all the money the car represents, be-
cause he has had to pay for it himself.
First a comprehensive policy is taken out
to cover the loss of the car by theft, lire
or otherwise. Then the hire-purchase agree-
ment and the p.n.’s endorsed by the seller
of the car are sold to an investment co.; and
the investment co. takes out still another
policy, if it can. to insure payment of the
p.n.’s. This insurance, which seems to have
been thus far pretty readily available, costs
from 20s. to 30s. p.c. Nominally, the in-
vestment co. pays for this policy, whereas
the hire-purchaser had to pay for the policy
covering loss by theft, fire, etc.; but actu-
ally, of course, Brown, whom we have lost
sight of since he drove off with his new
“purchase,” pays the lot. Or, if he doesn’t,
somebody’s calculations go to pieces.
Among the people who decided to accom-
modate Brown was Australian Securities,
Ltd., which had the backing of those very
capable auditors and business managers,
Allard, Wav and Hardie. The prospectus
was dated April 12. 1926, but “the co. did
not receive the full benefit of its capital
before Jan. 1, 1927.” In all 400,000 shares
of £1 were offered by Federal Underwriters,
Ltd., the latter to have the right to take up
the other 100.000 shares at par within three
years. Though there was no other payment
to the promoters or underwriters beyond
commission, no less than £40,373 of the
present paid capital of £195,432 is repre-
sented by preliminary expenses—that is to
say, nearly 21 p.c. Considering the pull of
the names on the prospectus, this seems an
awful lot of money—for Brown to pay. So far
he has been doing it pretty satisfactorily.
With the help of £41,414 of outside money
(including deposits at call), a profit of
£10,842 is claimed after providing for taxa-
tion ; and that is only a shade over 10
p.c. p.a. on the present paid capital, even if
nothing at all was earned in the first half
of the year. The results don’t seem quite
as brilliant as the prospectus estimates;
but it may not be a fair thing to judge
on this first year’s figures. All that is
obvious (apart from the high cost of flota-
tion, etc.) is that there lias been no shortage
of Browns. The directors nay a 5 p.c. div.“
£8338, which will leave £2504 towards replac-
ing the intangibles. It would have been
better had £10,842 been left. While one-
fifth of the capital is represented by no-
thing but hot air, obviously the intact
capital will have to earn 10 p.c. to enable
the co. to pay an 8 p.c. dividend.

Sales were a record, the expense ratio was
more favorable, and the two combined sent
the year’s net profit un with a rush. Gross
profit (£127,111) improved 76 p.c., and exes,
ate up 77.9 p.c. of the total compared with
87.8 p.c. in 1926. Chief contributing factors to
the improvement were the cutting out of one
or two costly but disappointing lines, exten-
sion of business to suburban areas, bigger
trade in the billiard and player-piano sections
and standardisation of the Salonola wireless.
Competition was exceedingly keen, and is
likely to remain so in the talking-machine
business. The question therefore arises, What
is the quality of the trade and can the in-
creased business be held? Of course, one
can’t tell for certain; so much depends on
conditions quite outside the co.’s control. But
so far as inquiries can be carried the pro-
spects seem quite good. Thus during the year
investigating accountants overhauled the whole
of the T.P. book debts, and provision for con-
tingencies was made before net profit was
written. Also collection costs were put under
the microscope, and every visible leak was
stopped.
But though a good profit was earned, it had
not all been collected, so rather than increase
the already heavy debt to outsiders the board
held over a half-year’s pref. dividend and
passed the ordinary div. The wisdom of that
action is apparent when it is remembered
that T.P. houses are finding it more difficult
now to get all the accommodation they re-
quire, except at a high rate, and results in
the current term should benefit.
Here are balance-sheet figures:—

The thing that strikes the eye there is that
the co. does a good deal of its trade on other
people’s money. Liabilities were £59,256
heavier at March 31, and for every £1 in the
business owned by shareholders there was
13s. lid. of outside money at risk; the
amount was about Ds. a year earlier. Book
debts, up £57,602. represent 51.4 p.c. of the
tangible assets. The year’s increase in. stocks
was £14,671; plant, etc., £5829. and freehold,
etc., £12,386.
The market looks at the co.’s capital, of
which £74,500 is represented by intangibles,
takes a cautious view of the risks and pays
only 15s. for the £1 prefs.—there are 200,000
of them carrying a cumulative 8 p.c.; they
have priority as to capital in a wind-up and
are backed by net tangibles totalling 235. 3d.
each. For the much less attractive ords.,
with a net tangible backing of 7s. Bd. per
£1 shares, 14s. is paid.

Costs got a flying start, three years back
and threatened to overtake the 10 p.c. div.
Net profit, however, lengthened its lead last
year by £3768 and now wears a much
healthier appearance at 17.5 p.c. of paid
capital compared with 10, 11.1 and 8.6 p.c.
back to 1924. From what was left after
paying the last div. £3712 went to wipe
out the cost of building alterations, leaving
only a few pounds for reserves, which are
comfortably proportioned at over 41 p.c. of
capital. In addition there is a depreciation
reserve —£22,645 (up £lBBB) —which is not
counted in with savings in the table. Good-
will does not appear on the sheet, and with
a capital of only £50,000 the co. has been
able to get itself ticketed in the public mind
with propositions many times wealthier. A
long-headed management and a ballroom
have helped tremendously in that direction.

But when you cast your eye down that
profits column it should be remembered that
paid capital has nearly doubled in the last four
years. Growing competition and climbing
costs have put a brake on net profits, for a
while at any rate. Last year’s £40.554 was
equal to 13.5 p.c. on capital compared with
15.1, 17.2 and 21.4 p.c. back to 1924. The
year’s gross returns fell away £8932, but
the management was able to make a cut of
£7987 in outgo, leaving net only £945 poorer.
Exes, gobbled up 80 p.c. of gross earnings,
the same proportion as in the two preceding
years: in the pre-war year the bite was
only 68 p.c. The 10 p.c. div., which called
for £2443 more than in 1926, was neverthe-
less handed out with comfort, £B5OO of the
year’s profits remaining in the business after
£2169 had first been set aside for contin-
gencies. The latter reserve, £30,553 at June
30, is not included with the lay-by in the
table, but it is probably true profit: twice
in the last dozen years has that pot been
dipped into for bonuses to shareholders.
To meet the competition of bottled ales
and lagers from the Southern States the co.
has put a light bitter ale and lager on the
market. That and the effect of the drought
in Central-Western Queensland, where the
co. does a large trade, are seen in these
figures:—
1020. 1924. 1925. 1926. 1927.

To get the new brew-on the market meant
that customers had to be financed. Book
debts increased £109,536 to £276,058, and
£40581 was spent on freehold, plant, etc.,
during the year. The board went outside
for the money, and liabilities more than
doubled. The nature of the debt is not dis-
closed, but it is possible that a good deal
of paner was issued. The co. has never
been able to win a market in the North,
where the Carlton, of Melbourne, has a
strong hold, but keen management and a
progressive policy has given it a good grip
all over Central, Western and Southern
Queensland, where it is well placed to meet
competition. Paid capital (£298,851) has
the visible support of £104,053 of reserves,
including the store for contingencies, and a
lot of modern machinery has recently been
installed. For the £1 shares, with a net
tangible backing of 265. lid., there are
buyers 28s. 3d., sellers 28s. 4d., giving a
return of 7.1 p.c. on a 10 p.c. div.
* * * *
Australian Benefit Life Assurance
Society, Ltd. (Sydney).—This co., now in
its eighth year, has a share capital of
£43,544, of which no less than £21,374 is
represented by Establishment Account; and
there is no Assurance Fund. This means
that less than £22,170 of the capital is
intact, because against that residue has to
be set the accrued liability under the poli-
cies in force. Nor were last year’s figures
at all cheering.. In this seventh year of
the co.’s operations the total premium in-
come was £7571 and the exes., apart from
brokerage on new capital issued, were £6936,
the balance being insufficient to pay claims
and surrenders. Interest brought in £783;
there was £477 left, which was used, not to
provide for future claims, but to reduce
Establishment Account. That on such a
capital the interest earnings were so small
suggests that there are a good many odds
and ends among the assets which are non-
productive. It is astonishing that, on such
figures, it should have been possible to get
in more capital during the year, but actually
6415 new 10s. shares appear to have been
sold. The co.’s only hope lies not in getting
in more capital but in severely cutting
costs; and no doubt that is easier said than
done.

* * * *
Dunlop Rubber Co. of Australia, Ltd.
—This co. has sold a half-million fl ordi-
naries at 255. to the English Dunlop co.,
which also has a four years’ option over a
further half-million at 305.; and a subsi-
diary, jointly owned, is to be formed in
Maoriland. The deal should greatly
strengthen the trade position of the
Australian co., which will not only secure
ample funds to finance development, but will
gain the use of valuable trade secrets and
processes. The deal, nevertheless, disap-
pointed the local market, which had expected
a new share issue and paid as much as
325. Gd. for the ords. a month ago. Recent
sales have been at 205., showing the investor
just a shade under 7 p.c. p.a. on a 10 p.c.
div. basis.
* * * *
The A.P.A. —While the meeting of the
distraught A.P.A. was in progress, a share-
holder rose and said that it was all very
well for these gentlemen to throw writs
around, but would they please, remember
that his (the speaker’s) money was at stake
—and the money of hundreds of people like
him? Couldn’t they compose their differ-
ences? Promptly Boyd, of Victoria, jumped
to his feet. “I’m ready,” he said; “I’ll go
into a room with Mr. Paul (the chairman)
and one or two others, and agree, if they
will, not to come out till we’ve threshed this
business out and arrived at an agreement.”
That began the settlement. In E. S. Paul
the co. fortunately had a man who could
think quickly and clearly, and before the
representatives from the various States went
back home the dozen or two writs that had
been flying around were withdrawn, a basis
for future work was agreed on, and a couple
or so men are to be got in from outside ro
strengthen the board.
* * * *
A. Volkman returns to the charge:—
The clerk of the Townsville Harbor Board has
almost foamed at the mouth over the reflections I
lately flung at the authorities of that port on account
of the outrageous charges imposed on miners and
others having the misfortune to be obliged to use
it. But the foam won’t alter the facts; I have
paid, and I know —and I forward my documents
that The Bulletin may know, too. I sent 13 tons
13cwt. 3qr. from Burketown to Cairns. The Cairns
charges were: Forwarding and agency, 145.; labor
loading to trucks, £2 13s. 4d. ; total ss. per ton.
Now for Townsville on a shipment of 37 tons 12cwt.:
port charges, 7s. per ton, £l3 3s. 3d. ; import
dues, 35., £5 12s. lOd. ; agency, 2s. 6d., £4 14s. ;
total 12s. 6d. a ton. In addition there is Harbor
Board storage, £2 10s. Bd.; and a further debit of
19s. has been received. The storage consisted of
leaving the ore uncovered for about six weeks, with
resultant bleaching, being mostly responsible for
much rebagging at Soviet rates. In explanation,
Samuel Allen and Sons say: “The item of 7s. per
ton shown on our statement as the port charges covers
landing charge on all cargo landed on to the jetty,
and is a charge made by the shipping companies.”
But the same cos. make no such charge at Cairns.
Hence I wired the owners (John Burke, Ltd.). They
replied: “Know nothing of any such charges.” Inci-
dentally, a refund of Is. per ton has since been
made owing to the charge having been made as for
concentrates instead of for crude ore. I don’t care
what the charges are called —harbor dyes or spongers’
dues; they are collected by and at the port, and
the fact remains that the unfortunates who use the
port must pay them.
* * * *
The stoppage at Mt. Morgan will just
about close down the Baralaba coalfield,
Dawson R. (Q.). The output from the
two mines barely touched 20,000 tons for the
past six months. The Valley mine supplies
Mt. Morgan, and the State also supplies slack
at a cheap rate. The State mine is finding
the railways a poor customer owing to the
loco, crews’ objection to the fuel. Styx coal
now gets the preference, and the State’s
capital in the Dawson Valley railway and
mines looks irrecoverable.
* * * *
The Stanhills tin battery, on the Croydon
field (N.Q.), is certainly not working over-
time ; it must be confessed, after a period of
four months in commission, that everything
is disappointing. There has been nothing of
the bonanza type to stir the prospectors’
pulses, and operations are sluggish and on
a small scale, while exemptions are num-
erous. The average of ore crushed thus far
is only between 2 and 3 per cent., and as
several individual crushings have touched 10
p.c. it means very poor ore for some claims
—they have no doubt already dropped by the
wayside. The whole year will probably not
see more than a couple of hundred tons of
ore treated, and the Mines Department will
find mill redemption a slow proceeding at
the present rate. The Cassiterite, Queen
and Mauretania are putting out small ton-
nages only.
* * * *
The Hugarloaf Tin Co., operating at
Ivyoomba, has given the Stanthorpe (Q.)
field a new impulse, and this year’s figures
will greatly surpass recent years. The co.
is working four sluicing plants around
Sugarloaf. including the old Brisbane and
Chinaman’s Flat. At present between 50.000
and 70,000 cub. yds. are treated monthly
and gross values range from £9OOO to
£lO,OOO. It has given a great tillip to the
local dredging industry.
* * $ *
Irvinebank tin shows (N.Q.) are pegging
along on hope and a little rich tin ore, but
there are no purple patches on the metal-
liferous horizon. The Perseverance and
Wyatts are producing small tonnages, and
the oldest prospector firmly believes that they
will pick up the big lode in the Great
Southern, lost as far back as 18S5 ; it has
not been seen since that date. The Jumna
has about 90 tons of fair-looking crushing
stuff, and if the tables confirm this the Iteid
option will be exercised.
* * * *
No output is coming forward from the
Silver Spur mine (Q.), now and lately
under six months’ exemption from labor con-
ditions on the ground tnat the mine is unpay-
able under present conditions. Edgar Hall’s
activities are therefore Confined to seeing the
old mine kept unwatered and repaired until
someone hits a bright scheme. Prospects are
glum.
* * * *
All the Palmer River Gold Co.’s plant and
gear, which lay at Cooktown for the past
two years, has been transported to Laura in
readiness for motor-trucking to the field. The
co. now awaits the steel pontoon-dredge,
which is en route from Melbourne, and the
directors estimate seven years’ work ahead
once the dredge gets at it. Boring tests
proved 3.484,000 c.y. gravel, averaging
Is. Id.; in some sections the average was
as much as 4s. 2d. per c.y. General-manager
Baker anticipates no difficulty in transport-
in'’ 1 the plant from Laura to the field. The
future of the plnokv co. will be watched with
interest by old Palmer miners.
* * * *
California Tin, with a capital of £60,000
in 240 000 shares of ss. each, has secured
three miles of California Creek and a 10-acre
reefing lease near by. The property is at
Ord, on the Cairns-Einasleigli railway (Q-).
Sydney people are running the show; C. T.
Elmslie is chairman.
* * * *
N.S.W. Government-geologist Jaquet says
the Tallanook tin lodes, near Albury, are of
the same tyne as the Euriowie. VVaukaroo
and other tin-lodes on the Barrier. Those
shows looked fine in spots; they were beauti-
ful things to float cos. and syndicates on.
But you couldn’t tell from the showings in
one hole what you’d have a foot away, and
it was generally nothing. Euriowie alone cost
over a hundred thousand and never returned
a penny.
% s’: #
New Guinea Gold and Exploration Co.,
one of the several Bulolo alluvial shows that
Mush (or otherwise) almost unseen, has
issued a brief development report. _ The co.’s
holdings are on Edie Creek, and include a
half-share in one of four leases pegged out
on the lode formation reported to have been
discovered there in .Tune last, and 20 acres
cut off from the original Edie Creek leases,
the latter being still in the “applied for”
stage. The half share is being worked, arid
in spite of heavy overburden and the fact
that the dirt has to be carted several
chains to the creek to be washed, the last
week in July returned the co. 89oz. for its
half share. This show is now being mined
with a drive, the wash being sft. deen and
3ft. 6in. wide. This should cut out the
overburden difficulty, for there seems to be
no lack of handy timber for propping, etc.
But, after the manner of Bulolo enterprises,
not a word about costs.

W. G. Royal, of Edie Creek (N.G.),
writes to say that the “Wild Cat” errs in

vendor’s interest in the Edie Creek Consoli-
dated.
* * * *
Tom Horton, one of the original Austral
Malay Syndicate, and of late connected with
the Rundell Miles interests in Sydney, is
still on the Bulolo goldfield. Some time ago
he headed a party to New Guinea, which
included H. Bethune, one of Sydney Siam's
field engineers. Their objective was the
testing of several leases held by Guinea
Gold on the Bulolo below that co.’s
original holdings. Work has been de-
layed for various reasons, and the direc-
tors have decided to allow Horton to con-
centrate on the Guinea Gold Central leases,
three in number. An option covering 400
acres of these has been given by Guinea
Gold Central to the people whom Horton
is representing. The cabled report is some-
what obscure, for it was understood that aii
option had already been granted. Possibly
the inspecting party is desirous _of investi-
gating- still further into possibilities before
making a final report.
* * * *
Southern Cross (W.A.) is deeply inter-
ested in the proposed Federal and State
assistance to Westralian mining. At this
centre is the derelict Frasers, which it is
claimed is worth further development. In
the ’nineties this mine paid a large sum in
dividends and was then acquired by the
British and Foreign Development Syndi-
cate on terms most displeasing to a num-
ber of shareholders. Mine development soon
afterwards ceased suddenly and the syndi-
cate proceeded to exhaust the reserve of ore
the former management had blocked out, and
also treated a 200.000-ton heap of residues,
the average gold content of which was 12s.
per ton. When everything in tms way was
cleaned up as thoroughly as possible the
mine was let on tribute, and when tributors
were finished water was permitted to rise
in the sand filled workings. Later a local
diamond-drilling syndicate put down several
bores to a depth of 1000 ft. Since then por-
tions of the property have been worked from
time to time by parties of working miners,
but without much success.
* * * *
Chairman Moreing, at the Sons of Gwalia
London meeting on June 30: —
The State and Federal Governments and the
leaders of the unions are to blame for the gradual
wiping off the map of the self-contained and pros-
perous community of Leonora. The directors have
striven patiently and arduously to avoid this
calamity. More than that they cannot do.”
To which State Minister for Mines Munsie
(W.A.) replies:—
The State Government offered to financially
assist the co. some time ago conditionally on it
making an effort to help itself, lut it declined to
make such an effort and suggested that the Gov-
ernment should do everything. Later, when salvag-
ing was threatened, the Government offered to find
£5030 to form part of any loan which might sub-
sequently be decided upon, but this offer was curtly
refused by Mr. Moreing as chairman of the co. Later
still the Government offered to render considerable
financial assistance with a view to prolonging the
life of the mine and preventing disaster to Leonora ;
but Mr. Moreing and his colleagues have not yet said
whether they will or won’t accept this help.
We all know that every mine has to come
to an end; but we fight against recognising
that the end has come to our mine. There
are nearly always vested interests concerned
in getting somebody to put up a few pounds
to keep the jobs going. One time it was the
shareholder who was “stung.” Nowadays it
is the State. For Private Enterprise to
howl for State help and charge the Govern-
ment with being responsible for the death
of the show when the help isn’t forthcom-
ing is more than inconsistent; it is in-
fernal impudence.
* * * *
As a result of Assistant-State-Mihing-
Engineer Wilson’s inspection Glenelg Hills
leaseholders (W.A.) may bank on a treat-
ment plant at an early date. The main
difficulty is lack of water. While ample
rain falls in winter, there are no catch-
ment areas, and mine developments are not
deep enough to disclose underground sup-
plies. Wilson was sufficiently impressed
with prospects to recommend the Minister
to bore, and the Minister has approved.
Glenelg Queen, Great Beacon and Reward
leases have considerable tonnages raised to
surface and blocked out, while a number of
leaseholders have smaller parcels ready for
a mill.
* * * *
Numerous tributors in Great Boulder
Prop. (IY.A.) ’frankly confess to breaking
out and exposing very satisfactory quanti-
ties of high-grade ore, but complain that
the co. will treat only limited quantities
and dawdles over them. - But, they say, like
the victims of the razor pushes, “we deem it
inadvisable to squeal.” Tributors maintain
that the reason behind the policy indicated
is that the Kalgoorlie cos. do not wish gold-
production to reach too high a point pend-
ing the decision of Federal and State Gov-
ernments with regard to assistance to min-
ing.
* * * *
From London: —
Oroya Links’ new finance co. is taking over Oroya
Links Co.’s investments valued at £115,000, paying
for them 1,150,000 fully-paid shares of 2s. each,
which will be distributed to Oroya Links share-
holders on the basis of one for one. That the capital
available shall be commensurate with the operations
undertaken by the finance co., the Oroya Links Co.
proposes to give each of its shareholders the right
to apply for one additional share at par in the finance
co. for each Oroya Links share now held. If Links
shareholders do this, a further £115.000 will be
provided, making a total of £230,000 of issued
capital. The authorised capital of the finance co.
has not yet been fixed owing to negotiations with an
important group whose co-operation the board hopes
to obtain. In order to distribute the shares mentioned,
Oroya Links will go into liquidation. As regards
the co.’s Kalgoorlie mine and plant, the board has
decided, in the event of an amalgamation of com-
panies on that field, to distribute among shareholders
shares received by the co. in such amalgamation. If,
however, an amalgamation of Kalgoorlie properties
doesn’t take place, the board proposes to form a
Westralian co. of £60,000 in Is. shares to acquire
Oroya Links mine and plant and provide a certain
amount of working capital, in consideration of the
allotment to Oroya Links Co. of 1,150,000 fully-paid
Is. shares for distribution to Oroya Links share-
holders. This, with one fully-paid finance eo.’s 2s.
share which Oroya Links proposes to give its share-
holders, will make a total distribution equivalent to
3s. per share to them. Pending all this or something
else being done, Oroya Links has asked the South
Kalgurli Co. to represent it at Kalgoorlie.
* * * *
The original Kampar River, F.M.S., ran
short of funds with its suction-cutter dredge
half built, though the property was, and still
is, considered a good one by people who know
it. In its adversity it was put under option
to a London syndicate. The latter liked the
proposition, but wanted its own boring tests.
The verdict of its expert was that values
were not up to the required standard. It
was then agreed that the property should
be rebored by an independent valuer, by
whose decision both parties would abide. His
decision backed the original boring estimate.
Thereupon the London syndicate, which had
paid no deposit and was subject to no
penalty, faded out of the picture. Rex
Thompson, one of the directors, then took
the option around Melbourne, and Thomo
Tin, after further boring trials, decided to
have a shot at it. The old name of Kampar
River, with its associations, was dropped,
and as Tujoh Tin the Kampar River propo-
sition was floated in Melbourne in July with
a nominal capital of £350.000, of which
£251.000 may be considered as subscribed,
though it ineiudes share adjustments, and the
actual new cash is about £90.000 —quite
enough for the now co.’s purposes. To the
oriainal Kampar River property has been
added a small adjacent area, known as
Bayas Tujoh, whose name has been borrowed.
Original Kampar River shareholders get out
with 15s. in the £. Considering all the
circumstances, they are lucky.
