The papers reported last week the death
of the first manager of the great Broken
Hill Prop. mine. But Sam Wilson was
scarcely that. It is hard to say who was the
first manager. When the men on Mt. Gipps
station formed a syndicate the first odd
jobs didn’t need a manager; but when a
100 ft. shaft had to be sunk it was from
Dave James, the station contractor, that
information could be got, and he had to pass
the work. After him Willie Jamieson, the
surveyor, was appointed manager—he was
the first man to get paid for it. Then came
S. B. Wilson, who knew a bit about mining
T. E. RUTH, of Pitt-street Congregational
Church, Sydney, the most broadcasted
parson in Australia.
but not much; so, when things got bigger,
W. H. Patton was imported from the
Comstock —he was supposed to know every-
thing, and never denied it. After spending
a while in Westralia Sam Wilson came back
to N. S. Wales, bought Cowal station near
Wyalong, and he and his boys have ever
since worked it.
Australia’s old acquaintance Lord Deer-
hurst died in England the other day. He
was aide to a Vic. Governor nearly 40
years' ago and obtained some notoriety
through his differences with Bob Sievier, then
a cash bookmaker, who was destined to win
tlie Oaks and marry a Marquis’s sister.
Deerhurst was 62, but he didn’t live long
enough to become a real nobleman and take
his seat in the House of Lords. His father,
that tough old sportsman the Earl of
Coventry, is in his 00th year.
Arthur Goodhew, Mayor of Goulburn
CN.S.W.), sends ornamental trees to New
Caiedonia and other islands in the Pacific.
His nursery turns out a wide variety of
trees and flowering plants. He served a
quaint apprenticeship to the business, start-
ing as a cadet ’board ship; he left the sea
to become a chef, and finally decided to say
it with flowers.
Frank Green, the new Sergeant-at-Arms
in the Reps., is not likely to be nervous
when he announces Mr. Speaker for the first
time. For some years he was Clerk Assistant
in the Speck's House of Assembly, and after
his nonagenarian predecessor laid down the
mace Green carried on both jobs; every-
thing seemed settled for a half-century of
quietness when the war come. Green cap-
tained a company of the “Fortieth” through
about three years of the unpleasantness with
scarcely a day off duty—he was adjutant at
the finish—and came back a long time after
the Armistice. Then he crossed to a clerkship
in the Reps. He is the oflicial historian of
the “Fortieth,” and his book is likely to sur-
vive : it is full of quiet humor and vivid
writing.
The willingness of Kiernan, M.L.C., furni-
ture and wireless purveyor, to take the
Agency-General has not impressed a majo-
rity of the Hogan Ministry (Vic.), and it
looks as if the job will be left unfilled for a
while, or it may be changed into a general-
agency. Gillies once exclaimed indignantly
at the idea of the Agent-General being
turned into a “mere butterman,” but now-
adays a butterman might be of more use
to the State than a mere politician.
Sub-inspector Mick Porltey, dead in Bris-
bane at 60, was an excellent type of the Aus-
tralian mounted policeman. He was a close
student of the abo., and one of liis most
characteristic feats was based on his know-
ledge of their ways. A Normanton black
murdered a white man near Chinchilla.
Calculating that the murderer would make
for his old haunts, 1500 miles to the north.
Porltey caught a steamer for the Gulf town
and waited for Binglii to walk into the trap,
which, in due course, he did.
Arthur Tonge, whom Canterbury (N.S.W.)
Laborites placed streets ahead of Mostyn,
Sydney’s Bud Mayor, in the selection
ballot—the usual protest was lodged—is
another of the numerous Labor politicians
whose union affiliation is with the Clerical
Association. 1 Inlike many of the others,
Tonge was really a clerk. Pie is a nominee
member of the Leg. Assembly, having suc-
ceeded A. D. Kay when the Government
bought that worthy’s seat with a billet at
£l5OO a year. Incidentally, the Federal con-
stituency—Reid—which includes Canterbury,
is represented by an'otlier Clerical Unionist,
Percy Coleman —an ex-sec. of the organisa-
tion.
Howard Ashton wins the distinction of
being the first artist to have a painting
purchased by the Commonwealth Art Advis-
ory Board, which has the job of getting
together a collection for Canberra. Two
etchings by Sid Long and a couple of litho-
graphs by the Adelaide artist Goodchild
have also been purchased. This is apart
from the selection to be made from the pic-
tures lent for the opening b.v the Duke.
The purchase of a number of these has
been recommended by the_ Aijt Advisory
Board, the chairman of which is Mann, of
the N.S.W. National Gallery, the other mem-
bers being Will Ashton —who, by the way,
is not -related to Iloward—and Harold
Herbert.
George Gordon McCrae, who died in Mel-
bourne this week, could remember the city
86 years ago, and Sydney earlier still. His
father, a Scotch lawyer, took up country in
the new settlement in IS4I ; but the boys went
off the land then as they do now, and two of
the McCraes joined the bank. One stayed and
was an inspector in Sydney when he retired:
Gordon passed into the Public Service and
was Deputy Registrar-General when the
regulations decided that he was too old to
stay, and that was 33 years ago. All his
life his tastes were artistic and literary. Pie
was the friend and helper of that group
which made the Colonial Monthly a
brilliant thing in the ’sixties and which
founded the Yorick Club —Marcus Clarke
was one of them and Gordon and Kendall
came under his influence later. Half a dozen
books stand to his credit and hundreds of
scattered ballads and other verse. He re-
fused to grow old; his hand was steady at
90, and when past that age he wrote
humorous verse for The Bulletin, to which
lie contributed for over 35 years. No Aus-
tralian writer ever made more friends or
better deserved them. Of his children two
are well known to this generation of readers
—Dorothy Frances Perry, of Christchurch
(M.L.), and Hugh, the co-editor of tne
Trtad.
Eddo Cooper, who died at Nerang (Q.)
recently, was an ardent amateur ethnologist
and anthropologist. At Birribon, the home-
stead where he lived his 45 years, he did
valuable research work among the aboriginal
kitchen middens with which that shellfish-
abounding littoral is studded. He was a
recognised authority on abo. archaeology.
Arthur Beavan. retired from the N SAV.
Prisons Department after 43 years’ service,
can give a biographical sketch of most of the
State’s notorious criminals. For 37 years he
was on the staff of the Parramatta Gaol,
and as latterly he supervised the training of
the long-sentence men his knowledge was
first-hand. He knew Freeman who, in 1900,
broke gaol by squeezing through a window,
throwing himself upon some wires 10 feet
below and 60 feet above a stone-paved court-
yard, and crawling along them to liberty.
The Sydney dentist Louis Bertrand, who did
29 years before he was released and deported,
was the long-distance prisoner of his recol-
lection.
A. L. Herdman, most suave and polished
of all Maoriland Judges, started as a bank
clerk —his father managed the Dunedin office
of the National of M.L. —and then took to
law and politics. Massey made him Attorney-
General when the Reformers came out of
JUSTICE A. L. HERDMAN.
the wilderness in 1912, and he had a Cabinet
seat for six years, when he resigned from
the Government to sit on the Supreme Court
Bench. He has lived down the imputation
of the envious that his appointment w'as a
reward for political services.
A. 11. Whittingliam spent most of his life
in Queensland, but the terms of his will
show that he must have cherished the kind-
liest memories of his schooldays at Geelong
“Grammar.” Whittingliam died childless.
His will makes provision for his widow, and
is a curious mixture of racing and church
bequests; but the bulk of his estate goes
to liis old school. The gift is worth about
£lOO,OOO.
• Australian scientific circles show another
gap with the passing of Tom Wliitelegge, of
Sydney Museum. Wliitelegge was trained
as a hat-maker, but his love of nature
moved him to other walks of life. Coming
to Australia, he joined the Museum staff,
the lower invertebrates being his special
care. When he was 58—in 1908—lie re-
tired ; but enthusiasm is a great driving
force: he kept on as a voluntary worker.
Wliitelegge was an authority on Australian
fauna.
In most cases sponsors or parents are
responsible for the chain of names that have
to be carried through ife, but Brown, sac.
of Vic. Parliamentary Standing Committee
on Railways, has gone to the trouble of
executing a deed poll to enlarge his signa-
ture. Instead of plain George Brown he
is now George Wallace Wilson Brown. It
lias necessitated altering official records,
including his re-gazettal as a J.P.
J. W. Lee, warden for the Cook elector-
ate (N.Q.), thinks nothing of a thousand-
mile jaunt with horse and pack-saddle.
During a recent trip to inspect the gold
find at the Daintrce he had to cross Mt.
Borneo. I..ee was leading a party along
a precipice when an 18ft. python startled
him into a wild jump. Both warden and
python-escaped. When he isn’t doing warden
duty Lee acts as P.M. and coroner and
chairman of almost everything.
A shire president told Bailey, Vic. Minister
for Lands, the other day that he had “a
deep up-hill to fight.” Bailey didn’t look
alarmed, probably because he had got used
to that sort of thing when he served in
the South African War. The Boer holders of
kopjes were pretty deep, and he fought a
few of them.
LI. W. Lee, ex-M.H.R. and one of the
pioneers of the Ma State’s dairying industry,
will judge no more at country shows. He
represented Cowper in the second Common-
wealth Parliament, and just missed being
re-elected in 1901. Seventy-one at the finish,
he lived to see cold-storage—of which he
early visioned the possibilities—transform
the haphazard dairying activities of the
coastal farmers into one of the State’s most
important industries.
“S.G.”: I wrnnder whether Sydney’s old
friend Carrington (B. 11/8,/’27) joined in
commemorating the Canning centenary,
briefly mentioned in the cables? The first
Carrington answered to the name of Smith.
Dining, at his house after the ennoblement,
the impish Canning wrote in chalk on the
outside of the hall-door
Little Bobby Smith lives here.
Billy Pitt made him a peer
And took the pen from behind his ear.
Carrington, late Smith, never forgave the
author of the gibe, which his grandson can
scarcely have forgotten.
P. A. Blundell, just retired from the
managing-directorship of Queensland Trus-
tees, is one of a clan closely connected with
Bananaland’s beginnings. The fruits of his
34 years of service with the co. were too
valuable to be lost; he retains a seat on the
board. His fellow-directors and the staff
marked his dropping of the reins with an
illuminated address and expressions of good-
will such as do not often come the way of
commercial heads.
“Dan Sofo”: The record of Victorian geo-
logists abroad is even better than stated (B.
13/l/’27). In West Africa the Directors of
Geological Surveys in all three colonies are
Victorians. The Gold Coast G. S. is run by
A. E. Kitson, with whom is O. A. L. White-
law ; both were for many years with the
Vic. Geol. Survey. The Director in Nigeria
is Dr. R. C. Wilson and in Sierra Leone
Dr. N. C. Junner. both graduates of Melb.
Uni. Dr. E. O. Teale (also of Melb. Uni.)
controls the survey in Tanganyika, East
Africa, and R. A. Farquaharson, formerly
of Westralia, combines the posts of Director
of Geolog. Survey and Director of Agricul-
ture in Somaliland.
The Last Post has sounded over the
remains of William Charles Smith, a 97-year-
old Crimean veteran, of Oatlev, near Sydney.
Smith served three years under Raglan and
his successors. Almost the whole of his work-
ing life in Australia was spent in the service
of the N.S.W. Harbors and Rivers Depart-
ment, but he retired before Federation came.
“M.H.”: My grandparents, who came to
Tassy in 1522, lived next door to Captain
Kelly, Hobart’s first harbor-master. I have
a vicarious mental picture of the old captain,
jovial, portly and autocratic, back from one
of his successful sealing or whaling expe-
ditions, titivating before a mirror in prep-
aration for some social fray, whilst his
dutiful daughters fluttered round him. He
bulked large in the public eye physically,
no less than socially, and once won a bet that
his trousers would hold five bushels of wheat.
His soundings of the west coast, made when
he discovered Macquarie Harbor, are still in
use, and Kelly’s Basin is a lasting reminder
of his trip round the Speck in a whale-
boat. Kelly was born at Parramatta in
3791. He must have been one of our first
native sons.
The three knights in Vic. Assembly—Pea-
cock, McPherson and Bowser—all sit in
opposition. Peacock and McPherson are on
the front Bench, and Bowser in the corner.
If Speaker Snowball lasts in the chair for
three years he is sure to get a sirship.
CAPTAIN C. H. PETERS, M.C., of
Kovertson and Mullens, Melbourne,
the driving force behind Australian
Authors’ Week.
Celebrations connected with the opening of
additions to Malvern Town Hall (Vic.)
brought to light the fact that the late Alfred
Deakin, then Minister for Public Works
under James Service (in the Great Coali-
tion), laid the foundation-stone of the hall
in 1885. Malvern is proud of this link with
the man who in after years was thrice
Prime Minister of the Commonwealth.
THE CROWNING GLORY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The summit of Mount Blanc has been christened
‘Peak Mussolini’ by Italian Fascists.”
Aloft, severe, immutable,
Its gaze o’er storied lands it flings .
Since history’s dat n a breathless spell
Around its dazzling summit clings.
A myriad morns and evenings
It stabbed the skies —and lacked a name
That with supreme achievement rings
Till Benno Mussolini came.
Set hard and high in heavens to dwell,
To hear the stars’ dim communings,
Awe ever destined to compel
O’er all imaginable things,
Poor human ants in countless strings
Upon its glories gazed aflame.
Nor ever ventured christenings
Till Benno Mussolini came.
It gazed for leons none may tell
Through winters bleak and melting springs
On conquerors innumerable,
On gentle knights and lordly kings
Whose prowess many a minstrel sings.
But never mere terrestrial fame
Sought to annex such soaring wings
Till Benno Mussolini came.
Envoy.
Mount Blanc, no pjean of men to swell,
A pedestal none dared to claim,
Unpegged, untagged—at last you fell
When Benno Mussolini came.
N.s.w.

ABORIGINALITIES
MADE IN AUSTRALIA
BARNES-LINSEED OIL
“Veronal’': Recently on the banks of the
Tantawanglo River,. South Coast (N.S.W.),
I was struck by the abundance of mistletoe
on the gums near the stream. Almost every
other tree had one or more of these para-
sitic shrubs. The mistletoe bird, or Aus-
tralian flower-pecker, has spread the plant, as
it feeds on the pollen of the flower and its
berries. A little search disclosed a female
mistletoe bird faintly tinged with scarlet
busy in one of the clumps, but the gorgeous
male was not in evidence. The beautiful
domed nest is often built in clumps of the
parasite. The deeper parts of this swiftly-
flowing and cold stream harbor fine trout,
introduced many years ago.
THE BARGAIN.
COUNTRY MAGISTRATE: “Five shillings, or a week."
JACKY: “You gibbit ten tchillin’, boss, and this-fella take it out two week. Chop ’im
big-fella wood’eap belonga sargint.”
“Bihai”: The fruit mentioned by “A.M.”
(B. 21/7/’27) must have been Durio zibeth-
inus, otherwise the durian (or civet-cat)
fruit: it is commonly cultivated in Burma,
the Straits, Java and elsewhere, but I have
never heard of it growing in N.Q., though
there is no reason why it should not flourish
there. It would be interesting to know
where the Chinaman got hold of it. Inci-
dentally, the durian smells so vilely that it
is tabu in hotels in Java. The unsuspecting
tourist who drags one in with him is im-
mediately told that he or it must quit.
“Li-Chee”: The durian (“A.M.,” B.
21/7/’27) smells so atrociously that its sale
is not allowed in the main streets of Singa-
pore, yet it is sought and consumed with
gusto. It is queer to see dainty white
children devouring the putrid-smelling stuff.
The tree is a beauty—lofty, handsome and
dark-leaved —and though it bears as many as
200 fruit in a season, I paid a shilling each;
but there’s a lot in a durian. The natives
roast the large seeds like chestnuts; each
fruit contains about a dozen of them and
they are very palatable.
“Wat L. Blossom”: The fruit that smelt
to heaven was evidently what is known
in N.Q. as a jack-fruit.' Visiting a farm
near Cooktown we were persuaded to bring
home a sample, weighing about 141 b., but
the stench was such that we had to throw
it out. We were afterwards told that a
taste for it has to be acquired. A queer
thing about the jack-fruit is that it grows
from the main branches and trunk of the
tree—not from the smaller branches. Its
stalk is from 4in. to Gin. long, and up to
2in. thick, and it weighs anything from lOlb.
to 401 b.

“Brud”: On what authority did “F.R.”
(B. 7/4/’27) base his statement that. the
source of the Fly River is in the Victor
Emanuel Range 7 When he wrote, the actual
source of the Fly had not been discovered.
This has now been accomplished. “F.R.”
emphasised the value of the data collected by
tlie three previous patrols ; this consisted of
warnings of a belt of almost impassable lime-
stone country and of scarcity of water in
the region. I am able to inform members of
the Council that water was found easily, the
limestone belt was crossed in two places
and the Mandated Territory was entered and
traversed for 40 to 50 miles. During the
journey the Strickland River was found to
rise iii the A r ictor Emanuel Range, to the
north-west of the Fly. At least two points of
geographical interest have now been defi-
nitely fixed.

“Momokeni”: A coating of white clay for
widow’s weeds (B. 14/7/’27) is a quite com-
mon custom in the Purari Delta.' A widower,
on the other hand, daubs himself with pot
black and grease. For sheer cruelty the fate
of the widows of Rarai takes a lot of beat-
ing. The husband’s body is “buried up a
tree” —as the Irish R.M. put it —in a nest
of boughs; the widow squats underneath
until the body is disintegrated. The bleached
bones are then gathered up and placed in a
kiapa, or string bag, and the widow is cloistered
with it in the “widow’s hut,” a dark, gloomy
thatch structure where never light of day
penetrates. There she stays for the rest of
her life, becoming blind as a pit pony, with
limbs atrophied from lack of exercise, a living
corpse, fed on the leavings of the family
meals.

“Sea Nomad” : A wonderful sight in Torres
Strait and thereabouts are the massed clouds
of dewa, seabirds the size of pigeons and
colored black with white top-knots. Recently
I spied (as I thought! an island where no
land should be, and on levelling my glasses
distinguished what appeared to be the
branches of many trees. The “boy” crew
exclaimed “Dewa!" bu,t not till sundown was
I fully convinced that I had not discovered a
new island. Then it was transmuted into
heavy clouds visible till they melted into the
dark, as they moved towards Halfway Island.
Though I have been long at sea, the illusion
was so complete that I have since wondered
whether some of the mystery islands charted
and never found again were not in reality
clouds of these birds, hovering in the dis-
tance. On their favorite roosting islands the
tree branches are weighed down by them, so
numerous are they. They lay eggs spotted
dark-green and the size of hens’. Sacks are
filled with the eggs as fast as men can pick
them up. When tired the birds float in great
numbers on the water. The sunlight has a
most peculiar effect on them when flying.
They often manoeuvre in an endless line, then
for some reason wheel and form two lines
which pass one another. One line will then
appear snow-white ; the other jet-black.
* * * *
“L.L.P.”: At a little mining camp in
Queensland the blacks had a bad habit of
pestering the womenfolk for tucker while the
men were at work. One big buck got cheeky,
and the housewife, dragging out her hus-
hand’s shotgun, ordered him to hop over the
front fence—about 80 paces distant. I’ll
give you till I count 10,” she said. Jacky
reached the fence well within time, but he
offered such a tempting target that the angry
woman let him have the right barrel. I met
him well away from camp some time after-
wards, and asked why we hadn’t seen him
around of late. “No good to me, boss,” he
explained. “Woman down there count 10
too plurry quick.”
# * * *
“Mulga Mick”: In Nov., T 9, I was travel-
ling from the Macdonnell Range country to
the Stuart Range opal field (S.A.) with
seven horses—five packs, a spare and a riding
hack. At Alice Springs the old hands told
me I had no chance of getting through to
Oodnadatta, as drought hadn’t left a blade of
feed on the stock route and if I left the route
I could get no water, i meant to get through,
as the opals were calling; so I packed seven
extra “fifties” of flour and made johnny cakes
for the prads night and morning, hobbling
•and side-lining them at night. Without see-
ing one blade of feed, except cane-grass in the
dry river-beds, we did that trip of about
350 miles in 14 days, the neddies carrying
their own tucker. It took a fortnight in
Oodnadatta on pressed hay before the mokes
were fit to carry light packs over the last
stage of 200 miles to the opal fields.

“Graeme”: Peak Range, where I did my
little bit of jackerooing, was a mixed cattle-
and-sheep property. Old Yorky, one of the
boundary riders, was in charge of the. prize
bull at his outpost on the far n.-w. line of
fence. One day the ration carrier returned
with the news that he had found Yorky
dead in his bunk. “Good God,” roared the
manager, “is the bull all right?”

“Aquila”: According to “Black Tracker”
(B. 14/7/’27), one Jim Abrahams accounted
for 43 eagles recently. But why shoot these
birds at all? How many people can swear
that they have actually seen them kill lambs?
Of course they will eat dead animals they
find lying about, but by thus cleaning up car-
rion they lessen the blow-fly pest; for this
reason some breeders of stud sheep won’t
have eagles killed on their places. At the
Melbourne Zoo, in an enclosure containing
half a dozen eagles, lambs and kids have
been kept for years without casualties so
far. Kill the imported fox, by all means,
but leave us our noble wedge-tailed eagle.
* * * *
“Tongio West”: In my boyhood, spent near
Ballarat, I was envious of the money another
lad made by selling a variety of parrot
which he was the only one to snare or even
see. Red lory parrots were fairly plentiful,
but his birds had practically no green
feathers, and their bright colors made them
always in great demand. His method, 1
have now discovered, was to take the young
birds from the nest, pluck the feathers o 5
their backs and shoulders and rub the naked
surface with the blood of a frog. The
callous brute confessed that many birds died
during the process, but a sufficient number
survived to make the “industry” profitable.

WATTLES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
I loves all kinds o’ trees,
W’ether they’re big or small;
Pines an’ gums an’ wattles —
I’ve always loved ’em all.
An’ it ’urts, some’ow, inside,
To see one of ’em fall!
Big tall w’ite gums,
The giants of the bush —
W’erever you sees them trees
You’ll ’ear the native thrush—
The Bee-beek —an’ w’en she calls
All other birds say “Hush!”
The dark, solemn pine-trees
The long night-watch they keep ;
Talkin’ o’ this an' t’ other —
O’ floods an’ droughts an’ sheep.
Pine-trees is always talkin’,
Even in their sleep.
But w’en I smells the wattles
My ’eart turns in my breast,
An’ my mind strays back to boyhood
W’en I first went to tlie West
An’ rode with Em in the mornin’....
I think I loves them best.
K.A.
CHARLES HENRY SOUTER.

“Outback” : The Darling River fisherman
has no worry about bait, for cod bite greedily
at a caterpillar procured from the river bank.
This caterpillar tunnels into the huge river
gums, leaving home when ready to turn into
a chrysalis. It makes a silk-lined burrow
about a foot deep and caps it with a compo-
sition of silk and dirt. The fisherman, armed
with shovel and a piece of wire, locates the
burrow and removes the cap. Inserting the
wire, which is sharpened at the end and
twisted like a corkscrew, he encircles the
inmate and pulls it out.
=i= * * *
“Tiffy” : Undergoing its final tests in North
Westralian waters is a new diving dress,
the invention of a Japanese pearl cult-
crer. Very light in construction it
allows the diver to move about the bed
of the ocean with freedom and comfort. It is
topped oil with a close-fitting padded helmet
which gives the wearer a greater range of
vision than is obtained with the three port
domes now in use. By an arrangement of
the inlet valves, the air supply comes
directly under the diver’s control, instead
of being regulated from the tending boat. A
simple breathing device ensures the continual
inhaling of fresh air; when exhaled the
breath passes out directly through an exhaust
valve. A redistribution of the lead weights
gives a better balance and lessens the danger
of accidental “blowing up” to the surface.
Tests up to SO fathoms have been success-
fully made. If adopted, the new dress should
lighten the running costs of the tending boats
owing to the comparatively smalEquantity of
air required. The tender’s work will also be
made easier, as the strain on the life-line
is reduced.
* * * *
“Sea Nomad”: A wonderful thing is the
egg of the bailor shell-fish. I never knew
that shell-fisli were oviferous until I saw
an egg attached to this big fellow. It re-
sembled a mass of jelly, in weight from
one to two pounds and in form like a pine-
cone. It was perforated by many tiny holes,
in almost every one of which was a minute
black speck of matter. The egg eventually
becomes detached and floats to ground on
a reef suitable for its development. Each
black speck is an embryo shell, and the
islanders explained to me that as the egg
matures the holes expand and a little shell
drops out from each. I estimated there were
hundreds of such shells in this mass of
jelly.

studying in dingo shooting where a decoy
call is used with effect. The dingo will
approach to within 20yds. or so of the caller,
will stop, bare his teeth and snarl. It is

“Rob P.”: In Queensland nowadays short
shrift is given to the man who carries his
swag out back. Squatters
rarely employ labor that comes
to them on Shanks’s pony,
and ration hand-outs were
stopped by agreement years ago
—in W.Q., anyway. When an
additional man is required on
a station the rule now is to
write to or ring up the local
labor-agent, and anyone not
carrying his introductory letter
goes unemployed. The experi-
enced station-worker knows this,
so he hangs round the township
getting rations from the police,
if necessary, till something turns
up. When he does go to a job
he leaves behind him an agree-
ment that the labor-agent gets
the first week’s pay. Under the
old order a swagman not finding
work on one station was sure
of enough rations to carry him
on to the next. The custom was
universal in W.Q. and N.S.W.
Squatters agreed that it brought
them necessary labor, and that
what one couldn’t use might be
useful to another, so the whaler
was helped on from station to
static.-i until he found a job. Those days
are gone.
* * * *
“Up North”: The Torres Islanders erect
shrines to the wongai-tree, which gives them
the rich red plums. A few years back each
island altar was jealously cared for and had
its seasonal ceremonies, but the younger
generation is discarding many of the time-
honored beliefs. The shrine was generally
within the centre of a grove, arid the
trees themselves were venerated. It was
built of big white clam shells, intersected
by stones of red and black, symbolic of the
green and ripening fruit. Enclosing all was
a patterned fence of corals and handsome
trumpet shells. A picturesque approach of
the same kind' of shells capped on coral
blocks led to the inner sanctuary. Every-
thing, even to the decorative pebbles, had
a symbolic meaning, but the old men j are
chary of imparting information as to their
beliefs. Trumpet shells bedecked almost all
the tribal shrines, but their significance is
not fully understood by us. Within the
wongai shrine at the fruitful season, foretold
by the stars, the zogo-man entreated the
Spirit of Life to flush the trees with bounti-
ful crops.
“Sea Nomad”: York Island is a strip of
sand resting upon a huge coral reef. On it
grow many eoeoanut-trees, and it holds a
grass-grown village of sturdy natives. It also
harbors centipedes of large size and of great
variety in color. A particularly nasty sort
clings to the grass-blades by night and
hitches to the natives’ legs as they brush
by. Its bite is exceedingly painful. An
old lady there is known as “the centipede
woman.” She pins down a big crawler with
a stick and (using her finger-nails, appa-
rently) nicks something from its jaws, or
possibly touches it with some stupefying
juice. Then she lets the loathsome thing
crawl all over her body, but never receives
a bite. On a speck of pure sand just off
Coeoanut Island, a swarm of centipedes will
come from apparently nowhere to eat the
crumbs from your meal. flow they got
into such inaccessible spots, far out to sea,
is a mystery. The only place I have seen
them in equal numbers is at Rum Creek,
in the Territory. One night after a thunder-
storm they, swarmed over the verandah of
our house,' and in the moonlight we killed
.‘>o within 10 minutes. Then they disap-
peared as suddenly as they had come.

THE SOIL ANALYSIS FALLACY.
“Chemico”: The farmer who thinks that
any chemist, no matter how well qualified,
can take a spadeful of his soil into a labora-
tory and give an infallible recipe for enrich-
ing it has too much faith in science. Pro-
ductivity depends on soil texture, quantity
and quality of colloidal matter, position of
water table, climatic conditions and previous
treatment, as well as upon chemical in-
gredients. If a soil doesn’t produce well, all
those factors have to be examined. Even
supposing the deficiency is a chemical matter
and clearly indicated in the analysis, there’s
a lot of room for error. Soil may cry for
superphosphate, for example, but what’s the
right proportion? No chemist can say
absolutely unless he has checked his results
by practical experiment. The average super,
dressing contains about 501 b. of phosphate
to the cwt. Sprinkle lewt. over an acre.
It's mixed with the top nine inches of soil,
which weighs about 2J million lb., and gives
a flavor of O.OOOS per cent. There isn’t any
method of chemical analysis that I’ve heard
of that could detect and estimate the dif-
ference between a soil in its natural condi-
tion and one so flavored. Yet it means a
lot to cropping fecundity.
At Lincoln Agricultural College (M.L.)
lately two soils from neighboring districts,
each apparently equal in phosphate content,
were treated with lewt. of super, to the
acre. In one ease there was an increase
of six bushels to the acre in the wheat crop.
In the other the crop was neither better
nor worse. A more striking case w ? as in the
North Island of Fernland. Two plots near
Auckland were analysed. One was found
to contain 0.009 per cent, of phosphoi’ie
acid; the other only 0.002 per cent. Both
were reckoned deficient. But when the plots
were treated with super, the richer soil of the
two responded with an increased yield of
half a ton of hay to the acre, whereas the
poorer soil gave no better result than before.
The moral is that field experiments are
essential, and that it’s better to establish
a hundred or a thousand little experimental
farms in various districts than to centralise
the business.
WHEAT-SOWING IN THE MALLEE.
“Glenfield”: When the land was new, in
the early days of the n.-w. Mallee (Vic.),
light sowing was the general rule, as low as
301 b. of seed to the acre giving a 12- to
15-bushel crop of wheat in a fair year. The
same paddocks were often sown three and
four years in succession. Then the weeds
asserted themselves, and scientific fallow-
ing had to be practised to grow good crops.
With the constant working the land became
consolidated, and capable of carrying a
greater body of grow T th. To-day we can sow
a bushel to the acre, increasing to 701 b.
as the end of the sowing period approaches,
with a possible yield of 24 to 30 bushels.
The new settler sowing new land after
a good timber burn should not sow heavier
than 451 b. to the acre. As the crop grows
rank if sown heavier there is not sufficient
moisture in the soil to feed the growth
when the hot north winds set in, with the
result that the crop wilts, dries off and
becomes a failure, while the lighter, sowing
would have produced a good average crop.
THE SPIKED ROLLER.
“Paul C.”: With the exception of sandy
or very friable soils, most farm land, when
dry, has a tendency to run together like
cement, and turn up in great lumps that
defy all efforts of the harrow or ordinary
roller to break up thoroughly. Air is for
the most part excluded, and light rains only
moisten the outside of the lumps, leaving
hard, dry masses within, that only heavy
soaking rain will melt.
To deal with ground in this condition, no-
thing is better than a spiked roller. The
farmer who can’t afford a specially-con-
structed iron or concrete implement can
make one. Here goes.
An ironbark log (red-gum or other tough,
heavy timber will do), a foot in diameter
when finished and six feet long, is adzed
free from sap. Starting three inches from
the end, a row of spikes made from harrow-
tines are driven three inches into holes bored
along the roller and spaced six inches apart,
leaving the snikes three inches long. Five
other rows six inches from each other are
similarly inserted round the roller, so that
every alternate row fills the spaces between
the spikes in the row before it; the shorter
rows having 31 spikes and the longer ones
13.
An oblong frame, six and a half feet long,
by three feet wide, to which a pole for a
pair of horses and a seat for the driver are
attached, is mounted on a pair of angle
irons, in the elbows of which the gudgeons
or axles of the roller revolve. Under the
driver’s seat (the seat of an old mowing-
machine will do) is a box the length of the
roller, and a foot wide by a foot deep.
This is used for ballast if the ground is
extra-rough.
When fully loaded this spiked roller will
burst and pulverise the roughest ground, and
a couple of liarrowings and rollings will
reduce a farm that resembled a rubble
quarry to a decent tiltli.
BARLEY AS SHEEP FEED.
“Tritici”: I’ve pro\ed that barley is
cheaper than wheat or oats to feed to sheep.
For some months I have been holding 300
wethers on a small farm. Dividing the mob
into three of 100 each, I fed to No. 1 wheat,
at a cost of £6O; to No. 2 oats, at a cost of
£4B; and to No. 3 Cape barley, at a cost of
£4O for three months; in addition to what
natural feed they could get, on three equal
areas of identical country. I have been
offered exactly the same price by auction
for each lot, and would have been £2B in
pocket had I fed the lot on barley.
THE DRAFT ON WOOL.
“Trepex”: “675’5” kick about the allow-
ance to the wool-buyer of 11b. in every hun-
dredweight which he purchases (B.
14/7/’2T) may seem absurd when we con-
sider the perfect weighing appliances avail-
able in these days, but, after all, have we
got such a lot to complain about? We sell
our wool on excellent terms —cash within 14
days and no discount asked. How many
commodities are cleared under such favor-
able conditions? Regarding the draft as a
discount, it is under one per cent., which
isn’t much to pay for cash in the bank.
There is another aspect of the matter.
Wool has to be bought on judgment, and
human judgment in such matters is never
infallible. But the principal expects omni-
science in the buyer or he loses his job. Is
it any wonder that he asks for a small safety
margin? Woolgrowers, in buying sheep, de-
mand a better margin themselves.
If you take the traditional margin from
the buyer lie will have to fix one for him-
self, and it isn’t likely he’ll do himself any
injustice or take any long chances. He would
retaliate in a manner that might be more
costly to us, so let’s leave well alone.
THE AUSTRALIAN KELPIE.
“R. Mort” : The collie will always be as-
sured of a certain number of adherents, but
for good all-round sheep-work give me the
kelpie. Weeks on the road under the most
trying conditions leave him tired but game,
and after a few days’ rest he will be as
fresh as paint. The spirit of the collie,
poor beast, may be willing, but the flesh is
weak. Even when spelled frequently he gets
badly punished by the heat and flies, and
his feet are invariably knocked about.
When buying a kelpie or any other sheep
dog, it is best to see that the pup comes
of an inbred strain, the more inbred the
better, provided the job has been done with
an eye to perpetuating points and eliminating
defects. The animal should be fairly chesty,
with well-sprung ribs, a broad head, sharp
muzzle and good hard feet. Twice a day
is often enough to feed him and the morn-
ing meal should be on the light side. At
night, after the day’s work is over, his
ration can be heavier. If he is fed on raw
and cooked meat he will be wiry and not put
on more condition than is sufficient to keep
bis coat sleek and healthy-looking. Make
a point of never throwing him scraps on
the midday dinner camp.
The dog is the host of the tape-worm dur-
ing the latter part of its life. To prevent
him passing on the eggs to the sheep, give
him a purgative every month at least. A
dose of one tablespoonful of castor oil with
12 drops of turpentine will quickly destroy
worms or eggs.
The ideal kelpie is a wide and silent
worker, whose obedience is almost as much
a matter of intelligence as training, and
who can be trusted to carry on for a couple
of hours out of sight as well as he does when
under his owner’s eye.
ISLANDS FOR SHEEP-RAISING.
“N.M.L.” : Along tlie Queensland coast are
a number of islands almost ideal for sheep-
raising. They have their disadvantages, the
greatest being the big rainfall. It is not a
disadvantage to sheep accustomed to it, but
western-bred sheep take time to get used
to the new conditions. Once they have gone
the round of the seasons they are set. Among
many advantages are low rental, permanent
springs, no dogs or rabbits, freedom from
noxious weeds, secure boundary fencing and
economy of working. Here, among the group
of sheep islands in the Whitsunday Pas-
sage, goods are delivered at the front door —
or the home bay, which is much the same
thing—freight from Brisbane being 445.
a ton. The same steamer will call in and
pick up the wool and deliver it in Brisbane
at 20s. a bale. The rainfall is somewhere
about 80in. a year, the climate equable and
running between 65 and 85 degrees. The Pas-
sage is sheltered.
Sheep with a dash of crossbred in them
grow to immense weight and mature early;
they gather a bit of seed in the wool, but
wool should be a secondary consideration in
this locality with all the big towns in North
Queensland within easy touch and waiting
for fat lambs. Horses do well, and on
Lindeman Island may be seen half a dozen
of the finest-conditioned bullocks that over
tempted a cattle buyer. All tropical fruits
grow in profusion and vegetables are easy
to rear—here in our garden we have at
this present moment 11 different varieties
of vegetables, ranging from peas and beans
to pumpkins and cabbages, and this in the
middle of July.
To a western man the great drawback
is the rain—he can’t accustom himself to
this 80in. touch. But there’s not a scrap of
bog on all these islands. As soon as tlv
rain has cleared the ground is dry, and
boot-scrapers at the front doors are un-
known.
POISONING OF STOCK.
“Azac”: if.S.W. Chief Government Vet.
says: “Complaints are being received of
poisoning of stock by baits laid for ’possums
and rabbits. The whole question of poisoning
wild animals at present is in a very unsatis-
factory condition. In parts of the State
poisoning for any purpose might well be
prohibited.” Why this outcry against poison-
ing? As a means of ridding the land of
rabbits it has been employed for donkeys’
years. There needn’t be any risk to large
stock. Mixed with care and in just sufficient
quantity for a fatal dose for bunny, there is
absolutely no danger from the phosphorus
poisons, at any rate; and all kinds of poisons
have been extensively used for a long while,
and their use brought down to a fine art.
On the other hand, it is quite possible to
gather instances of stock being killed through
being caught in rabbit traps. I have seen
cases myself. I have also seen stock hung
up in rabbit-proof netting fences. Humans
have been killed by prussic acid. But
such points cannot be used as a logical
excuse for abolishing all these things and
letting bunny have a free run, in which
case he would eat out all the grasses and the
stock would then starve to death.
A point to be remembered is that the
poisoning of stock is always associated w T ith
the poisoning of ’nossums. This suggests
that rabbiters in poisoning for skins, osten-
sibly rabbit skins, make the bait “overproof,”
so that when it is eaten the ’possum, which
he is really after, will be sure not to get
too far away from the poison trail before
dying. The moral is, do the poisoning your-
self and keep the “professional” poisoner off
the premises.
THROWING A HORSE.
“N.M.L.”: Let’s throw a horse, using dif-
ferent methods and employing ropes.
The first means we’ll adopt is handy for
roping a foal for branding or other opera-
tions. Let the mare and foal in the round
yard together—the old woman being with her
baby helps to Lessen his fear and give him
assurance that all isn’t too bad. Let ’em
run round the yard a couple of times, the
foal keeping on the outside, and the man in
the middle of the yard may turn with them
as they swing round. After going round a
few times just to let them know that things
are right, and the foal being on the out-
side where he can’t see the rope coming, the
man throws the rope over the mare’s back
so as to fall round the foal’s neck. If it’s
a good throw the game starts straight away;
if it’s a bad one you start over again.
Let’s allow the cast has been good. Quick,
now, take the end of the head-rope through
the rails of the yard to men waiting out-
side. Pull, there —pull that foal towards the
rails! Now he’s near enough, unable to
reach the gate with the length of tether he’s
got, and you can let the old mare out —she’s
going mad in her excitement. Keep on pull-
ing that foal up there, and shut the gate on
the mare as soon as she’s out —we don’t want
the excited old thing to come charging back
on top of us. Here, you leg-rope men—when
the foal's been choking a while, you with the
hind leg-rope take liis tail in your hands and
pull him about till, in staggering, his off
liind-leg steps into the loop of your rope.
If you take his rudder before he’s choked a
bit, most likely he'll land you one, so be care-
ful. You on the front leg-rope, get in near
the foal’s shoulder, leaning your hip against
him, and trail your loop to trap the off fore-
foot. That’s it. Run your ropes through the
rails to men outside. Now, having them
pulled tight, both of you stand in close to
the foal. Slack the head-rope, there. The
foal bounds, both off legs being snatched from
under him by the restraining ropes, and lie
flops to the ground. Now, you men, quick
and lively. You of the fore leg-rope, drop on
his neck, lift his muzzle from the ground, put
a half-hitch round it if you wish with the
loose head-rope, and hang tight. You at his
flank, drop forward on the foal, resting on
your knees, draw his tail between liis hind
legs and hard against his body, pulling to-
wards his hip and pressed in his flank. Now
you’ve got him. If you wish you may put
on other ropes and ties; but for branding
that’s enough. Off head-rope, slack leg-ropes
and the foal staggers to its feet, whinnies for
its mother and trots out of the yard.
This chap’s a broken-in horse; at least, one
which is handled, and we want to drop him
carefully. Very well, let’s use the collar-
rope. (let your head-rope—that’s the long
and strong one—and double it. About two
to three feet from the loop of the double,
just tie an ordinary knot in the doubled rope.
Slip that loop over the horse’s head, both ends
hanging free, and make a start on that.
Take each rope back through between the
horse’s front legs, swing it round his hind
fetlocks —he may kick once or twice when lie
feels it; but that’s nothing—and then run the
end through the loop round his neck, one on
each side. Now, having a man on each side
pulling on the ropes, lead the horse forward.
Pull, there—pull good and steady without
jerks. That’s the way, and the horse sits
down more or less*gracefully. Catch hold of
his mane on the wither and roll him over on
his side —keep those hind leg-ropes tight !
and you’ve the horse on his back, his hind
legs trussed in the air, and he’s safe. But
just one word of warning: don’t try any sur-
gical operation without adding a loin-rope.
The horse may double his neck right in on
his chest and then that loop round liis neck
will slip over, the tension on his hind feet
is gone, and that horse rises with nothing on
him but dust. I know, having had the thing
happen when but half-way through the
operation.
Now we’ll put another fellow down, and.
this horse being a quiet old fool, we’ll let him
down as light as a feather pillow. He’s got
a bridle or a halter on him and we’ll tie up
his near front leg. That’s done by buckling
any strap round his forearm and shin, the
knee being doubled to allow a close tie. It’s
simple, ridiculously easy, and it's a perfect
tie. Having done that we’ll next use ordi-
nary hobbles to join his off fore-foot and his
near hind. Don’t hobble him too close —full
length chain will do. The old horse wonders
what the dickens is happening to him when
you tug at the reins to lead him forward;
but. being willing to oblige always, he essays
to follow. As easily as if he was standing
on a melting pat of butter that horse goes
down without a struggle and rolls over on
his side.
There you are, three simple throws, no
danger attached to any of them —so long as
you’re reasonably careful.
INSURING STUD SHEEP.
“Borer”: No traders stand in such need of
a better insurance cover as the buyers and
sellers of stud sheep. The brutes are very
liable to injury in being carried about; many
of them have died of blood poisoning before
now, owing to cuts and knocks sustained .ui
their travels. Of course, a ram can be in-
sured, but the premium is so high as to be
almost prohibitive. The trouble is that the
beast has, as his main and very intangible
asset, his capacity as a sire, and there is no
salvage on this in the case of accident. All
that is to be got is the skin, and that is not
a great deal more valuable than that from
any ordinary sheep.
SHEEP-WORRYING ALSATIANS.
“Bourri”: The sheepowner’s fear of the
killing proclivities of the Alsatian is based
more on performances than on pedigree, and
is on that account well founded. Recently
in England a flock of pure-bred Suffolks
were hunted by a pair of Alsatians. 10 being
pulled to ribbons and others killed. The
dogs were caught in the act and shot, and
the owner had to pay heavy compensation.
In another English case the owner of the
offending dog paid £SO to the flockmaster,
some of whose ewes were worth £2O apiece.
In both cases the Alsatians were great pets
with their owners. Of course, it may be
protested that very few of the larger breeds
of dog can show an entirely unblemished
reputation in this regard; but in the case
of the Alsatian there is a possibility of nip-
ping the damage in the bud. The purpose

EXTERMINATING BLOWFLIES.
“Borom” : Most of the so-called blowfly
traps of wire gauze are no good. While the
flies are in the trap they multiply freely,
and the young maggots make their escape
through the meshes. Good results have been
obtained with large shallow pans tilled with
a fairly strong solution of potassium cyanide,
baits of meat being placed around the edges
of the pans. Most of the flies attracted fall
into the solution and expire. But, of course,
cyanide is a risky thing to have lying about.
WE NEED BETTER STOCK
STATISTICS.
“Hoki Mut”: Estimates of drought losses
of sheep usually omit account of the deaths
from natural causes. Sheep-masters com-
pute this at from three to four per cent, per
annum, which would account for a loss (not
considering the question of natural increase
for the moment) of perhaps two million
sheep in -N. S. Wales. This year, owing to a
dx\y autumn and winter, such a reduction
will not be counterbalanced by the usual
lambing, and to these two factors are due
most losses likely to be experienced.
Much more reliable stock statistics are
needed. At present three separate authorities
call on the N.S.W. pastoralist for returns,
the Federal and State Statisticians and the
P. P. Boards; and it is much the same right
over Australia. All three use different
methods of compilation and two have dif-
ferent balancing dates. The forms used pro-
vide no check on past returns, and the trouble
and confusion involved lead the sheep-owner
to supply approximate tallies only. The issue
of a uniform type of stock return, similar in
requirements to that used by the Taxation
Commissioner and covering a uniform period,
is urgently needed. In such a return the
debits asked for would be stock on hand as
at the previous balance, purchases and natu-
ral increase. The credits would be rations,
sales, losses and number on hand as at the
currrent balance. The computation of the
figures in this manner would provide a con-
stant check, and the result would discourage
wild scares and encourage the use of sensible
measures to check losses.
WHY NOT BREED BUNNY?
“E.S.”: A Victorian settler has asked per-
mission to enclose an area in Gippsland for
the purpose of breeding rabbits. He calcu-
lates that as skins bring a bob apiece and
carcases nearly as much, there is more money
in bunny-breeding than in grazing or dairy-
ing. Why shouldn’t he? The wild rabbit
insists on staying, anyhow, on large holdings.
In closely-settled parts it is easily dealt with,
and an odd one that might escape from a
breeding estate in such a district would have
no chance of multiplying. The rabbit is as
capable of being improved as the merino
sheep proved to be, and careful breeding
might do a lot for both his fur and his
carcase.
DAY-OLD CHICKS.
“.Tack Point” : The day-old chick business
is more than a side-line to many Austra-
lian poultry-farmers, for the demand seems
always ahead of the supply and prices are
sufficiently good to show a handsome profit
on capital outlay. But there is such a thing
as killing the goose that lays the golden
eggs and the sooner we realise it the better
for the industry.
Australian prices for day-olds are almost
fifty per cent, too high. Here are some
average American prices: Leghorns, various
colors, $9 (£1 17s. 6c1.) per hundred; Wyau-
dottes, Orpingtons, R. I. Reds, Minorcas.
$l2-13 (£2 125.) ; .Jersey Black Giants and
Light Brahmas $lB (£3 155.). There are
substantial reductions for larger lots, 100 per
cent, live delivery is always guaranteed, and
generally there is a guarantee that the eggs
weigh 240 z. or over to the dozen and the
cockerels are bred from not less than 20oz.
families. Australian prices are generally £5
a hundred for Leghorns and £0 for Black
Orpingtons, whilst the less common breeds
bring mostly what the breeders care to ask.
The time has come when the production of
hatchable eggs should be one man’s job, and
the hatching of eggs and the distribution
of the chicks another’s. That is what makes
the Yankee chick so much cheaper than ours.
It takes a man all his time to look after
a thousand head, culling, mating, rearing of
young stock, etc., without bothering with
incubators four or five months of the year.
Even the usual number of chicks required
to replace half the laying flock each year can
be better hatched in the mammoth machines
of a hatchery than in the farmer’s small
and less reliable machines. The buyer of
chicks is amply protected under the Ameri-
can system, the hatcheries sending out ex-
perts to all their suppliers to see that pens
are correctly mated, no immature birds used
in the breeding pens, and all eggs up to
weight. And if the suppliers can get, say,
25 per cent, better price for their eggs for
about four months of the “glut” season
merely by correctly mating the stock (or
having the job done for them) and gather-
ing the eggs and sending them to the hatch-
eries, they are doing pretty well.
FRESH EGG SUPPLIES.
“Practical”: Eggs are much easier to
obtain in spring and summer than in the
colder seasons, yet many people find it more
difficult to obtain “really fresh” eggs in warm
weather. The only point to be considered,
however, is whether the eggs are fertile or
infertile. Eggs laid by hens not associated
with male birds, being infertile, keep good
longer and are much better at any time of
the year, but more so during the hot summer
months, when even if the temperature tops
100 in the shade it has very little effect on
the infertile egg. On the other hand, if
the weather is warm, almost every fertile
egg. if not stored in a cool place, will be
more or less unpalatable. The presence of
a male bird in a pen of hens has no in-
fluence on the numbers of eggs laid by these
hens. Ilis office is the fertilising of eggs
laid for hatching purposes during the breed-
ing season. All males should be removed
from the hens at other periods of the year,
thus assuring the highest keeping quality
in all eggs laid.
OIL SPRAYS IN THE ORCHARD.
“Boronia” : "D.R.” (B. 14/7/’27) is right
about the cutting of fruit-tree bark. But the use
of oil sprays only, to the exclusion of others,
is not advisable. Oil-sprays certainly stimu-
late the bark processes: but too much has a
softening effect and renders the bark some-
wnat porous. When this happens American
blight or woolly aphis is liable to get a good
grip on the tree and cause much trouble.
This fact was unearthed at Bridgetown
(W.A.), where it was discovered that those
growers who used oil-sprays only were con-
stantly engaged in a struggle to eradicate
woolly aphis, while it gave very little con-
cern to the man wno occasionally used a
lime-and-sulpliur spray.
GRAPE FRUIT.
“W.M.F.”: “Grape-fruit, local 6s. to 10s.
per bushel case; American, to 40s. per case.”
That is a market quotation of July 23 last.
It is not unusual. Why is there this out-
rageous disparity in prices? The writer
learned to eat grape-fruit in U.S.A. He
has continued the habit here and can state
quite definitely that the best Australian-
grown grape-fruit is equal to the choicest
American.
Grape-fruit varies a great dpal. Some of
it is delicious; some not worth eating.
Unlike other fruits it is not all a matter
of how it is grown ; it is a matter of the
varieties. Some Australian arc of the wrong
sort, but even they should be worth more
than 6s. a case to make into marmalade.
(We had grape-fruit marmalade for break-
fast this morning, made out of local fruit,
and very palatable.) The disparity isn’t a
matter of quality, either. It is merely pre-
judice, and ignorance. Many people believe
that “American grape-fruit” is the name of
all real grape-fruit. The Yankees are cute
at that kind of thing. They have dubbed
the navel orange the “Washington navel.”
though it was known before a brick was laid
in Washington. Quite a number of citrus-
growers now have a few grape-fruit trees.
They are rapid growers and frequently free
bearers. Any number of the choicest grape-
fruit can be produced in Australia, and if
people will drop their prejudice and give
the local producers a chance they will find
that they will soon be able to get all
the grape-fruit they want, at a much lower
price than they are now paying the foreigner.
HINTS FOR THE FARM.
“J.G.”: The best handle for a kerosene
or petrol tin is a piece of hoop-iron riveted
on with pieces of galvanised wire about half
an inch long, and No. 8 or No. 6 gauge. Be-
fore riveting take a pair of pliers and bend
about a quarter-inch of the edge of the
hoop-iron upwards in the centre of the
handle, to give an easier grip. The tin is a
rule-of-thumb measure for four gallons of
liquid, |cwt. potatoes, -J-case of apples, one-
fifth of a bag of spuds, or a bushel of pollard
or bran.
“Yufu”: Better than kerosene for mam-
mitis and calloused udders (B. 28/7/’27) is
turpentine. Rub warm turps well into the
udder morning and night, and milk what
you can from the teats. Even the worst of
cases will respond in time. Have persisted
on one calloused section of an udder for over
six weeks, and cured it.
“E.W.”: The best way to preserve fowl
eggs is with waterglas.s (sodium silicate)
one part to 10 parts of water. Only clean-
shelled eggs should be used and as soon as
gathered from the nests. Eggs for treatment
should not be washed. When the last layer
is put down, have at least Sin. covering of
waterglass to allow for evaporation. Un-
fertilised eggs are superior for preserving—
the removal of the male bird from the pens
a few weeks before the work is taken up is
all that is necessary. When duck eggs are to
be “put down,” choose the green-colored eggs
—they keep longer than the white. But on
no account mix duck and fowl eggs in the
same tin of waterglass.
“Carisbrook, Jun.”: In an orchard near
Cooktown (Q.), when the trees began to
die and drop their foliage, it was found that
the roots were alive with white ants.
Between each tree —they were 10ft. apart—
a small hole was dug and half a plug of
dynamite was inserted and blown off. The
shock and fumes killed every white ant in
the vicinity of the trees, and they quickly
began to pick up again, until they were per
fectly healthy.
“BuH’s-meat”: I’ve been amongst cattle
all my life, and when I want to operate on
a bull I just put his nibs in the crush (a
pretty strong affair) and push him well
forward. In cases where a bull is very wild
and powerful I tie his head by the horns,
back to a rail, with a strong rail jambed
in behind him and two of the crush posts.
Then I get in behind with the knife. The
operator need not be a bit afraid of getting
kicked.
“Jack Point”: Probably 50 per cent, of
chick losses are caused directly or indirectly
by feeding the chicks too soon. When a
chick emerges from the shell it has enough
yolk in its little tummy to sustain life for up
to five days, and to feed it before the elapse
of 72 hours is to court disaster. And then
the first feed should be a little finely-ground
charcoal and a drink of water. This cleans
out the' last of the yolk. The first two days
thereafter the food should be given dry—pre-
ferably rolled oats. Even when the chicks
are started on wet mash it should never be
the least bit sloppy, but mixed to a crumbly
consistency.
“Tom Enry”: “Jacques’s” theory that
one or both of female twins will not
breed (B. 14/7/’27) is bunkum. Take goats.
Twins are the rule, but whoever saw a
nanny that wouldn’t breed? When I was a
boy we had a pedigree cow. a prize-taker.
She had three lots of female twins in four
years. The two first sets were sold as
vealers because of the superstition, but with
the third lot dad chanced it. Both bred
and both were splendid milkers and prize-
takers—-one, first and champion in her class
at Melbourne Show.

Our fathers came of roving stock that could not fixed abide:
And we have followed field and flock since e’re we learnt to ride;
By miner’s camp and shearing shed, in land of heat and drought
We followed where our fortunes led, with fortune always on ahead
And always farther out .
“BANJO* PATERSON.
But progress marches down the days, and out where white noons glow,
By farms afar and cattle-ways where still the rovers go,
The wide land knows a newer creed, and men trust less to luck:
They match their method to their need, as onward, with exultant speed,
Goes the great Reo Truck ,

MOURNING.
In sober black the vice-reines go,
But dance night-long on nimble toe.
From neck to knee vice-regal woe
For Ferdinand their dresses show.
But as they gaily have their fling
Their legs, ‘tis clear, are Carolling.
n.s.w. Flossie Fluffytop.
Never lend a friend money. It ruins his
memory.
Before marriage you kiss her a dozen
times; afterwards it’s touch and go.
The dressmaker is a moral reformer, too.
She has lately been all in favor of cutting
out women’s little excesses.
An American writer says that the day will
come when there will be very few marriages,
if any. Well, it will be a cold time for the
divorce lawyers.

space.

TIMES HAVE .CHANGED.
In olden days, so frail and wan.
Sweet Patience waited for her John.
And pondered, as the days wore on,
“I wonder where my beau lias gone!”
But nowadays they’re not so meek.
If Jack does not turn up each week.
They’d climb the mountain, swim the creek.
A-shouting, “Have you seen my sheik?”
N.s.vv. Doric.

She played to me in the twilight,
With the touch of a hand divine,
Fragments from Liszt and Chopin,
That go to the head like wine;
“She must have the form of an angel,”
I mused, from that wondrous tone ;
And when they switched on the electrics
I saw she was sixteen stone!

When a single man.
Now he’s married. Cattily
His female friends, who scan
His shabby clothes, say latterly,
"Isn’t he a guy !”
Willie dresses Natalie—,
She’s his wife—that’s why!
victoria. E. Turner.

CONVERSATIONAL COMPLEXITIES.
The maid of yore this problem set :
“When gentlemen and she were mot.
What kinds of things could be discussed
That neither side need feel nonplussed?'’
Then man with maiden, prude and prim.
Her niceties restraining him,
Unsullied themes alone* could touch,
And found he could not tell her much.
The maid to-day does not eschew
Those topics which were once taboo;
And questions then thought delicate
With man quite frankly she'll debate.
Though never banned is smoke-room jest,
Yet still for words man’s sorely pressed
lie knows, too well, maid’s wisdom’s such
That he to-day can't tell her much.
QUEENSLAND.
OMEGA.
THE KNIGHT OF BARRENJOEY.
A big moon had just floated up from
behind Barrenjoey as two young men
dragged their boat up on to the beach and
commenced to count the day's catch.
"1 thought I’d keep these cattish for Jim
Sylvester,” sai,d one of the men.
“Yes, they make good feed for his pigs.”
While this was going on Jim Sylvester
was making his way towards the fishermen.
He was a queer little figure; his short, tlun
frame was slightly stooped, and as he
stroked his wiry beard he kicked up tangled
seaweed which closely resembled the matted
hair that dangled from beneath his old grey
felt hat.
“Good-night, Jim!” called out one of the
fishermen. "You’ll find three or four catfish
in that lot.”
Putting the fish into his bag, the old man
sat down on the loose sand and laid well
back, with his elbow resting on it. The
fishermen could see he was in a reminiscent
mood, and to “get 'im goin’ ” they urged
him on with stories about shipwrecks and
‘‘booze-ups” and all the rest of the drama of
life connected with the sea.
“Well, boys,” he said, “per’aps I didn’t
oughter tell yer this one. But it won’t ’arm
nobody.
"It was a night like this when I first see
Freda Wise’eart. She was workin’ for the
cow cocky where I useter get skim milk for
me pigs. Every night I useter go up with
two pails and get ’em filled —it was Freda
used ter fill ’em. I didn’t know too much
about wimmen them times, but some’ow she
seemed different from the rest. When I left
with me milk everything seemed ter get in
me way, and if ever I reached ’ome with one
pail full me luck was in. One day I gets
bold an’ brings ’er up a fine buncher
flowers ; an’ when I give ’em to ’er she said
such things about me I lost me block abser-
lootly.
“Well, to make a long story short, that
same night she ’ad a row up at the farm and
after slappin’ the boss’s face she cleared out
to Sydney.”
“And didn’t yer see ’er again?” asked
one of the fishermen.
“I’m cornin’ ter that. I met ’er last week.
Whenever I goes inter Sydney I always ’ave
a porter-gaff at the Starboard Light, and
last We’n’sday when I was puttin’ over the
money—you could ’ave struck me dumb with
a bristle —who d’yer think was serving me
but Freda Wise’eart! ’Adn’t altered a bit
—though, per’aps, she looked more beautiful
with ’er luvverly golden ’air and cheeks, and
swell city chaps laughin’ and talkin’ with
'er. I bucked up me courage and asked ’er
if she knew me. She didn’t appear to re-
member me at first—l expect she was
only foolin’. But when I ’minded ’er of the
buncher flowers she threw ’er arms around
me and called to the city blokes: ‘Come,
HIT IT IN ONE.
“I suppose you’d like to see your wife back
again?”
“Yes, I would. She’s home.”
look, boys, ’ere is my long lost darlin’ Jim.
the pig-man from Barrenjoey !’ Just as full
of fun as over, she was !”
“Say, Jim, why didn’t yer ask ’er to
marry yer?” one of the fishermen inquired.
“Well, a man like me couldn’t do a thing
like that now with all them flash city blokes
after her. I should ’ave put the question to
s er .before she left for Sydney—let’s see: that
was thirty-one year ago.”
FRANK LYNCH.
