[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
RIRD of Paradise” Plumes, christened
William Gladstone, was known by
this highly ornamental title in bank-
ing circles because of the splendor and ex-
quisite taste of his dressing, in combination
with unusually fine physique. Physically,
Nature had set her hand to make a master-
piece. Plumes was tall and well built and
very handsome. He dressed with a daring
that would have been ridiculous in a man
less favored. But the unrepressed adornment
fitted this magnificent fellow just as the
painting of a Greek statue, if it was done
as well as the sculpture, must have added
to its beauty.
That he was merely the manager of a
branch of a bank in a suburb of Sydney
was one of Fate’s misfits. He should have
been general manager of all the banks in
Australia.
Of course Plumes was a brilliant social
figure. He danced divinely. He was the
beau of every ball. Shingled flapperdom
went on its knees to him and matrons im-
plored him to dance. His primrose path
was strewn with opportunities. It might
be. assumed that this darling of the gods
would be effeminate. Nothing of the kind,
lie emanated masculinity and vibjated with
virility. He might have been expected to
marry one of the enamored rich widows or
heiresses who offered themselves, more or
less openly, on the altar of his pulchritude.
But, in the middle of his golden shower of
popularity, he married a beautiful creature
without any fortune. In a sense, the mar-
riage was consistent. Beauty mated with
beauty.
The daughter of a moderately-paid civil
servant who had imprudently filled his quiver
with a dozen other daughters and sons,
Margaret Willis became the teacher of a
children’s dancing class in order to help out
a frightfully-strained family exchequer. As
became her avocation, she danced perfectly;
and it was after the excitement of a dance
and in the intoxication of her radiant person-
ality that Plumes suggested marriage to her.
In the heady atmosphere of the moment
Margaret said “Yes.”
There is reason to believe that what
is called “love” in the romances is one of
the rarest things in life. But beyond doubt
"Bird of Paradise” Plumes found his com-
plete mate when he took Margaret Willis
to wife.
Tony Starling was the accountant in the
hank, and a greater contrast to the resplen-
dent manager could hardly have been found.
He was. small and weedy, a mean-looking
specimen with stooped shoulders and large
nose and ears. Nature had dealt with him
in as niggardly a fashion as she had been
generous to Plumes. But although he had
none of the effervescent brilliancy of his
chief, Tony Starling had a mind. He was
a good accountant. Entering the service
of the bank when he was fifteen years old,
his twenty years of service should have made
him more than the accountant of a branch
bank, but preferment passed by just, as other
things did. In the records of the institution
his reputation stood high. Nevertheless,_ he
was never in the running for promotion.
He was overlooked. This lie accepted_ with-
out demur. Looking at his reflection in the
mirror he found the explanation.
“I am one of life’s losers.” he said to
himself, and went his way without bitterness
toward the more fortunate, but not without
discontent.
The chief cause of discontent with life
in this mistreated bank official was Margaret
Willis. She was his idol. For many years
he had adored her in pathetic fashion, with-
out hope of achievement. He never told his
love, but it had spread a light over the pages
of his ledgers and made music in his life.
Her loyal, generous friendship for the over-
looked Tony was the most gracious thing
that ever came his way.
Without hope for himself, he was not
uncontented while Margaret remained single.
When Plumes carried her off he suffered in-
tensely. But his just mind assured him
that the marriage was harmonious and en-
tirely fitting. So he abandoned the suburban
social functions where he was likely to meet
her and stayed in his bachelor quarters
nearly every night, reading from the stack
of books which he had accumulated. But
for his love of reading he might have taken
to drink.
In spite of all, Tony had the greatest
admiration for Plumes. Their intercourse
was by no means merely official. They
were friends.
Half a year after her marriage medical
advice suggested that Margaret should go
to the western plains for “a change of air,”
and she was packed off to the homestead
of a friend on the Macquarie. Before his
marriage Plumes had been accustomed to
spend his week-ends in the city—w There, Tony
did not know, nor did it intrigue him. He
knew T very well that a sensible bank man-
ager is prudent in not taking his recreations
in the particular suburb in which he func-
tions officially.
After Margaret’s departure Plumes re-
sumed his week-end programme. It was an
accident that led Tony Starling to the door
of a wine-shop in a mean street in Surry
Hills. Tony liked to prowl about the city
on occasions and he had a special liking
for the poorer, often the most undesirable,
quarters.
A girl reeled from the wine-shop door,
so drunk that only Tony's intervention saved
her from falling on the rough pavement. She
was young, still in the teens. What sug-
gested itself to Tony as the proper thing
to do was to take this slim young human
tragedy back into the temporary security
of the wine-sliop. Under the ferocious glare
of the Greek proprietor lie supported her
to a chair, into which she huddled. It was
near closing time and the half-dozen small
tables were occupied with patrons of both
sexes, mainly birds of prey, drinking wine
the parentage of which no self-respecting
grape would have acknowledged. Better
Avine was available, of course, to those who
knew, and bottles of this were on one or
two of the tables. Tony's eyes sought about
the unattractive company, all more or less
intoxicated, and rested on a couple seated
at one of the marble-topped tables. The
girl was young, and had been pretty; her
vocation was unmistakable. The man beside
her raised his eyes and looked at Tony,
who controlled himself only with a great
effort. Those brilliant eyes were unforget-
table, though their power was marred by
wine. They were the eyes of “Bird of Para-
dise” Plumes. With grey hair, his face
blotched, his clothes and boots shabby, his
shoulders stopped, a soiled old felt hat on
bis head, lie was unbelievably camouflaged,
and Tony would have thought himself the
victim of a delusion but for the eyes which
no art could change. Staring at Tony, they
yet gave no sign of recognition. So impassive
were those eyes that Tony speculated as to
whether Plumes was doped or in some kind
of trance.
After his first uncontrollable start Tony
suppressed all sign of recognition and went
shocked and forlorn. This sort of thing
lie had never expected. It was not the fall
from grace of the handsome and upright
banker, the conspicuous social figure, which
distressed him, surprising as it was. It was
the wrong to Margaret, and his heart sank
when he involuntarily compared her with
the woman in the wine-shop who had tem-
porarily replaced her.
Tony had read enough of psychology to
understand the curious reversionary pro-
cesses which sometimes manifest themselves
in human conduct —luciferian swoops from
stars to mud—but he had never dreamed
that “Bird of Paradise” Plumes was a fre-
quenter of the mud-bath. The fall of his
idol had a poignant personal interest for
him because it affected his ideal woman and
foreboded tragedy to her. He went home
crushed.
On Monday Plumes turned up as blithe
and vital as usual and with such apparent
unconsciousness of what had happened that
Tony was almost bluffed into a belief that
it was a dream, and that ho was living
through a chapter of unrealities. If not,
what sort of a being was Plumes, who could
act this double part and so serenely ignore
discovery? It was a tangle Tony could not
unravel.
With his thoughts far away from the
ledger before him, Tony pondered.
A few days ago there had been an in-
fraction of the rules of the bank. The
treasury was opened by two keys, one kept
by Tony and the other by Plumes. The
accountant and the manager opened the
treasury on the last day of each month and
together checked the cash there, which some-
times amounted to as much as thirty or
forty thousand pounds. The bank rules
enforced this practice. In addition, the
teller sometimes required to be reinforced
with cash from the treasury, and this was
obtained by the manager and accountant.
A few days previously, after the last count-
ing of the funds. Plumes had come hurriedly
to Tony, and asked for his key to get some
money for the teller. Without hesitation,
Tony gave him the key. This was against
the rules, but between Plumes and himself
there was absolute confidence, on his side,
certainly.
Yet, after the startling interlude in the
wine-shop, Tony wondered whether he had
done a prudent thing. Now lie knew that
he did not know Plumes, or, at least, knew
only half of him. There was a side which
had been concealed from him as completely
as the unseen side of the moon. What its
secrets were he could not fathom, but he
knew there were secrets, disgraceful cer-
tainly, and probably dangerous. The “Bird
cf Paradise” could not sink into the under-
world without having his feathers soiled and
draggled. For aught Tony knew, the man-
ager might be in the grip of some of those
vultures of the underworld by whom the
lucrative business of blackmailing is well
understood. In that case....
At the end of the month, when they opened
the treasury together, the cash was £3OOO
short. Tony looked at Plumes.
“I took it,” the manager told him, “but
I expect to put it back before the auditor
comes round.”
“Expect or hope?” Tony asked.
“It’s all the same,” said Plumes.
Tony looked at him again. “Do you ex-
pect me,” he asked, “to put my signature
to the statement that the treasury cash is
correct?”
Plumes put his hand on Tony's shoulder
affectionately. “One thing you will not do,
Tony,” he said. “You won’t go back on a
friend,”
“It’s asking a lot. old chap,” Tony said,
with a sigh. He signed the report.
“That’s all right,” said Plumes, cheerily.
“The cash will be O.lv. before the auditor
comes.”
“I wouldn’t lose too much time.” Tony
warned him. “These fellows drop in with-
out warning. I’m in this now as deeply
as you are. but there is another in it who
must be considered. There is Margaret.”
His face wms pale as he added, “For God’s
sake, remember Margaret!”
Tony's expression and words suddenly re-
vealed" to Plumes what lie had never guessed
before.
“Poor Tony,” he mused. "If Margaret s
baby is a boy I’ll call him Anthony. No
doubt lie’s game—dead game. Poor old
Tony!” .
The auditor generally made his inspection
once in twelve months, but not always. There
was a quality of unexpectedness introduced
occasionally. About nine months had passed
since his last visit, and the outlook was that
lie would not trouble the branch for another
three months.
As it happened, two days after the delicit
in the treasury had been revealed to Tony,
and as Plumes and Tony were about to leave
the bank, the auditor appeared.
It was in the closing hours of a hot,
January day with a scorching nor-westerly
wind and the auditor, who was extremely
fat, was also extremely tired.
“I’ll leave the inspection until the morn-
ing, Mr. Plumes,” he said. "Just now I
want a bath most of all, and a cold drink,
and something to eat. If you give me your
key I’ll see you when the bank opens to-
morrow.”
Tony glanced at Plumes. At the coming
of the auditor his face had grown white,
but at the announcement that his investiga-
tion would be postponed till the morning,
the color ebbed back. Strange, thought Tony,
that even one night's respite should have
restored the vast optimism of the manager,
whose doom seemed so near and sure that
only a miracle could avert it.
The auditor, having secured the key, ex-
cused himself for the evening by declaring
his intention to retire early.
The manager and the accountant walked
up the street together.
“How do Jve cross this bridge?” Tony
asked Plumes.
“Something may turn up,” the manager
said.
“If not?”
“Then there’s the bank’s automatic. I
want to save my name, for Margaret's sake.
If there’s no other way, then I must take
the only way. But I'll leave a letter ex-
plaining that you arc not responsible, Tony.
The bank is always decent and will not
expose me. I didn’t spend the money. I
was blackmailed. When I was made, Tony,
a kink was put in me. I didn’t put it
there. However it turns out, Tony, old
friend, blame the kink and not me. If tin l
bank is agreeable it will be made to look
like another instance of a fool handling a
firearm. It is all I can do now to save
Margaret and the child from shame. I can-
not save her from sorrow.”
“I can,” said Tony, quietly. “Go down
to the •bank as usual. You will find that
things will be quite all right. The mouse
will help the lion.” He turned away.
“But, Tony,” said Plumes, eagerly, “how?”
Tony regarded him with a friendly smile,
“Wait and see,” lie said, cryptically. Then
with a sudden impulsiveness he took his
friend’s hand and pressed it,
Tony ate no evening meal that day. There
was no time to waste. He wrote a letter to
the auditor, telling him he would find a
deficiency of a large amount in the bank’s
cash. He explained that he had sneaked
the manager’s key and helped himself, and
added that he had arranged his get-away so
carefully that the police would be unable
to find him “this side of sunset,” Then he
walked to the bank, opened the side door
and placed the letter on the manager’s desk.
Also, as he was taking no risks, lie left a
note on the counter addressed to Plumes,
telling him to hand the letter to the auditor
as soon as that gentleman arrived and before
lie should begin his inspection.
After that Tony walked to the railway-
station and caught the next train for Sydney.
There were several coasters leaving: that
night for ports north and south of Sydney.
“I suppose,” reflected Tony, in the train,
“I should hate Plumes. He took the woman
I loved. He has taken my job and my
reputation. But I could never have had the
woman and neither the job nor the reputa-
tion matters, as there is nobody who will
be hurt by their loss. I have only one thing
else to give.”
Jim Downs was going home after a scarlet
time in Sydney. The wool cheque tlie broker
gave him was the best for years, and at the
Easter races Jim had spotjted winners so
consistently that he had acquired a convic-
tion that punting was a better game than
any other for a white Australian to follow.
He went on to the steamer very drunk.
He was still very drunk at midnight, when
he sought out the captain and solemnly
assured that sceptical salt that he had seen
a man jump over the after-rail into the sea.
The captain said it was nonsense, and that
Jim was drunk. Jim candidly admitted
that he was drunk, but persisted that he
was correct in his narrative.
“The bloke just sang out, and jumped
over.” he said.
“What did he sing out?” asked the captain
roughly.
_ “He sang out ‘Margaret,’ ” replied Jim
Downs.
P. E. QUINN.
£1700 PRIZE STORY COMPETITION.
"The Bulletin” wants the Australian novel to take the place or
the, American and English productions which now flood the bookshops.
A school of short-story writers has been built up in Australia
through “The Bulletin’s” efforts during more than 45 years past.
Now "The Bulletin” is going to see whether a school of novelists
cannot also be built up.
To this end it offers a minimum of £l7OO in prizes for novels
during the next three years.
The first competition will close on June 30, 1928. For this the
prizes will be: First, £500; 2nd, £125; 3rd, £75.
All persons Australian-born are eligible; also all persons bona fide
resident in Australia.
The stories are to be available for serial publication in ‘ The
Bulletin” and “The Woman’s Mirror.” Otherwise, copyright will
belong wholly to the writer; all picture rights will likewise remain his
property.
Further particulars are given on the Red Page.

■ft • from pimples for the last
],'■.' '"w have tried various ointments, etc..
-11 Nil C (,n anything be done?
do 11 °.* sa y how old you are; and
A* V<ry lm Portant, for the pimples of
limnrrn ei -* S are n igh impossible to cure.
6 111 many cases they can be remark-
ably relieved. If you are in the ’teens, you
will grow out of them. If you have passed
that stage, probably your complaint is of
the common ‘‘blackhead” variety, in which
case a thorough bi-daily washing of the face
with hot water and much soap will loosen
the blackheads, after which they can be

the use of X-rays are worth trying. But
it is essential that one should see the patient
before prescribing such things.
(1) What is charged by the Fort Kembla
works for treating gold-ore {quartz)? (2)
is this the best plan? (3) What value need
a quartz reef 10 miles from the rail-head
be to be payable by trucking away? {We are
374 miles from Sydney). (4) What would
a small battery and concentrating plant cost,
and what value of reef 1 ft. wide could be
profitably worked by this? (5) Would a
2000-jyd. tank be sufficient? —1185.
(1) Kembla will charge you about £2 5s.
per ton for treatment and pay you for about
05 p.c. of the gold contents at £4 per oz.
(2) Unless you can rail and cart for 35s. a
ton to a battery treating gold-ore, your best
plan would be to send a trial parcel of 12
or 16 tons (to fill a truck) to Kembla and
get Frank Hambridge, 22 Bridge-street, Syd-
ney. to represent you at the weighing, samp-
ling and settlement. Hambridge makes a
nominal charge for that service. (3) If
you can cart the ore to the railway for
10s. per ton, then, allowing for cost of bags,
railage and smelting, you will require an ore
carrying at least ljoz. gold to pay its way
at the smelters. Anything over l|:oz. would
be available to pay cost of mining. (4)
Depends on quantity to be treated, but you
cannot erect a new plant to work effectively
at much under £5000. (5) Water supply
depends on your power plant and quantity
of ore you want to treat daily and on the
frequency with which your dam water can
be replenished.
(1) Difference between an insolvency and
an assignment? (2) What check is kept on
a, liquidator? —1092.
(1) An insolvent is one unable to pay his
debts. A bankrupt is a person, usually in-
solvent. whose property has passed into the
control of the Court, and who is personally
under the Court’s control until discharged.
AVhere a person assigns his estate, he does
not suffer any of the personal disadvantages
which may be imposed by the Court upon a
bankrupt ( e.g., penalties for extravagance,
etc., restrictions of his civil and legal rights).
(2) Depends entirely upon the provisions of
the deed. In some assignments and liquida-
tions accounts are filed at the Registrar-
General’s Department, where they may be
inspected.
Dow can I make a harness polish? —1219.
Two ounces mutton suet, 6oz. beeswax. 6oz.
powdered sugar-candy, 2oz. soft soap. loz.
indigo or lampblack. Dissolve the soap in
i-pint water, add the other ingredients; melt
and mix well. Add 1 gill turps. Bay it on
the harness with a sponge, and polish off
with a brush.
yw> {) Cm I m,, b'c a cheap asphalt path?
Take two parts very dry lime rubbish and
one part coal ashes, also very dry and both
sifted fine. In a dry place on a dry day
mix them,_ leaving a hole in the middle of
the heap; into this pour boiling-hot coal tar;
mix, and when as stiff as mortar put in Sin.
thick where the walk is to be. Sprinkle over
it coarse sand. When cold, pass a light
roller over it. If it is to bear heavy loads
it will need a broken-metal foundation; the
best job is made by first pouring hot tar over
the stones and thoroughly mixing.
Exactly how much can J earn, and my
wife and I still get the O.A. pension ? —1167.
Where husband and wife are in receipt of
the O.A.P., their pension is regarded as a
joint one. You would be allowed to earn
2;>s .per week provided your wife earned
nothing; or you might earn 25s. per week
between you. An unmarried pensioner is
allowed to earn 12s. 6d. p.w. before any
deduction is made.
What are the animals found, in Northern
Australia—alligators or crocodiles? —1158 4.
Crocodiles. In addition to a slight differ-
ence m the teeth, the alligator has a more
rounded head and is more sluggish.
Which is the best district in N.- S. Wales
for raobit-trapping at the present time? Is
the Darling River country still good? —1171.
Far Western skins are not valuable. The
best are secured in the Monaro, Blayney,
New England and Barraba districts.
(1) I am a boundary rider on a N.S.W.
grazing farm. What -wages am I entitled
to? {The employer is finding me in keep.)
(2) Is thei'e extra money for Sunday work?
—1143.
(1) Where the employer is a respondent
under the State award, boundary riders’
wages are £4 45., or £2 16s. and keep. Under
Federal award, £4 2s. or £2 14s. Bd. (2)
Boundary riders are required to work 52
hours per week, the hours being mutually
arranged. When necessary work has to be
done on a Sunday, the boundary rider can
be called upon to do same. Hours worked
in excess of 52 hours per week are to be
paid at an overtime rate of time and a half.

(1) What are chances of buffalo-shooting
in the Northern Territory, and what rifles
are generally used? (2) Would it be pos-
sible to a little more than clear exes, on the
trip—travelling from Brisbane and staying
in N.T. for six months? Has anyone written
a book about it? —904.
Years of shooting by professional buffalo-
hunters has thinned herds. Better to get
into touch with professional shooters like
G. Freer and H. F. Hardy at Darwin and
arrange to join their party. (Shooters
seldom engage the services of outsiders un-
less they are experienced, neither do they
encourage persons who merely want to join
up for sport sake.) To start out on your
own you would need two or three trained
shooting horses, a number of packhorses,
camp and equipment and abos. for skinning!
Also shooting grounds would have to hie
secured. Rifles most favored are the old
Martini-Henry carbine with bullet about
450 cal. and the 303. Service rifle. Shooting
is done by galloping up alongside the buffalo
on the near side and firing the rifle, one-
handed, into his flank, aiming to rake him
fore and aft. An adept on a good horse can
get several beasts from one herd before it
settles. Very dangerous work. 2261 buffalo
hides were exported from N.T. for the last
period for which figures are available. (2)
Consult A. E. Jolly & Co., Darwin, re ex-
penses. They vary greatly from time to time.
(3) Mrs. Aeneas Gunn in “We of the
Never Never” and the late Alfred Searcy
in his book on the N.T. give details.
What size electric generator would be
needed for lighting six rooms? Hoio many
revs, per minute must it travel? As T. in-
tend driving it with a water-wheel, what is
the lowest power it could be satisfactorily
driven with? Approx, cost? —ll76.
The most convenient size generator for
lighting six rooms would be one with an
output of 500 watts at 50 volts. Lamp for
an average room is 60 to 100 watts, and it
is not advisable to work on less than 50
volts owing to resistance of the wiring. A
generator of 360 watts might be sufficient,
but it is advisable to have one with a slightly
greater capacity. This would need appi-ox.
fh.p. to drive it (actually ,67h.p.). The
revs, depend on make of generator. The
slower the revs, the bigger the machine and
the greater the cost; a good average is 2000
r.p.m. Approximate cost of a generator this
size, £2O, including switchboard, etc.

(1) Who invented the gramophone and
ichen? (2) Who invented the cinematoqrauh
and ichen? —ll2o. 1
(1) Thomas Young (1807) recorded the
vibrations of sounds, and in 1876 Edison re-
produced sounds so recorded. His first
phonograph registered the vibrations on tin-
foil. Engravings on wax were introduced
by Tainter and Bell; and Emil Berliner con-
ceived the idea of engraving on a flat plate
instead of a cylinder. (2) The cinemato-
. graph is a development of the zoetrope or
“wheel of life,” described by W. G. Harner
in 1833. The modern affair was rendered
possible by the invention of the celluloid
roll film employed by Marey in 1890.
Information Supplied.
1051.—T0 get a surface on an earth floor,
level off, soak and then stamp well down.
Whilst still damp apply equal parts of cow-
dung and earth well mixed to a consistency
similar to that of mortar. Lay this on the
floor about two inches thick and allow- to
almost dry out before finally surfacing with
the same mixture. The latter application
should be “smeared” over rather than laid,
in a manner similar to that used by cement-
Avorkers Avlien floating their work. Inci-
dentally, if ox or other blood can be used
instead of water in the mixture a very
decent polish can afterwards be effected.
A further “smearing” from time to time will
strengthen and improve the floor, which, if
properly done, will set as hard as ivory.—
X.5.A.
981. —Here is a method which I have
proved very successful in stopping leaks in
a brick-and-cement tank. Procure a good
canvas. Cut same into strips Ift. wide.
Also some elastic carbon paint or bitumanie
paint of good brand. Thoroughly clean the
tank Avail. Then give it a coat of the paint
about 7in. wide each side of the crack. Also
give the canvas a coat of paint on one side.
Place the canvas over the crack; make sure
it has been thoroughly secured, leaving no
“bloAA’s” in it. Give it a coat of paint after
it is in position; allow it to dry and then,
as an extra precaution, give another coat of
paint. Of course if the crack is wide enough
to stop Avith cement this Avould be better.—
G.F.P.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Under the Whip
The 46th Australian Steeplechase was a
thrilling affair from start to finish. Most
punters neglected ltoisel because of his failure
in the Hurdle seven days before, but when
the grand old fellow passed the judge almost
his own length ahead of Clan Robert, after
a neck-and-neck struggle all the way down
the straight, they cheered and waved their
hats until the numbers went up for the next
race. The old fellow deserved his reception,
for no gamer bit of horseflesh has been seen
on the turf. Thirteen years old, and a
veteran of nine seasons, he has won the
Australian Hurdle thrice, the Australian
Steeple twice and been twice placed in the
Australian and the G.N. Hurdle. His burden
on Saturday (12st. 91b.) was the greatest he
ever carried to victory, and his 16 successes
have been worth £10,987 in prize-money, to
say nothing of wagers. R. T. Paterson will
indeed be an ungrateful man if he doesn't
now retire the wonderful old horse from the
dangerous game. He ought to remember
what happened to Haoma.
There were 12 starters, and naturally Nyan-
gay, on the strength of his runaway win in the
Moondah Steeple, was a hot favorite. Twos
were on offer at the start. Clan Robert being
at 6 to 1, Epergne and Derision at 8 to 1,
Roisel and Bellanius at tens and Mt. Cooper
at 15 to 1. Mt. Cooper was the first to show
up. and the Onkaparinga dead-heater was
still in the van at the back of the course;
but Nyangay was in charge at the sod wall,
which he jumped two lengths clear of the
South Aus. gelding; he had increased his
lead to six lengths passing the stand. Pass-
ing the half-mile post the second time the
Grand National winner was eight lengths in
front of Mt. Cooper, with Unolioo, Glentronie,
Epergne, Clan Robert, Perpetual, Derision
and Roisel following. He took the first of the
stand treble —where Unohoo, Glentronie and
Perpetual fell —cleverly enough, but at the
next fence he fell, leaving Mt. Cooper in
front. Racing up the hill, Clan Robert
caught the visitor, who dropped back beaten,
but Roisel challenged and the two top-
weights made the turn locked together. Clan
Robert came out a bit and interfered with
Roisel, but it piade no difference; getting
the big Sydneysider’s measure, the old
Blankney II. gelding drew away near the
post and won just on a length. Bel-
lanius, plodding on, was eight lengths away
third, Derision, Epergne and Mt. Cooper
being next. Roisel covered the “about 3J
miles” in 6min. 59|sec. Bill Murrell, the
jockey, and George Murrell, the trainer,
shared in the congratulations and deserved
them, for Roisel was a ball of condition and
was ridden with energy and judgment
Arcadius (6 to 4) looked to have a mort-
gage on the Arkaringa Hurdle, and it was
6 to 1 bar the Honorious gelding. As in
the previous Saturday’s race, he hit the
front early, looked a certainty, and spoilt
his chance at the last obstacle. He didn’t
fall this time, but lost ground by jumping
clumsily, and Nobility <6 to 1) beat him
by a half-length, Saltum (10 to .1) being a
fair third. Don Wraight, who rode Arca-
dius, got a consolation prize in the Kuows-
ley Steeple on West Wimmera (5 to 1),
whom he landed a winner after a great race
with a 25-to-l shot, Heath orton, West
Wimmera once beat Clan Kobert at Caul-
field.
Lucy Manette, favorite (at 3 to 1) for the
Chatswortli Plate, a mile race for three-year-
olds, was left at the post. Nereus (10 to
1), a nice cut of a gelding by Sea Prince —
Brakette, outstayed the others. Punters
went for a recovery on Duncan’s mount
Valwyne (7 to 2) in the Balaclava Stakes,
but the Yalais —Beanbri mare was pipped
on the post by St. Lazar (20 to 1),
a St. Anton gelding unthought of as
a stayer—he ran the 11 furlongs in
the good time of 2min. 20see.. A
few big backers got square on Phonic (9 to
4) in the St. Aubin Welter, which, despite
his lOst. 91b., he won very easily; but for
most bettors it was an unspeakably bad
day. Phonic is not in any of the big Spring
handicaps, which is a pity, for he is un-
questionably a good horse.
* * * *
With Davidson, the crack N.S.W. jockey,
to do the riding, Horton Gag has had a suc-
cessful trip to Brisbane. He just failed to
give Kitty Paddington 91b. in the Q.T.C.’s
National Plate, but won the Metropolitan
Handicap (carrying Bst. 121 b. and running
the 12 furlongs in 2min. 32!|sec.) and the
Denman Plate, for which Mountain Song, a
visitor to Sydney two years ago, was backed.
With only 7st. 101 b. in the A.J.C. Metropoli-
tan Handicap, this son of Ran gag—Horton
Queen is not to be despised. Laddie Blue,
also from Newcastle, won in 1921, after hav-
ing been toughened by winter racing.

* * * *
“Thormanby”: Something new to Aus-
tralian racing will be the King’s Cup, which,
the Governor-General announces, George V.
will present annually for six years. The
trophy will be worth £IOO, and each State
will have one in turn; dates and details have
been left to the November conference of Aus-
tralian racing authorities to fix. The race will
possibly be a w.f.a. affair, but it is more likely
to be a handicap. Charles 11. founded a
race for horses ridden by women which is
still run annually at Newmarket. Queen
Anne showered quite a largesse of Queen s
Plates on the English racing community of
her day. So George V. is reviving an ances-
tral custom.
* * * *
“C.D.”: When the favorite had weighed
in, Cracker turned to the goose he had in
tow. “I told you I’d put you on to a
winner,” he said gleefully. ‘‘Give us the
ticket and I’ll go and collect for you.” The
goose complied and Cracker departed. Ten
minutes sailed into eternity and the messen-
ger failed to come back. The goose wag
about to move away when a man who had
been standing near by accosted him. “What
was that chap I saw you with trying to do
to you?” he questioned. “I didn’t like the
look of him.” The goose explained, and
the stranger laughed. “Gone to collect the
money?” he gasped as soon as he could g_et
his breath; “why the man’s so crooked he
couldn’t lie straight in his bed. You won’t
see him any more.” “So I thought,” said
the goose, producing a betting-ticket. “That’s
why I gave him an old brief I picked up near
the scratching-board-”
* * * *
“Fitzcray”: Chairman L. K. S. Mackin-
non told the annual meeting of V.R.C. mem-
bers that the club’s profit for flip 1926-27
racing year was £81,920. Prize-money
(sweepstakes included) totalled £100,361;
since the club began in 1864 it has paid out
over £2,900,000. Support for the. Totalisator
Bill is promised by the V.R.C. if the book-
maker is allowed to carry on, as in Sydney,
Brisbane and Perth. But some very objec-
tionable features of N.S.W. tote legislation
will need to be guarded against in the Vic-
torian enactment, if a Victorian enactment
there is to be.

“Mechanic” : In spite of the excessive Gov-
ernment deduction of 9 p.c. and the asinine
method of reckoning dividends recently intro-
duced, £1,243 693 ss. went through the
machines at Randwick and Warwick Farm
last racing season. It is stated that the
Treasury officials are satisfied with the result
of the new dividend system. If they are,
they are in a class by themselves. Merry
Mint, 6 to 4 on in the ring, was 10 to 1 on
with the machine at Moorefield on Saturday.
Metallic (10 to 1) paid less than 6to 4. •
* * * *
“Ike Mcßagg” : The British betting taxa-
tion laws led to an interesting discovery
lately. Every registered bookmaker has to
furnish a detailed statement of his betting
transactions to the Inland Revenue Commis-
sioners. It was noticed that one ot the
punters was having an uncommonly good
trot, and inquiry showed that he was work-
ing in with a post-office man. The latter
held up his wires to the odds-layers till
results had come through, and then, hlling
in the winners’ names, sent them along carry-
ing the time stamp when the wares were
originally lodged. By British custom, a wire
stamped as being lodged at, say, 12.30 p.m.,
is good for any race not decided before that
time, no matter what delay there may be in
delivery. The same joke lias been worked
in Australia, but since radio has come m
odds are not available after advertised start-
ing time, and, in most instances, a fixed
period before that.
M.Q. (and Rafferty) Rules
Sydney Stadium patrons witnessed some
really sturdy punching when Billy Harms
(list. 41b.) encountered black Charlie Long
(list. 411b.) on Saturday night. The pair
waded into one another from the first round,
when Long tangoed across to meet his foe-
man almost in the latter’s corner, to the final
bell. Long’s charges and his two-hand wal-
loping were met by the Afrikander with
clean, hard blows to the body, buffets cal-
culated to damp any attacker's ardor. Harms
was too quick a thinker for his opponent;
the latter, as he too often does, spoilt an
otherwise fine performance by continual use
of the outlawed kidney-punch. Almost every
time they came to close quarters his left
would glide around Billy’s body and his right
start furiously trespassing on the forbidden
territory; referee Wallis cautioned him
twice and more than once intervened physic-
ally. Long complained that he had been hit
low in the second session; in the sixth he
landed low himself, but it was noticeable
that the white man made no claim, though
in his case the foul blow was seen by every-
body near the ring. In the final rounds the
pace was still fast and furious, Harms’s
hooks to the chin repeatedly bringing to view
every tooth in Long’s head. In the four-
teenth, after a couple of hot ones on the
head, Harms reeled, struck, missed and
pitched on the floor; and only sheer pluck
and ducking saved him over the few seconds
to the bell. It looked as if he might yet
find himself in a tight place as Long rushed
him to open the fifteenth; he pulled himself
together, fighting as nimbly and strenuously
as ever, and fully deserved the verdict he got.
* * * *
“Glen A.”: Les O’Donnell, coach and
trainer of rising Sydney pugs, had a unique
record in the ring. He fought in every class
from paperweight to heavyweight. Back in
the misty past he boxed the boy-wonder of
the period, Tommy Hanley; their combined
weight was under 14st. And he was a
“heavy,” like his uncle Steve, when he boxed
Dave ‘Smith. Steve O’Donnell, by the way,
was about the cleverest big man Australia
has produced, but stage-fright spoilt him for
the game.
* * * *
An interesting booklet, “Boxing and
How to Box,” of a size to fit easily in a
coat-pocket, comes from the pen of Will
Lawless, “Solar Plexus” of the Referee.
The amateur of stoush should find it an
invaluable addition to his stadium outfit.
What Lawless doesn’t know about the game
isn’t worth knowing.

“Mitt Shover”: .Tack Delaney, one of the
innumerable American candidates for the
heavyweight championship, got a step fur-
ther up the ladder when he won his match
against Paolino IJzcudun on a foul in the
seventh round. If the Spaniard is ever going
to do any good at the game he will have to
change his name to Mulligan or something
like that. With his original monnicker he
will have as much chance against the Demp-
seys and Delaneys from Eastern Europe and
Jerusalem as Don Quixote had in his mix-up
with the windmills. Delaney is now to fight
Sharkey in a preliminary to the Dempsey-
Tunney bout at Chicago. It seems a horrible
come-down for Sharkey, but the wages of
skite are usually paid in this kind.
*« - * * *
John Kilonis, vanquisher of Ted Thye.
gave a further exposition of his “all-in”
methods against Mike Yokel, of Wyoming,
at Melbourne on Saturday night. Early in
the bout game little referee Bridges, when
trying to separate the combatants, received
an open-handed slap on the cheek from the
furious Greek. Yokel, catching John un-
awares while the latter was remonstrating
with the referee, scored the only fall of the
night with a double leg hold and body press;
this was in the sixth round. Kilonis made
frantic efforts to get level, but the watch-
ful Mike was taking no chances. He raised
loud laughs by simply locking his arms
round the Greek’s mid-section from behind
and reclining on the mat —tactics which re-
duced Kilonis to a state of helpless fury.
Yokel applied the same stonewalling hold
in the last round. He was declared the
winner amidst a burst of cheering and a
final outburst of stupid petulance from
Kilonis, who walked to the referee’s corner
and wanted his. right arm raised in token
of victory.

BALLAD OF SPRING IN THE
SWINBURNIAN MANNER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Possibilities for the A.J.C. Spring meeting are
engaging earnest attention.”
The Johns of Spring are on winter’s traces,
Shifting the old bloke “under the Vag. ’
The coves with the keen and the deep-lined
faces .
'Throng to the corners, again, and mag;
And the lean, lorn punter is half elate
As he wonders how he will fare, this gate,
With his springtime tips, at the springtime
races—
While Ikey is buying a larger bag.
She comes with the glamor and prancing
of noddies
Spring, with her train of attendant rooks;
With a clamorous crying of ramps and
readies;
With biting of ears, and with chatting of
crooks;
While the sleek, suave Urger inveigles the
mug,
And hope gleams bright in the eye of the
Tug,
And jubilant every Gun and Head is,
And Ikey has ordered some extra books.
When the joy of her coming is past and over,
What will our harvest be, fat or lean ?
Will the birds of prey that about her hover
Every cent from our pockets glean?
Must we hoof it back from a barren course,
In black repentance and sharp remorse,
With barely a shirt our hides to cover,
While Ike rolls home in his limousine?
N.S.W.
J. B. H.

Footbrawl
(Continued Jrom page 39.)
Thus far ideal weather has favored the
Australian football “carnival” in Melbourne,
and the takings will pay expenses —neces-
sarily heavy—and leave a satisfactory bal-
ance.' There were 12,000 on the Melbourne
C.C. Ground, exclusive of deadheads, when
the Westralians downed the Croweaters. The
same was good in spots, S. Aus. staging a
fine recovery in the last quarter; Westralia
scored 12 goals 15 behinds to 9 goals 18
behinds. The Swan River men have a beauti-
ful system. They mark and pass with
machine-like precision, and they kick well.
Against S. Oss, and again on Saturday
against the Ma Staters, they set going a
drive from man to man with almost infallible
certainty that got goals as they were
required; how they will fare against the
hard-bitten Vies., used to countering such
tactics, is another matter. Craig is a star,
while Gukl, McDiarmid and Outridge are
quite first-class. The Croweaters are fast
and clever, but do not mark particularly
well, nor do they kick brilliantly.
The Cornstalks, beaten on Saturday by
W.A. —122 points to 103 —are improving, but
still have a lot to learn. They have but
little idea of concerted play, do not shepherd
one another and wander all over the ground.
Still, they have grasped the fact that they
must bog in to win. On Thursday they
put up such a dashing last quarter as to
overhaul the Tasmanians and win by three
points—83 to Tas.’s 80. R. Smith and
Justice shone out.
Saturday’s game, between Vic. and S.A.,
brought 37,210, plus a few . thousand dead-
heads, the offertory being £1918 12s. 9d.
The play, was fast and open throughout,
but, after an initial dash, the Croweaters
were palpably overmatched. A lesson to
the weaker State teams was the way in
which Don or Duncan would pass to Warne-
Smith or Geddes, and they on to Gordon,
Coventry or Moriarty; if the latter missed,
Baker was handy to pick up the ball and
slam it through. The machine was sparking
correctly and working exactly. The Crow-
eaters battled hard, pulled up a trifle, then
fell back again. They were beaten in the
air and in getting the ball in a crush, but
the effective system of the Vies., as with

“Hotham”; The “carnival” dates back to
1908, and has been held every three years
since then, save for the break from 1914-21,
due to the war. In the 1908 affair, held m
Melbourne, Maoriland was represented, as
well as all the Australian States. Victoria
won on that occasion ; it was also successful
in 1914 at Sydney and in 1924 at Hobart.
South Aus. triumphed at Adelaide in 1911
and Westralia at Perth in 1921.
“Macedon”: A piercing cry for help from
Canberra fell on the ear of the Australian
Football Council in Melbourne. The Can-
berra League implored the governing body to
permit it to affiliate with Victoria, instead
of N.S.W., which State, it pleaded, was not
strong enough to help the Australian game.
Canberra, the memorial proceeded, was over-
run with Rugby and Soccer, and unless the
Victorians rallied to the rescue it would be
given over altogether to those powers of
darkness. It was decided to give the Peti-
tioners separate membership of the Council,
but without voting power, which seems akin
to giving a Scotchman a whisky-bottle with
nothing in it.
“Rucker”: Another attempt to lessen the
number of players in an Australian-rules
team was made at the conference in Melbourne.
Queensland missed with a drastic suggestion
to cut the side down from 18 to 13 players.
The failure of the national game to become
solidly established in N. S. Wales and
Queensland was the subject of an interest-
ing debate. The greater popularity of
Rugby and Soccer in those States was
ascribed by some delegates to the visits of
international teams, which no doubt help to
increase public interest. But one speaker
struck the right note when lie said that the
establishment of a game in the schools was
the deciding factor. The Queensland dele-
gate mentioned that his league was now
concentrating on getting the Australian
game into the primary schools. A fair per-
centage of these in Sydney play Australian
rules, but they are confined to a sharply-
defined area, beyond the boundaries of which
the game does not spread. Time was when
the Australian code had it all its own way
in Queensland. Its practical extinction, first
by Rugby Union and later by Rugby League
and Soccer, is one of the curiosities of Aus-
tralian sport.
Sydney has never witnessed a more
brilliant exhibition of the Australian
game than that given by Carlton and
Geelong at the Cricket Ground on
Saturday, and 21,000 people were there.
Carlton won by 76 (11-10) to 60 (9-6).
It held a slight superiority all through, save
in the third quarter, when Geelong roUsed
the hopes of its supporters by settling down
to a better combination, which resulted in
two goals going up in quick succession.
Beasy, Clover and McSwain were Carlton
favorites with the thotoughly-worked-up
crowd; Hickey, Rankin and Bfagger filled
the same positions with the Geelong bar-
rackers, who seemed just as numerous and
quite as vociferous.

The Union games, with the Shute shield
allotted to University, are dimming in
interest. Saturday’s matches resulted in
Y.M.C.A., Manly and Randwick being left
in the semi-finals of the knock-OUt competi-
tion which will end the season; owing to
the programme arranged for the .Japanese
these will not begin till August 27. In the
G.P. Schools competition the powerful St.
Joseph’s team was defeated by King’s School,
25-16. “Joeys” were 16-9 at half-time,
but King’s determined attack and working
of the co-ordination between the line-out and
the backs, against which “Joeys” could not
provide, brought them home. This competi-
tion is attracting fine crowds and, with
three strong sides in St. Joseph’s, King’s and
Newington now level on points, has reached
an exciting stage.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Soccer world also witnessed
an expected reverse; Gladesville-Ryde was
conquered by Woonona, 2 goals to 1. With
Balgownie victorious over Corrimal, 2-0,
Gladesville-Ryde leads for the Metro.-Illa-
warra premiership by two points only ; Bal-
gow.nie and Wootiona are level for second
place.
“Feng Shoot”: The Chinese team, after a
beating at Maitland (N.S.W.), suddenly
decided to go home, although there were
seven more matches on its programme. The
team was financed by a Chinese syndicate;
the local associations, which took 10 per
cent, of the gate, were under no expense
save advertising. It was hawked all over
Australia regardless of expense, and no
wonder financial troubles brought the tour
to au abrupt finish.
The Nineteenth Hole
“Ploughman” : Much of the mumbo-jumbo
mystery in which golf has been draped by
its high priests is being dissipated by its
increasing popularity. Famil-
iarity has bred contempt, not
for the game, but for many of
its hidebound conventions'.
There is Jimison, for instance.
A famous lad on the green
himself—he has been known,
on his day, to hole 25 per cent,
of his putts—he recently
wasted a precious Saturday
afternoon teacliing his wild
how to putt correctly on their
tennis court, which is fitted
with a tin in the middle under
the net. Mrs. Jimison listened
patiently to precept and ex-
ample as to stance, grip, wrist
flexion and all the rest of it,
while Jimison kept up his
usual deadly holing-out aver-
age. Then the lady grabbed
a cleek, under the impression
it was a putter, and sank a
30ft. putt in a manner that
outraged every convention.
Continuing, she put them down
unerringly from all distances
and angles, till at last, in con-
tempt, she let go a backhand
knock that sent the ball into
the tin at 30 miles an hour
from the baseline. Jimison’s
face was ghastly. I had to
help him up the steps and
round the corner of the veran-
dah to where the whisky de-
canter stands on the table by
the ice-chest. It is a dreadful
thing to see a strong man weep.
# % * #
Adelaide golf lost a keen supporter of the
game when F. T. Harcus, secretary of the
Kooyonga club, died last week at 60. Widely
known and a general favorite, Harcus was
himself a capable golfer. In earlier days
he was keen on cricket, as a member of the
Norwood club, which has included in its
ranks many players famous in cricket history.
* * * *
The Victorian country golf championship
drew an entry of 70 to the Victoria club’s
links last week. A very high wind, which
increased to a gale in the afternoon, was all
against good golf, and the winner, L. II.
Thompson, of Mansfield, took 173 for the two
rounds of 18 holes each, with scores of 84
and 89. His wood and iron shots were first-
class, but he dropped a lot on the greens, as
did most of his adversaries.
* * * *
“Delia”: As to Toodles and the golf balls
(B. ll/8/’27), I saw an interesting item of
the same sort at Rusheutters Bay, Sydney,
one morning recently. A young girl was
practising brassy shots and had a nippy fox-
terrier to retrieve her drive. To anyone who
has done anything of the sort unattended,
the return of the ball without the wearying
trudge to the other end of the park was a
tip worth noting, and one that almost per-
suadeth the writer to buy a pup.
Racquet and Bawl
“Base-line”: Tilden has been coming back
to form in the U.S. Last week-end he
easily beat George Lott, one of the coming
Americans, at the Meadowbank Club's invi-
tation tourney and, with Hunter, carried oft
the doubles, beating White and Thaleimer
in three straight sets. In the States this is
taken as making the winning pair a cert,
as Davis Cup double representatives, what-
ever may be the case in the singles.
“Blink”: These lawn tennis tabbies arc
a long way from the also-ran class when it
comes to pushing into the personal para-
graphs. A lot of us were inclined to echo
Suzaune’s declaration that Wimbledon had
had its day when we read every morning
about sweet English maidenhood, as personi-
fied by Betty Nuthall, and sweet Spanish,
as exemplified in D’Alvarez, and so on. Now
the visit of the Englishwomen’s tennis team
t’o America has let the treacle go again. A
passionate cable in my favorite pony paper
tells m e that “before 10,000 gasping specta-
tors” little Betty, “the 16-year-old British
marvel, smothered Helen Jacobs, the 18-
year-old pride of California, who recently
conquered Mrs. Mallory.” I suppose the
poor 10,000 were gasping for a spot—and 1
don’t wonder.

“Cut Shot”: The result of the inter-State
tourney, N. S. Wales v. Queensland, at
Brisbane, was never in doubt. On the first
day N. S. Wales took 7 rubbers to 1:
Queensland’s solitary success was scored by
Moon and McGrath at the expense of Cum-
mings and McCausland, 6-1, 6-2. On the
second day the story was continued. Moon
strove gallantly against Crawford, who beat
him 6-4, 6-4; and, partnered with McGrath
again, the Queenslander was triumphant over
Kalms and Willard. In all the other tussles
the Ma State was victorious.

“Trembler”: The first motorists’ “inter-

next. Every question interesting car-drivers
will be debated, and the attendance of dele-

expected, the main objective of the conference
being the furtherance of road transport and
all that appertains thereto. In addition to

interests do not seem to be particularly
prominent in the published forecast of the
conference’s programme is the pedestrian;
but, then, one cannot have everything.

“Jeremiah”: The latest American oil
figures indicate that, after all, the world
isn’t in for an oil famine just yet. Reserves
are now increasing in America at the rate
of ten million gallons per day, following
on the record production of 1926. Already
many plants have been closed down owing
to a congested market, and the poor old
Standard Oil Cos. has been complaining that,
if the present degree of over-production lasts,
it will bring disaster to the industry—which
probably means that distributors will have
to cut their profits a bit. Another factor in the
over-accumulation of petrol stocks is stated
to be the increasing efficiency of the modern
engine in improving the mileage per gallon,
an advertisement for which car-makers will,
no doubt, be grateful. “High prices for
petrol.” the report concludes, “are not likely
to occur in the near future,” which is satis-
factory, or will be when Australian distribu-
tors have heard the glad tidings. A lot of
’em are on the deaf side.

Car and Cycle
(Continued from page 41)
“Whiz”; Yes, they like their speedway
stuff served hot in Queensland ( B. 28/7/’27).
A newspaper account of the opening of a new
track in Brisbane mentions that “the shifty
surface provides wonderfully spectacular
racing.” In one final a Sydney rider and
his cycle parted company, the machine
"careering towards the fence, which it
struck. Rising, it hurdled over the heads
of three rows of spectators on the edge of the
track, and fell in the open passage between
them and the stands. The safety fence so
far resisted the impact that only one small
piece of paling was broken off; this unfortu-
nately struck a boy. stunning him. Two
women were also . hurt, but it could not be
ascertained, whether they were struck by the
machine or injured in the stampede.” The
writer regretfully adds that the Sydney lad
did not attempt to ride again that evening.
Some people are so easily saisfied.
"Buck”: A somewhat unusual cycling
stunt entered on its Australian edition last
week, when Captains Malins and Oliver, who
are motor-cycling round the world, arrived
by steamer from Malaysia. The pair will
do Southern Australia, including Tasmania,
on their cycles, and then run through Maori-
land; Canada and the United States will be
taken on tbe way home. The trip began last
December from London, and the crossing
of Europe and Asia lias been completed with-
out any untoward experiences. Possibly, it
remains for some of our N.S.W. main roads
to give the tourists a surprise.
* * * *
“Quadrant” : One of the world’s big motor-
ing events will drag a lot of international
cracks to Italy in September, when the
Brand Prix of Italy will be contested on the
Monza track. The stunt is for cars under
1500-e.c., and the Americans are sending
over, a strong contingent of professional
drivers, including Frank Lockhart. France
and. of course, Italy have entered heavily,
but there is no mention of a British entry
in the cabled reports. Outside one or two
makers, British car-builders are turning more
and more to specialising in the general-pur-
pose car. and leaving the elaborate and
highly-individualised racing chassis to the
other fellow.
* * * *
“Chevroford” : There is no need to go to
the meteorological bureau for information
about Victorian weather; as good a record is
the figures of local sales of closed and open
cars. In February this year, according to one
dealer, they were respectively 89.2 and 10.8
p.c. By June the position was 33.3 and 66.7.
It just shows how few motor-owners look
beyond their noses.
* « * *
“Chevroford”: The most ingenious sug-
gestion yet made in the Safety First cam-
paign is that cars should be compulsorily
painted the colors of the rainbow. It: is a
well-known fact that: if these colors are moved
above a certain speed they resolve themselves
into yellow. Then policemen need only take
the number of the yellow peril.
“Ball Race”: In October Hubert Opper-
man will have a shy at Ted Pearson’s figures
for the. Sydney-to-Melbourne ride on a push-
bike. The distance, from point to point, is
365 miles, and Pearson’s actual riding time
in 1910 was 43 hours 18 minutes. He never
slept or rested on the journey—a wonderful
demonstration of pluck and physical endur-
ance. Road conditions, machines and organi-
sation are a lot better to-day than they
were in Pearson's, time, but old hands doubt
whether the young Victorian, good as lie
is, will better the achievement of Pearson,
one of the greatest long-distance cyclists of
all time.

“Masse” : As anticipated, the British Bil-
liards Control Council has banned Reece’s
pendulum cannon. Since Peall exploited the
spot stroke in the early ’eighties, the rules
have been continually varied to cope with
special developments arising from the ingenu-
ity of the professional showmen. The push
stroke, the, nursery cannon, the red-ball
horror and other stunts have kill been
squelched in turn, and as fast as this has
happened something else has arisen ro set
Lords of Billiards gibbering. The limit for
“pendulums” has been fixed at 35. The aver-
age player won’t worry; he couldn’t do 35
cannons to save his soul.
A stocky crowd with swift feet and a habit
of hard hitting are the Maoriland hockey
players who beat N.S.W. at Sydney Cricket
Ground No. 2 on Saturday by 4 goals to 2.
N.S.W. began the scoring with a goal by
Nettlefold from a corner, but the tourists
were 2-1 at half-time, Watts, the inside-left,
scoring both goals. Nettlefold again goaled
for N.S.W., after Edson had added a third
point for the All BlacLs. Showing fine com-
bination, the latter easily held their adver-
saries ; ’ Seecoinbe, the centre-half, hit the
final goal. The visitors, who come from all
parts of the Shivery Group, met each other
mostly for the first time on board the
steamer, and naturally they have gained in
combination since their first match.

“Stop Watch”: Whenever the average per-
son reads the name “Borg” in the sporting
cables he turns hastily to some other column
persuaded that swimmer Arne Borg has been
at it again. But the Borg who broke a
record last week (Arne smashed another a
day or two later) is a Finn, and did it on
the hoof; Eino Borg ran 2000 metres in
smin. 23 4-ssec._, beating Paavo Nurmi’s best
by just on 3sec. One wonders where this
continued reduction of times is going to stop.
It may be that some day someone will run
the hundred in nothing. After that we will
have to record times in terms of the minus
sign—close students of Einstein will, to be
sure, agree.
“Amateur Pro”: An asinine aspect of the
law of amateurism in sport was revealed
when the Australian swimming authorities
ruled that Dick Eve, the Olympic champion,
must remain a professional. Eve will thus
be unable to defend his title at Amsterdam.
Under the Australian laws Eve became a
pro. when lie was appointed manager of a
Sydney swimming-bath. The English rule is
that, provided he does not personally attend
on or teach swimmers for gain, an amateur
may be a bath proprietor or lessee without
infringing his status. The N.S.W. Swim-
ming Association asked for a vote in favor
of adopting the English rule. The result
was a tie, three States for and against. Since
a majority vote was necessary to alter the
old rule, the fool thing must stand.

“Leander” : The Australian Rowing Coun-
cil has received word from S. A. Middleton,
rowing and Rugby Union rep. of other days,
that the Olympic oarsmen will race on a
dead-water course on a freshwater lake,
some 20ft. deep, with no tide or current.
Unless Westralians decide otherwise, the
only rowing representative from this side
will be a sculler. Middleton, who lives in
London nowadays, says a sculler will have to
cut out the 2000 metres in 6-Jmin. to win.

‘’Don Grew”: The match arranged between
Goodsell and Barry recalls old Ned Trickett,
the first Australian to win the title now
held by Goodsell. Trickett was born in a
little stone cottage which still stands at
Greenwich Point, North Sydney. He was
a source of delight to us kiddies as he daily
strode past on his way to the jetty; the fact
that one of his hands was minus two fingers
in no way lessened our respect for him. He
“got religion,” gave up rowing, and joined the
Salvation Army. An accident ended his
strange career a few years ago; he was
crushed by a fall of earth in Uralla
(N.S.W.).
* * * *
“Whirroo”: One of the sports which blush
comparatively unseen in Sydney is hurling.
Several clubs play it, but only the initiated
ever hear of them or attend their games. Yet
every year Victoria and N.S.W. meet in
battle; this year’s turmoil will take place
in Sydney next Saturday. For the benefit
of the uninstructed it may be said that
hurling is played with a cricket-ball and a
wooden implement which resembles the
wooden battle-axe of the Gilbert Islanders—
minus the shark’s-teetli edging. There are no
puling rules, as in hockey, against lifting the
war club higher than the elbow, or hitting
back-hand or in the air. In hurling you hit
as you like and as hard as you like, and it
doesn’t seem to matter much whether you
connect with the ball or one of the other
side, though no points are scorable , for!
knocking an adversary through the goal.
* * * *
“Amateur Urger”: People who earn a
more or less honest living by “sport promo-
tion” are in for a shock if Denes von
Mahaly can establish his claims for a recent
invention. Denes, who sounds like an
Irisher but is a Hungarian, says his gadget
can make everything broadcast actually
visible on a screen, with the accompanying
noises going on as usual. Equipped with
Dinny’s discovery people can stay at home
and not only see such things as fights and
football matches —the same thing in Sydney
—but “hear the incidental words and
sounds.”. No need to stand all day in cold
and wet to be edified by the crowd’s criti-
cisms of an “improved” performer who has
just beaten a hot favorite at Robbo Park,
or of Billo, the forward, who has sunk liis-
teeth in Pongo’s ear. One can have it all
at one’s own fireside, with the kid sent out
of the room and the parrot’s cage covered up.
Only one thing seems lacking, and that is
an attachment to allow' of getting a bit on
with a shadow bagman who can be trusted
to pay in real cash. If Dinny’s genius
proves equal to this a lot of the pony nobility
and the like will have to get, work.
Business, Robbery, etc.

"ILK.”: On Aug. 10, 1857, Melbourne's
streets were first lit with gas. The coal was
imported from Scotland, the gasometer being
located at Batman's Swamp. Twenty-two
and six per 1000 ft. was charged for the
new illuminant at first.
"N.F.” : The Armstrong-Whitworth people
recently launched their 144th oil-tanker, the
twin-screw motor-steamer El Aleto. The big
British co. pioneered this class of vessel; in
1884 it constructed the first boat to cross the
Atlantic with oil in bulk. The El Aleto is
designed to carry about 10,000 tons of it.
"Sus" : A lot of people found out somehow
that the massacre of native-bears was to be
legalised in Queensland. For months past
trappers, have been storing, skins ,in expecta-
tion of the embargo on the butchery of Blue-
gum being removed. The result is that you
may ride for miles through country where
bears used to be plentiful without seeing one.
* * *
“Solo” : There’s nothing new about Russia
buying Australian wool. Before Ihe war
it bought a lot through London or Bradford.
A Russian buyer with headquarters in Syd-
ney has been operating for some months. In
March passports were issued to six others; 1
presume, that the man who arrived recently
and has been so much paragraphed is one of
them.
“F.S.” : Vic. Construction I M y., Ltd., has
landed the contract for the longest bit of rail-
way ever let to one firm in this country. It
is the Oodnadatta to Alice Springs exten-
sion, and two years is the time limit. Five
rivers and 80 creeks are the engineering diffi-
culties along- the mostly : flat expanse. Almost
all the. material except, the rails is growing
or lying on each side of the route. The
gibber of the region will supply the. ballast.
j'i ■ sjs * 9
“Count Overdue” : A machine that almost
thinks, and looks as if it might be capable
of vituperation if abused, lias been installed
by Melbourne City Council. It is an auto-
matic cashier, and the first day it was used
it shot small change into the pay envelopes
of 180 employees in eight minutes, whereas
the human distributor was wont to take
close on an hour. As likely as not the cash
disappears just as quickly, however. Most
of the men have automatic small-change
extractors at home.
* * * *
“Nikko” : Dairying is not the only Maori-
land industry which lias had cause to regret
Government control. The Government
appointed a Kauri Gum Board, piled up
stocks and waited for the rising market. But
when buyers—in this case the Americans —
found that the screw was being put on them
they got their wants supplied elsewhere. So
the Government found itself with stocks
valued at about £60.000 and no prospect of
selling. Traders know that you simply cannot
tell the' overseas customer what lie wants
and what he must pay; but politicians think
they know better.
* * * *
“Retired Volt” : People used to clamor for
the penny telegram. It, didn’t come to pass
because, however much telegraphy was
improved, the labor involved in converting
a message into the Morse code and trans-
lating it back into ordinary language cost
more than the humble copper, even when
executed by an inexpensive flapper seated at
an electrical typewriter. It looks now.
however,, as if the thing may come. A
super beam is reported capable of changing
a photograph' of a telegram into a stream
of electrons and projecting it in this form
round the world at a speed of 300 words a
minute. At the other end the electrons are
carefully collected and transmogrified back
into a print of the original photo. Users
of the P.M.G.’s department will then be
enabled to appreciate what operators have to
put up with.
“S.G.”: Despite the exertions of “Good
old Piddo” and his tribe, more and more
city-dwellers seem to be unable to live
within their incomes. The Sydney money-
lender who was found in his office with his
head bashed in had hundreds of civil-service
clients—and he wasn’t in a large way of
business. When the Manly Ferry Co. stopped
selling monthly and quarterly tickets it was
soberly stated that a large proportion of
the local population could not raise the price
of a half-yearly ticket (£2 10s. or there-
abouts), and arrangements were made with
a city firm to issue weekly tickets for a
consideration. And a man in the trade
assures me that half the young females
wear cash-order clothing. I suspect that
this country would be happier if it sacked
all the “Good old Piddos” and wrestled
with the problem of reducing the cost of
living.

In opening lier season on Thursday
Frances Alda trailed a black feathered dress
that billowed about the stage like the pluck-
in gs of several ostrich farms, and was girt
close to her hips by a deep swathe of silver
hieroglyphics picked out in rhinestones. The
frontispiece or prologue was knee high, and
the V back and front ran its jewelled way
to about the waistline. One girl remarked
to her neighbor that she had seen the dress
“off” previously. “But,” she added quickly,
“to-night I am not sure whether I’m seeing
it off or on.” Diamonds and a black feather
fan and pink legs and jewelled black shoes
were the accessories. On the stage stood
picturesque low trellises of roses, but a barri-
cade 10 feet high would have been needed
to temper the Town Hall’s icy blast to the
shorn lady, who plainly shivered—she had
emerged from a bed of ’flu to give the concert.
The voice is really beautiful, with a satin
finish. After the first group, a long proces-
sion of grim-visaged men bore in the floral
tributes with the air of those who are taking
up the white man’s burden. In the audience
sat the Hugh Ward couple, Hugh smiling
as he recalled that old-time pantomime in
which he, the wicked baron, was foiled by
Frances Alda, the fairy Queen. May Moore
displayed the peacock sheen of a painted
shawl ; Mrs. McKelvey, whose husband is
keeping a reproving eye on Alda’s tempera-
ture, snuggled up to the ears in a white fur
coat; and the man in the gallery who replaced
the "Bravo !” of the front-seaters with a din-
kum Australian “Good-O!” was another who
caught the wandering eye.
Doesn’t his Excellency de Chair ever get
thirsty? At the Criterion on Saturday night
Sir Dudley, as usual, sat the show out
without moving from his box. He is the
only Governor this Letter remembers who
has remained thus rooted to his party; and
probably managements have got out of the
way of reminding him that there is a room
with a spare bottle or so in it. Lord Forster,
when he was Governor-General, never missed
the half-time drink, and seemed glad to meet
other guests whilst he consumed it. One of
his predecessors could not be got back again
to view the show until he had gone behind
the curtain and been introduced to some
of the ladies.
Brother Masons, whether they built the
Pyramids or put the smile into the Sphinx,
boxed the compass the other night and made
for the Palais, at Moore Park. Lest they
might be mistaken by taxi-men for dark
revolutionists and dropped at the police-
station instead of their ball, the brethren
left swords and battle-axes at home, and
practically only the partners of the debs.,
who numbered about 50, were fussed up in
regalia. The State vice-regals came with
the twofold purpose of greeting the debs, and
enjoying themselves. They were ushered
within the red curtains by the officers of
the Grand Lodge, led by Most Worshipful
John Goulston and Right Worshipful
Brother (or is it Master?) Hungerford.
Lady do Chair wore Persian-red georgettes
rich with gold embossings, and banded her
hair with a gold wreath; her color vibrations
harmonised with those struck from the flame
and crystal in Mrs. F. S. Boyce’s raiment.
Other important people were Mrs. Marlow,
in gold lame, Mrs. J. T. Pattinson, who
mixed lilac and silver in her frocking, and
Mrs. S. Goulston, whose ivory georgettes
were coated in gold sequins. Cheery Major
Longfield Lloyd was in attendance on the
vice-regals, and had a rosily-georgetted wife
to bear him company. ’Kelso King, who
was patron of the dance, brought daughter
Olive; and Elaine de Chair eyed the gather-
ing over a collar of white fur. The Masonic
Benevolent Fund benefits bv the crowded
night.'
Rotating in the strenuous dances of their
youth, the older Rotarians gave the younger
guests at their ball last week an idea of how
fit a man had to be to face a night’s pre-war
programme. Somebody has even suggested
that it is the lack of exercise in modern
dancing that has produced the frenzied tennis
and golf of the past decade. .Anyway, one
schottische as danced last Friday night at
the Wentworth must have provided sufficient
liver-shaking to last three days. There wmre
lancers, too, and the .Tolly Miller romped
about plucking the partner nearest to his
hand. It was very cheery, and president W.
11. Ifould and vice-president A. C. Burch
in company with others oft wiped the beads
of enjoyment from their brows. Their ladies
were in better case. Mrs. Ifould cooled off
above her pretty powder-blue velvets with a
curling ostrich fan, while Mrs. Burch kept
the fires of her greeny-gold sequins in check
by a similar apparatus. Brig.-General and
Mrs. Bennett blazed a glittering trail among
the dancers wherever the gold of her raiment
flashed ; the Noel Heaths tried the floor, as
did liyingrmah Captain Percival. The F. W.
Kitchings entertained numerous friends. The
James Browns (Dunedin) and the Baggetts
( Wanganui), the. Aubrey Hallorans and the
Justly Rawlingses were others who made a
night of it. '
Mr. Swainson, Y. Emma’s new secretary
in Sydney, is an upstanding, presentable
Manchester man whose clothes are as easy
and an pronounced as his English accent —
two things that will help to popularity
among the boys. In the afternoon of their
arrival Mrs. Swainson came momentarily
into the picture at the party staged in the
Y.M.C.A. Hall. Here in an atmosphere
charged with words of welcome from the
padres of the city, music and songs and
flowers and palms tried to whisper to the
lady of the sunshine and the pleasant friend-
. liness that the new world holds in store for
all her unofficial hours.
Margaret Lawrence proved the shyest of
stars wheri Saturday’s audience called upon
her to repeat the bad habit of making a
speech from the stage; but, Louis Bennison
having taken her hands and dragged her for-
ward, she said in a rather breathless, half-
frightened way that she was glad to feel she
had given her audience something, because
what you have given remains your prized
possession for always. A nice sentiment,
which suggests that the lady has been study-
ing her author, whose “Road to Rome” sug-
gests that lie, for his part, has sat at the
feet of Mencken, his predecessor on the
American Life.
The Big Brothers lose a cobber by the
retirement of Commander Victor Cohen, V.D.,
through ill health ; but they have made him
an honorary life member in recognition of
two years’ unpaid work for the object of his
old-age enthusiasm. Cohen was the third
bridegroom to be married in the Great Syna-
gogue ; it was consecrated on March 4, 1878
—he was married there eight days later.
There is a chance that the old building
in Elizabeth-street, now dwarfed by neighbor-
ing piles, will shortly disappear. The trustees
have had the constitution of their trust
altered to enable them to sell, and that is
significant. The place, including land, cost
£27,000 in the old days; but values have
leapt since then.
Viva Blashki, daughter of one of the most
stalwart pillars or orthodox Judaism in
Sydney, was married on Saturday, the first
Saturday wedding ever held in the Sydney
Synagogue. The ceremony was a night affair
and, as the Sabbath ends at sundown, it
was quite within Judaic law; the custom
which bans Saturday evening weddings has
been adopted through fear that some of the
preparations would fall within the holy
day.

A pretty bride of the week was Coogee
girl Kathleen Smith, whose family hails from
Armidale. Her change of name was made
at St. Jude’s, where, with Padre Cakebread’s
help, she became Mrs. Ralph Kellett, of
Mndgee. A silver train flashed like moon-
light after the froth of chiffon and lace that
formed the wedding garment, and a family
veil of old lace was worn in alliance with
orange-buds. Bride’s sister, in ethereal pinks
and blues, was glove-bearer; and her mother
spread the feast later at the Coogee Bay
Hotel.
Sister Nell Ocock, Sydney Hospital’s
expert “instruments sister,” will no longer
be at hand and ready for the surgeon’s
next move. On Saturday last she laid down
the scalpel and the swab and betook herself,
arrayed in pink georgette and tulle, to St,
Philip’s on Church Hill. Here, attended
by Sister Margaret, who is matron of the
Ryde Home for Incurables, she promised
Robert J. Marks, of Concord, to make his
home her future theatre. Petty's spread the
feast under the eye of the bride's mother.
One of the newest engagements is that of
Jean Anderson, the attractive daughter of
Knight Robert Anderson, to Selwyn Hague
Smith, of Leura.
The Government meteorologist has nothing
whatever to do with the shower teas that
threaten to submerge popular Una Symes
before her marriage with Dr. Mervyn Hall,
of Orange, next month.
The Josef Kretschman Club keeps green
the memory of the grand old musician who
starved proudly during the war because,
g\ od Australian as he was in spirit and in
sentiment, pride would not allow him to
make any endeavor to dodge the prejudice
against his enemy origin, or to make a single
gesture of appeal to the many friends who
would have been glad to help him. The
Kretschman Club was founded by Esther
Kahn, the Sydney musician and composer;
besides forming the basis for reunion amongst
music-lovers it maintains a cot in the Royal
Alexandra Hospital in memory of the old
man. To-night the Players’ Club is giving a
performance of Milne’s “To Have the
Honor,” to help the club in its good deed.
Here is a picture of George Palmer, said
to be the youngest motion-picture producer
in the world. George is a Melbourne youth
who at 15 had saved enough money from the
sale of bottles
a n d from
d i s tributing
a d v e rtising
bills and dod-
gers to finance
his first pic-
ture, “T h e
Mail Robber.”
It was 200
feet long, and
Geo r g e not
only Wrote the
story but acted
the principal
part. His main
outlay was for
the camera-
man. Then he
went around
1: h e theatres,
and by perse-
verance in-
duced a few
suburban
showmen to screen his little picture as an
addition to the usual programme. Wherever
the picture went, George went, too, and in-
