energetic youth has produced “The North-
bound Limited,” a melodrama of the tall
timber and the thundering iron-horse. On
September 16 it will be released at the
Sydney Arcadia; and budding movie direc-
tors will have a chance to hear George tell
the story of the picture’s making, and later
see him act the dashing hero in his five-
reel story.
Seeking rest Dr. Thomas Haigh lately
came to Sydney as organist and choirmaster
of St. Andrew’s Cathedral. He had tired of
years of world-travelling as examiner for the
Associate Board. Hardly had he settled into
his job when ill health demanded an opera-
tion, and early this week death struck the
last chord. Haigh, besides standing high in
his profession—his compositions are of wide
range—was a generous soul: almost his last
hours were occupied in helping a lame dog
over a difficult stile.
On July 20, at Yule Island, Papua, Sister
Mary Madeline, the pioneer of the Catholic
mission, died at the age of 84 years. Sister
Madeline took up her duties in the Terri-
tory 41 years ago, braving great privations
and dangers, and continued her labors Until
a few years back. Her sweet disposition,
piety, zeal and courage won universal admi-
ration.
If Mrs. Margaret Phillips, of Neutral Bay,
had lived until next January she would have
been 60 years a widow. Her husband, Cap-
tain John Duthie Phillips, died at sea on
January, 1868; the old lady who lived on to
be 80 years of age, but died last Sunday, was
the daughter of another master mariner,
and one of her sons was for many years a
popular skipper on the Vancouver run.
Those very successful young men Teddy
Cahill and George Brookes threaten to con-
quer the United States. In October they
are booked for three appearances at the
gEolian Hall, in New York. In August
they will be in London, and in early Septem-
ber" they make their first appearance before
Paris audiences. Christmas, 1928. or early
in 1929 will, they hope, find them back
in Australia.
New Minister of Education Davies has
ordained that September 2 shall be set aside
in the schools as a day of appreciation of
Australian literature. September 2 has been
chosen as that is the anniversary of Henry
Lawson’s death: on that day lovers of his
work intend to make a pilgrimage to his
grave at Waverley. How many know that
the bed in which Henry lies whs the original
grave of Henry Kendall? When Kendall’s
remains were removed to the spot on the
hill. Lawson’s mother bought the ground,
with the idea that it should be the grave of
the poet-son for whom she had done so
much.
Who’ll vote that “The Idylls of the King”
and such knightly tales be made a compul-
sory study in our schools? With the dying-
out: of old ideas of chivalry, even the mar-
riage vow is neither buckler nor shield.
Before the wedding sheets were cold one
bridegroom last week murdered the woman
he had just sworn to cherish. Another girl-
wife of 20 was found butchered, her husband
lying on the floor beside her with his arteries
cut. It seems that education experts, busy
with their gospel that the tree of knowledge
must be stripped bare, have overlooked the
serpent who calls for ceaseless scotching.
Anyway, some old-fashioned education,
founded on Quixotic and Arthurian prin-
ciples, might be tried on flaming youth for a
change.
Henley has written about the difficulty of
capturing the occasion when “place and
hour and means for once are met.” Plain
Joe Salvana goes out and does the scene-
shifting for Fate: when he wants to paint
a picture of tall trees, lie takes his wife,
a collapsible studio and some tucker, and in a
sort of Ford caravan they “go bush.” For
his big picture (bullocks straining at the
load under towering gums) in the R.A.S.
Show this year he not only went bush, but
hired a bullock-team for three weeks, so
entranced was he with the complexion of
their hides. The owner of the bullocks
became so interested that he travelled to
Sydney to see the beasties hung.
Art lovers are wondering why the purse of
the Gallery trustees could not have been
opened for another 35 guineas to buy Lyall
Trindall’s “Dreaming.” Evidently the dear
old daddies thought the National treasure-
house needed some fresh tints, so it bought
Charles Wheeler’s “Noon Dreams” at the
modest figure of 300 guineas. Perhaps if
Trindall’s price for his lady indulging in the
new sunray treatment had been more envelop-
ing, the apple might have been thrown at
her.
With the first notes of spring the Madrigal
Society gave voice to jocund sounds. Indeed,
listening to the delicate harmonies of these
unaccompanied musical tapestries one felt
that to be a jolly shepherd and to sport with
Amaryllis in the shade should be the whole
of man’s desire. Arnold Mote led the choris-
ters, and such sweet examples of the old-
time art as Gibbons’s “Fair as the Rose” and
Stanford’s “Corydon Arise” brought visions
of a world very young and very clean. The
more dramatic “Death Croon” of Bantock
and Elgar’s “Love Tempest” were given with
great effect.
Over plans to help cheer the lot of the
boy settler nearly 100 women members of
the Anzac Fellowship debated for an hour at
the Australia the other morning. The meet-
ing was under the baton of Dr. Mary Booth,
and to help the funds of the Empire Service
Club local Lenglens and Tildens will fight
with ball and bat on the 26th.
Sydney’s very active Mayor Mostyn wants
•£lO,OOO for the Picton Lakes T.B. Settle-
ment. From August 21 to 27 a drive is
being made; next Sunday 355 clergymen will
preach the gospel of Health, and at three
o’clock the Domain will ring on the same
subject. The consular ladies have organised
an international appeal from the 22nd to the
25th; on the 26th a carnival will rage
throughout the city; and on the 27th a
sports corroboree, in which school kiddies,
athletes, suburban aldermen and other beau-
ties will take part, will occur at Victoria Park.
The Conservatorium’s unusual opera.
Weber’s “Euryanthe,” ran for a week, sus-
tained by a double cast. Helped by both
singers and orchestra it was the outstanding
success of the three so far produced. The
others were “The Immortal Hour” and “The
Marriage of Figaro.”
The Sacred Heart Convent of Kincoppal
in Elizabeth Bay is little sister to the Rose
Bay Convent, so there was nothing remark-
able in the Old Girls of the two uniting for
a dance at the Ambassadors. Blue and gold
and red and white mingled in the decorations
and in the ribbon wristlets w T orli by members
of the committee. Mrs. D’Apice, as president
of the senior association, was the official
hostess, and with her husband entertained
reps, of many convent schools. She dressed
in white georgettes heavy with crystal fringe.
Roma O’Dea, of Kincoppal, in fragile rose
and silver, presided over a round table of
merry-makers. The Mark Sheldons 'were
among the dancers, and the Lane Mullinses
and the Hugheses formed a family party.
Dr. IV- D. Laneton brought his lady, whose
coral georgettes were rich with silver embroid-
eries ; and Rhodes scholar James Flynn was
another of those who embraced the rosy hour
—that’s a quotation, and for Heaven’s sake
don’t put an i on to it.
Moving quietly round the exhibition of
the late Charles Conder’s pictures the other
day, with a wreath of golden cowslips in
her millinery, was a gentle lady with a
pleasant smile and a sheaf of memories of
the artist in his youth. She is Miss Conder,
a daughter of that surveyor who, in the long
ago, took “Charlie” Conder in hand w T hen
he came from England.

Prince and Princess Purachatra, of Siam,
and their daughter had a day with the g„e-
gees at the Australian Steeplechase meeting
at Caulfield on Saturday afternoon. The
Princess wore a mauve crepe de Chine gown
with bead tracery, a sapphire-blue ribbon hat,
and a squirrel-collared coat: of musquash.
Her daughter figured in gold and dull-green
striped silk, glimpsed beneath a nutria-fur
coat, and wore a navy silk hat. Though the
attendance was big there was not a great
muster of society, and the vice-regal box
was vacant. Just a few brave women wore
spring frocks, but covered them with black
satin, or kasha coats. There was a caress-
ing touch of spring in the air, but it wasn’t
a perfect day for anyone but McShortodds,
whose smile grew more and more bland as
favorite after favorite also ran. Mrs. Ben
Chaffey, in a silk-fringed gown of ashes of
roses hue; Mrs. Ronald Gumming, of the
Western district, brightly clad in a pleated
coat of vivid blue with same colored hat
dangling silver tassels; Mrs. Harry Henty,
in petunia shade, topped by a black hat
swathed with silver tissue ; the Mackey lady,
whose millinery and gown were of the new
dregs of wine tone; and Mrs. Merson Cooper,
who topped kolinsky furs and a black gown
with a burgundy felt and velvet hat, were
amongst lawn promenaders. Gwen Tait
cheered a smart black tailored suit with a
water-lily on her coat lapel. Beatrice Fischer
Bong wag another good-looking young woman
who chose black. The Richard Lintons, Mrs.
H. S. K. Ward, Premier Hogan and his wife,
Mrs. James Grice, •Mrs. A. V. Kewney, Mrs.
Harry Gullett and Mrs. McCosh Clark, of
Auckland, were there, too.
The Findon Harriers had inviting weather
for their annual race-meeting at Moonee
Valley, and a big crowd went out. Rain
held off all day and the sun shone brightly
upon coat-posies of violets and pansies. These
decorations for winter coats and furs have
now reached the size of shower-bouquets. The
picturesque course looked barer than usual —
the trees had all come under the hand of
the pruner—-and only a few small blossoms
were visible around the lawns. B. M. Cuth-
bertson and D. G. Landale celebrated the
winning of yet another Hunt Club Cup by
giving a tea-party to many fair followers of
the chase. About the only new note seen in
feminine wrapping was a cape of mole model-
led on the old dolman lines and bordered
with silver fox. Mrs. Alec McCracken was
tailored in grey tweed, with a hat of sapphire
velvet. Mrs. J. B. McArthur was clad in
navy-blue topped by a dahlia-hued velvet
hat/ Muriel and Margaret Cumming both
wore lengthy coats of black cloth bordered
with grebe, Margaret adding a black velvet
beret. Norme Collins in a tailored suit of
beige- and- black-checked tweed, with a felt
hat, caught the eye. Attractively attired
misses included Mollie Hunt, Joan and Helen
Rempriere, Vera Whiting and Eileen and
Madge Taylor. General Johnston’s daughter
Gwen, Mrs. Jack Morrison, of Woolbrook,
and Mrs. E. T. Embling were other fol-
lowers of the hounds who watched the racing.
Decorations of St. Kilda Town Hall for the
Aero Club’s Ball included a model Wackett
seaplane in the vestibule, replicas of the
Pinedo and Cobliam ’buses, an airship push-
ing its nose from a hangar over the clock,
a De Havilland on the stage and spare parts
all over the place. The club colors, light
and dark blue and scarlet, were mingled with
the mechanisms. Colonel T. W. White and
his tall, good-looking wife did the honors,
stole ends of crystal and gold beads gleaming
on her cherry-red georgette. Irvine, C.J.,
and liis lady were greeted with the Anthem,
which was thought by some to be incorrect,
as the State Governor isn’t out of the coun-
try and the Lieut.-Governor has no vice-
regal status save in his absence. Mrs. C.
N. McKay, who can handle a joystick with
the best of them, secretaried the hop, aided
by Miss B. E. Stewart and W. R. Garrett.
Miss McKay’s gown of ivory lace featured
long sleeves. Group-Capt. Williams’s wife
he is skimming round Australia just now
chose a robe of powder-blue velvet; Flight-
Eieut. Rigby’s wife sparkled in white and
crystal ; Major Berriman’s spouse was in
rose-patterned floral georgette and Major
Coleman’s life-partner looked well in a scin-
tillating mauve frocx. Squadron-leader Eric
Harrison’s wife —she’s the daughter of Chief-
Secretary Prendergast—showed glimpses of
water-lily-blue beneath a sheath of silver
sequins; Admiral Napier, General Chauvel,
Mayor Gray of St. Kilda and Mrs. Gray,
Captain and Mrs. E. C. Johnson, Dr. and
Mrs. Tomkin, sundry consuls and their
womenfolk, the W. M. Robins couple, Mrs.
Richard Fitcher and Mrs. Edgar Dye were
dancers. Marie Greene, in a frock of apri-
cot taffeta, and Jocelyn Outlnvaite, all amber
tulle frills, were two of the most nicely
dressed youngsters.
Beneath trails of pink roses and festoons
of blue and rose lights, the ex-students of
Genazzano Convent danced at St. Ivilda Town
Hall on Thursday night. Forty-six hostesses
who did the honors were distinguished by
posies of pansies or carnations. There was
some dainty dressing. Three lassies wore
tier upon tier of narrow silk or bead fringe;
others had billowy tulle skirts linked to
tiglit-fitting bodices; others again affected
the lengthy full or panniered attire of the
early-Victorian era, and a few chose frocks
that were masses of tiny frills fyom waist-
line to hem. Crystal- and bead-sprinkled
raiment, of course, was well represented. The
president, Alma Simpson, wore apricot taf-
feta that blended into a hem of oxidised net.
Mrs. John Horgan, who, with Mary Hobbs,
secretaried the hop, had a glimmer of opal-
escent sequins on a petunia-colored gown.
Mrs. W. J. McLean wore black cheered with
gold and silver embroidery, and Mrs. Maguire,
another energetic worker, favored black
georgette. Dr. Inez Rarer, a cousin of the
flying man, showed a glitter of rhinestones
and a dash of green on a white frock.
Patsy, her twin sister, was frilled in shot
mauve taffeta. Green of the lily of the
valley, with a flicker of rhinestones, com-
posed Poppy Sic William’s gown ; Kathleen
O’Shaughnessy was in fairy raiment of white
tulle with a silver bodice; Dorothea Hart
waved a huge shaded orange feather fan over
air amber-toned robe, and Nora Lawrence
suggested a falling rose in a lengthy, deep-
pink velvet gown with a wide hem of tulle
sprinkled with petals.. Dorothy Dawkins
struck a new note in ivory taffeta bordered
with wine-liued velvet, scattered with stray
roses appliqued in beads. Dr. and Mrs.
Percy Shelton, the Grantley Shelton couple,
Dr. and Mrs. J. Ryan and Madeline Page
were among the dancers.
Mrs. Merson Cooner and the committee of
a bridge party for St. Vincent’s Hospital at
Menzies’ on Thursday afternoon induced the
Prince of Siam and his spouse to accept
invitations. The Princess wore silk alpaca
of rose tone striped with blue and allied to
georgette splashed with a feathery . design,
also blue, and crowned by a navy silk hat.
She received a posy of rare orchids from
Mrs. Clarence Marriott. Premier Hogan’s
wife, a study in Wedgwood-blue cloth and
felt, and Mayoress Morell, with her daughter
Sheila, helped to entertain the royal guests.
Mrs. George Bowcher and Nina Glass were
the secretaries of the show, which was well
attended. Frederic Bentley,, of the “Rose
Marie” company, exercised his talents as an
auctioneer, adding a bit to the proceeds. The
Tallis lady, Donna Grossardi, Mrs. Robert
Bruce, Mrs. C. J. Ahern and Mrs. George
Langridge were patrons. Mrs. Maurice Mac-
gillicuddy watched the exchequer.
This is a Lafayette picture of Vera Bed-
ford, one of the most promising of Aus-
tralian musical composers. She has to her
credit various
orchestral and
choral pieces,
and at the
Assembly Hall
on September
8 a selection
of these will
be performed.
T h e magnum
opus is a set-
ting of Keats’s
“Grecian
Urn,” arranged
for three
voices with a
string accom-
paniment. The
road is a hard
one for natives
of this conti-
nent who feel
iin p e lied to
climb to the
high peaks of
musical expression, so tlie concert ought
to have the support of all genuine music-
lovers.
Villers-Bretonneux, which has become a
part of Australia by adoption, will become a
little more like its pre-war self as the result
of a dance at Admiralty House one. night
last week. Madame Crivelli was chief en-
gineer, her black gown enlivened with gold
lace. Mayoress Morel! was in grey georgette
half concealed by a bridge coat of silver
tissue and fox fur. French Consul TurckS
wife dangled deep silk fringe from a frock
of ruby-hued georgette, and her daughter
Ninette, clad in moss green, perched a bunch
of cherries on a shapely shoulder. Frances
Woolcott, in ivory-chiffon velvet, and Mollie
Elliott, whose attire suggested a dew-
sprinkled blush rose, did good work as secre-
taries. Colonel Watson’s wife was in a
frock of black tulle, and Zillah Carter
arrested the eye with a silver-tissue turban
and a frock of oxidised lace that had a glimpse
of pale green beneath it. Donna Grossardi,
Sheila Morell and Koma Smith were
dancers.
Thurza Rogers, the little Maorilander
whose dancing talents were discovered and
made use of by Pavlova, lo ked winsome in
a yellow' and gold lace frock and a wrap of
sky-biue, all accordeon-pleated trills, wuen sue
said h r thanks after the curtain of the
first night of “Tip Toes.” Green is the color-
note or the piece in which Miss Rogers is
making her bid for stardom. Chorus and
ballet don’t get away from this color—
either by itself or mingled with mauve or
blue or petunia hues —till the last scene,
when there is a change to pow'der-blue. Lily-
of-the-valley green with a shower of crystal
is chosen for one lot of frocks, and taffeta
in sea shades (to blend with the scenery)
is used for another set. Bertha Ricardo,
Winnie Tate a 1 d Mona Barlee also wear
green. Pianist Henry Penn, of Sydney Dili.
Con., helped Jack Wright with the two-
piano part of the unusual orchestral score.
Though the weather is getting a little less
Antarctic, the stalls were full of white furs.
Among the first-nighters wrnre Dr. Hewlett’s
wife and daughter Nancy, Dr. Taylor’s wife
and daughter Janet, Dr. Bob Strong’s w r ife,
and Mrs. Peter Lynch. Viola Ramsay,
charming in green-blue, Beryl Ainslic, Mrs.
A. Scott, Mrs. Douglas Stewart and Nellie
Veitch were also in the forefront.
Mrs. E. T. Enabling, who rides to hounds
whenever there are any hounds to follow,
casts a fence-estimating glance out of this
window. She’s
a widow. Her
husband, Dr
Herbert Emb
ling, who died
about four

in command of
several hos
pital ships and
was stationed

at the clear-

After his death
Mrs. Embling
opened a rid-

Lorna. Hen
pupils are
taught tlie art and etiquette of what the

Dickinson Monteath made the picture.
The medical profession rolled up in force
to the uniting of Isabel Florence, youngest
daughter of Dr. and Mrs. Thomas Stang, of
Kew, and Dr. Leslie Anderson Love, the
X-ray specialist, at Christ Church, South
Yarra. The bride’s veil of Limerick lace
trailed over a silvery georgette frock, with a
gleam of pink, and her two ’maids, Alice
Clarke and Olga Christine Stang. harmonised
in pearl-grey frocks, sashed with coral pink
and jade blue respectively. There were
rejoicings at No. 9 Darling-street.
The E. A. Bateses’ elder daughter, Etliel-
wyn, wore a Brussels-lace robe, with furnish-
ings of ivory tulle and orange-blossoms, when
she exchanged vows with her Maorilander,
H. McKail Geddes, at St. John’s. Camber-
well, on Thursday afternoon. The bride-
groom is a son of the late John Geddes, of
Auckland. Mary Bates and Myra Watson,
in pale-pink georgette and silver lace, with
s^me-toned felt hats, attended the bride.
The Bates home, Maleeta, Balwyn, was the
scene of rejoicings.
Two learned young Australians were
made one in London last month. The bride
was Florence Young, M.A., once a member
of Geelong Preparatory School staff. The
man is a Sydney graduate, Rex Blumer,
M. B.Sc., Dip. Ed., wdio is one the
leading professorial lights of Prince of Wales
College, Achimota, W. Africa.
The wedding of Hilda, daughter of John
Grice and his lady, with Flight-Lieutenant
V. R. Scriven, was a social event in the Big
Smoke a few weeks ago. The bride wore a
gown of all gold softened with a tulle veil of

and yellow roses. Sister Evelyn, who held
crimson blossoms against a frock of per-
venche-blue taffeta, was attendant maid. The
Stradbrokes and their daughter Lady Helena
Rous attended the subsequent rejoicings at
Cla ridge’s.
A. R. Somers Cox. who recently married
a Sydney g’rl in London, is a relative of our

N. station is known as Stonehenge.

The Education Circle of the Victoria
League listened to a paper read by Professor
Ernest Scott’s wife on "Bolshevism in Art
at tlic Queen’s Hall on I 1 rid ay night, ohe
deplored what she regards as decadent in-
fluences at work in literature, painting,
sculpture, music and architecture, as exempli-
fied in what she had seen during a recent
tour of Europe. Bernard Hall, ot the
National Gallery, Col. Maudsley s lady and
the Mitchell lady supported the views ex-
pressed. Mrs. Scott wore flowered georgette.
Mrs. E. H. Lascelles and Edward Miller s
lady’ entertained Lady Somers and others at
supper afterwards at Melbourne Mansions.
The committee room of the Lyceum Club
glowed with the golden bloom when the
Wattle League had its annual summing up,
and the president, Mrs. Britomarte James,
poured tea. The only changes decreed were
the appointment of T. C. Alsop in the place
oil Secretary Rankin, retired, and a rise m
the annual sub. from a bob to half a crown.
Ex-Senator and Mrs. J. H. Keating boiled
the biily at their Toorak home on Sunday
afternoon to farewell Solicitor-General Gar-
ran and his lady and the G.-G. s oflieial
secretary, J. 11. Starling, and his life-partner,
all doomed to exile at Canberra. The Navy,
Army, Church, Law, Art and Stage were rep-
resented, and the two McDonagh girls gave
the Australian film industry a place in the
picture. Dr. Bartolini and Kenneth Impett,
lately of Maoriland, warbled to accompani-
ments played by the hostess, whose attire
was a blend of geranium-red and black.
Daughter Tulla, who wore a frock of floral
georgette with a reseda green hat, assisted
iier parents in entertaining.
Dr. Gengoult Smith is off to Europe to
ioin Iris sister, Mrs. Jimmy Dyer, and her
husband. The Dyers have written from
Heidelberg, where they have been enjoying
some splendid performances of opera.
Movies and dancing were mingled last
week at Carlyon’s ballroom, St. Kilda, now
known as the Cairo. The building has been
remodelled. The gallery, very much en-
larged, accommodates those who wish to con-
centrate, on the flickers, and downstairs the
Charlestoners and Blackbottomers can
gyrate between the fillums. Loges with
tables and chairs are retained for the
dancers. Geelong and Ballarat are making
the same experiment.
The Red Cross offers thanks for 800 beau-
tifully-knitted sweaters sent in response to
the appeal made on behalf of invalid soldiers.
Some of the knitters were anonymous, hence
this note.
Floating over the sea in our direction are
Lord and Lady Plunket. He is the successor
of the Baron who once governed Maoriland,
and she is a particular pal of Betty of
York. The lady was born under a golden
shower. Her father. Joe Lewin, made a
pile as a financier and his girl married one of
the Barnatos, who passed out and left her
richer than ever. Her mother. Fanny Ward,
is playing the lead at the Coliseum, though
over sixty. She was one of the pioneers
of face-lifting and helped to make the opera-
tion popular.
Mrs. Stanley Lewis has brought home her
two sons to visit their father, who went to
Sydney to meet his family. Ratcliffe, the.
elder son, who is at Cambridge, has not seen
his dad since he w 7 as a nipper. He will fol-
low in his father’s footsteps as a lawyer, so
they will have a topic of common interest.
The stay is limited to a few weeks and the
reunited family wall occupy the paternal
Lewis’s bachelor flat in Toorak.
Ballarat Golf Club’s latest member is
Bishop Foley, a cousin of Archbishop
Mannix. The Bishop used to go in for
pedestrianism pure and simple, but finds it
more exciting to walk after the little white
ball.
The nervous bridegroom is a common-
place of fiction, but truth, which is stranger,
produced a panic-stricken best-man at a
fashionable wedding the other day. When
called upon to produce the ring he passed
over a small corkscrew, and the parson’s
gaze grew bleak and penetrating as he
handed it back to the donor.
The Norton Grimwades seem likely to
return weary and travel-worn, for _ they
have planned to cram a lot of trotting into a
limited time space. After a stay in Scotland
son Geoffrey was collected for a trip up the
Danube, which means calling at several
cities. A pause to regain breath will be
made at La Toquet, a gay resort patronised
by a cosmopolitan crowd. Before boarding
a steamer for home the Grimwades will
cross the Atlantic, look in at New York, and
then wander south to cast an eye on the
Panama Canal.
Eric Manley has decided that a wife
would complete his lone Gippsland home,
and his choice has fallen on Miss Pringle,
who has been private secretary to Hobart's
Lud Mayors for the last five years.

Rexona Girls are world-famous for
their beautiful skin and hair. To be a
Rexona Girl is to use only Rexona
Soap and enjoy that natural beauty of
skin and hair which cannot be
imitated, but can be obtained by its
regular use.
Ordinary toilet soaps do not assert
this influence. Many of them contain
free alkali, which tends to dry the skin
and destroy its delicate texture. Even
the best of such soaps can only
cleanse; they cannot heal and protect
the skin.
Rexona Soap contains the same
soothing, healing, antiseptic balsams
as Rexona, the Rapid Healer, the
value of which in the treatment of
skin affections is known throughout
the world.
Simply use Rexona Skin and Facial
Soap regularly once or twice a day
and see if it does not quickly soothe
and cleanse the pores, lessen the ten-
dency to pimples and leave the com-
plexion clear, fresh and velvety.

Legal Notice.
DEBT HA AIJCUSTA SAUER deceased.
PURSUANT to an Order of the
Supreme Court of South Australia Common-
wealth of Australia dated the 16th day of
December 1926 and made in the matter
of the estate of Bertha Augusta Sauer
deceased (late of Parkside in the State of
South Australia Spinster who died on the
20th day of September 1926) No. 901 of
1926 whereby the following enquiry was
directed: Who were the next-of-kin of the
said Bertha Augusta Sauer living at the
time of her decease and whether any of
them are since dead and if so who are their
respective legal personal representatives.
NOTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN that all
persons claiming to be entitled under the said
enquiry are on or before the 9th day of Janu-
ary: 1928 to send by post prepaid to the Public
Trustee Supreme Court Adelaide South Aus
tralia their full Christian and surnames
addresses and descriptions and full particu-
lars of their claims. Claimants are to
attend personally or by their Solicitor before
the Master at his Chambers Supreme Court
Victoria Square Adelaide on the stli day of
March 1928 at 10.30 o’clock in the fore-
noon being the time appointed for hearing
and adjudicating upon the claims or ip
default thereof they will be peremptorily
excluded from the benefit of the said Order.
DATED the 7th day of July 1927.
(Signed) F. B. McBRYDE,
Deputy Master of the Supreme Court of
South Australia.
N.B.—Bertha Augusta Sauer was the only
child of Wilhelm Sauer and Elizabeth Nagora
formerly of Germany wljo were married in
South Australia on 6th July, 1857.
HOMBURG. MELROSE and lIOM-
BURG, Adelaide. South Australia, Solicitors
for the Public Trustee.

IN CONJUNCTION WITH MONSTER ART UNION
OBJECT: TO RAISE £25,000 FOR THE
KINDERGARTEN UNION OF N.S.W.
Will you help us to raise £25,000 so that we can erect more Free Kindergartens
and establish a Hospital Kindergarten where poor crippled children doomed to
a life of suffering can be brought each day, to derive the benefits of the beautiful
Kindergarten teaching and to have a little more sunshine brought into their lives?
Mr. Boyd Edkins—well known to all motorists, and a good sportsman—has
agreed to stand as a candidate in the “Best Sport” Competition. Racing Clubs,
Sporting Bodies and several large Business Houses are nominating candidates.
We need your help.

BRISBANE
The Centre of Queensland's
Distinguished Social Life!
QUEENSLAND.
Brisbane.
A wild westerly, with swirling clouds of
dust, accounted for a smaller attendance than
usual on People’s Day at the Show, but other
days and nights with fireworks and electric-
light races made up for that. The bitumen-
covered permanent ways and the hot-water
boilers for picnickers were welcome inno-
vations this year. The forestry court, a
thing of beauty, was informative in the mat-
ter of woods, and panels of pale biscuit-
tinted quandong wood were much admired.
There were specimens of satinray which
rippled with rosy light, and some fine carv-
ings to reveal hidden loveliness in our
Queensland timbers.
The Stonehavens are at Government
House. They were guests of the Women’s
College at Kangaroo Point. Lady Goodwin
came with them. Mrs. T. J. Ryan wore
silver-grey with white fox furs. Freda Bage
was in her cap and gown over blue. Jill
Ryan presented a posy of yellow and brown
pansies to Lady Stonehaven, who wore tango
mariette with a coat of rose beige and a
toast-brown straw hat. Frances Scott handed
red roses to Lady Goodwin, who was in grey
with a shell-pink feather mount on her black
hat. Mrs. Ryan and Freda Bage welcomed
the vice-regal guests. Speaker Bertram’s
wife, Dr. Jean White Haney, Mrs. A. J.
Thynne, the Philp and Morgan ladies, Mrs.
Journeaux, Mrs. J. N. Devoy. Mrs. M. For-
rest and Canon and Mrs. Stevenson were
amongst the throng.

lii the morning all the Exes, went up
Mount Coo-ha, Mayor Jolly doing the honors
and telling the visitors of the glories of the
2500 acres in the ranges where natural
foliage will be preserved for the benefit of
posterity. The pavilion was dressed up in
flags and pink roses blushed on the princi-
pal table.
The Priminister and Mrs. Bruce put in
part of their afternoon at the Q.T.C. races.
It was a fur-coat day and the enclosed ver-
andahs attached to the grandstand were
sought for private tea-parties. At Lennon’s,
in a setting of roses and sweet-pea, many
dinner-parties were given, and gay carnival
caps brightened the rooms. That night the
Authors and Artists’ Association and the
Lyceum Club gave parties and there were
several balls.
Alfred O’Shea gave two concerts in the
Exhibition, and his tenor voice was much
liked. On the first night, Mrs. T. J. Ryan,
Mrs. Dunlop, Miss Tracey, the Ted Baynes,
Mrs. M. Forrest, Alice Gore-Jones and Etta
Young were in the audience. The Irish
Club entertained the singer one evening,
and Mrs. J. Shaw hud a party for some
of the company at her home at Auchen-
flower.
At St. John’s Cathedral Allan Robinson
married Enid North, daughter of Mrs. Frank
North and grand-daughter of ex-71us tice
Chubb. The bride wore ivory mariette,
crusted in pearls, with a train of ivory satin
mounted on pale blue. Her veil of ivory
tulle was caught with a diamente bandeau.
Alice Hawthorn, in turquoise taffetas, and
Leila Langberg. in shot-blue taffetas, were
’maids. The cathedral was dressed in shasta-
daisies, sweet-peas and lilies.
The Authors and Artists’ Association and
the Booksellers’ Association are working for
the success of Australian Authors’ _ Week.
Brisbane has seldom known so much intellec-
tual activity.
Geranium, rose and gold decked the South
Brisbane City Hall for the Girl Guides ball,
at which all their Exes, were present, and
all the private secretaries and aides. Guides
Zoe Eales and Joyce Green presented
bouquets to Juady Stonehaven and Lad.v
SOUTH AUSTRALIA.
Adelaide.
This week has seen the launching of the
public appeal for funds to build a house
for the University Students’ Union, culmi-
nating in a meeting in the Town Hall, where
speakers included Chancellor Murray, Symon,
K.C. (who has given £lO,OOO for the
Women’s Union building). Jpdge Parsons,
and politicians from both Benches. For pre-
liminary there was a students’ procession
•with the usual embellishments and with an
unrehearsed addition of unemployed, and for
interludes there were constant irruptions of
song and interjection from the students’
gallery. Subscriptions, handsomely started
by the University Council and staff, are
already well on towards £12,000.
There was dancing—-lots of it —on the same
night. One-time pupils of Girton House
had their annual frivol at the Osborne 5-all,
bedecking it in the black and gold and
blue of the school colors, and empanelling
die school’s head, Mrs. Angrove, to do the
honors with the committee. Ivooyonga
golfers and their friends went jazzing in the
Masonic Hall, and at the Palais, a step or
two away, there was more jazzing by way
of adding to the Walkerville Boys’ Home
exchequer. Lady Bridges was at the Palais
hop, in green and red brocaded silver tissue,
and brought her daughter, who slipped a
pink-feathered silver coat over her pink
frock.
Bridge and dancing were the programme
at the Uni. Women’s Union at-home, staged
in Elder Hall, all aglow with pink roses tum-
bling from wicker baskets, and with festoons
of pink streamers on the white walls. A
committee of half a dozen included the
union’s secretary, Mary Frost, and the
greetings were murmured by Professor and
Mrs. Osborn —-she’s the union’s president.
The Sturgess exhibition was opened by
Mrs. H. H. Dutton, a painter and a con-
sistent and generous patron of Australian
arts. Lady Bridges and her daughter had
a private view the day before the opening.
From England comes news of the Maxwell
Fowlers, who are golfing at Burnham
Beeches, of which club he is secretary. She
is turning to account a talent she shares
with others of her family (she is one of
the Henry Ayers clan), and is doing carica-
tures of feminine golfers for one of the
journals.
Mrs. Britten Jones’s win at Seaton makes
her this State’s woman golf champion for
the fifth time—she was in England last year
when Lily Gordon annexed the title. Mrs.
Jones, Lily Gordon, Alison Thomas and
Rosemary Cudmore will probably represent
the South Australian feminine golfers at
Sandringham.
Included in Muriel Starr’s cast for “Nice
People” is Noel Boyd, who served her stage
apprenticeship with the local Repertory
Theatre and did so well as Dora Delaney in
“Fanny’s First Play” that she determined to
try her luck in Melbourne. She made her
first bow as a professional in the chorus of
“Tell Me More.”
TASMANIA.
Hobart.
The O’Grady and niece Margaret took
front seats at the matinee concert got up by
Miss Gaggin and James Marsh for the Girl
Guides. Miss O’Grady wore the navy blue
uniform as Deputy-State Commissioner.
She’s one of the lucky few who are suited
by that very severe hat. Notable among the
items were Mrs. Mary Grant’s violin solos
and Myra Gillou’s two songs. Posies of spring
flowers were sold by the Guides in the inter-
val.
Japanese peach blossoms and white
daisies made the big reception-room at the
Lyceum Club look gay when members gave
parting good wishes to Mrs. Jess. Mrs. God-
dard arranged a good musical programme.
Mayor Wignall led a batch of aldermen and
business men through our new fur factory
when the first batch of coney seal was
passed through. About 70 people are now
at work, and the 'director's have hopes that
the place will be the fur centre of the
whole Commonwealth before long.
WESTRALIA.
Perth.
Tue Perth Club’s annual frolic found the
rooms a bit too small for the invasion, but
everybody had a good time, and the club’s
reputation for hospitality stands as high
as ever.
The Uni. folk have had an afternoon
spree on the river-banks, where the old home
of George Shenton now houses students. The
completion of the new engineering building
supplied the excuse. There was a hop in
the evening.
The golf championships havc < been exciting
our , ollowers of the wee white ball- Isla
Taylor of Fremantle’s win over State cham-
pion Molly McMillan has been the occasion
of great rejoicings in me Port.
Hal Colebatcli and his lady are packing
their belongings for Sydney, where an im-
portant position awaits the head of the
family. She will be missed here, for she
was a good worker for many worthy causes.
Mrs. S. J. Anderson has gone to Sydney
to meet her aviator son Keith, who is due
in Sydney from ’Frisco if the flight be suc-
cessful. Keith will marry next month—if
all goes well.
This is a picture of E. J. Stuart taken
during a recent visit to London, where he
went on a mission in connection with the

The wedding of the week was that of
Maisie, second daughter of Mr. and Mrs.
Percy Armstrong, of West Perth, to Fred
Davies. Maisie is a niece of the Australian
writer Mrs. Hugo Throssell, wife of the V.C.
Mrs. James Brennan’s return has been
quite a triumphal affair. She and her three
girleens, Norma, Kathleen and Mollie, re-
turned on Monday to find their house a
bower of flowers, with stacks of letters of
welcome waiting. The girls were presented
at Court by Mrs. Amcry, wife of the
Dominions Secretary.

THE INKY WAY.
Coot : Melbourne Herald has struck a
new newspaper stunt; the quest is for a Vic-
torian farmer who has never been to Mel-
bourne. The hrst to satisfactorily establish
Ins claim is offered a free trip to the Mel-
bourne Show; if lie is married and has a
family, they are to be included in the shout.
“Landa” : Advt. from a North Coast news-
paper
Pastor Hunter will address W.C.T.U. on “Drink
and Degradation in Methodist Church, Fry Street
3.30 to-morrow afternoon.”
I was busy in the office that day, otherwise
I would have gone along. Country town
life wants a shake up like that now and
again.
“Lao-dah”: Vic. Chief Sec. Prendergast
who served his time at the case and was a
working comp, long before he was a poli-
tician, finds his knowledge handy nowadays.
Oftentimes when he has been speaking in a
country town he slips round to the office of
the local rag after the meeting is over and
sets up his own idea of what he thought he
said.
“Bangemall”: Touching these alliterative
headlines (B. 11/8/’27) the most piquantly-
laconic in my recollection were placed over
a cable published in a W.A. newspaper dur-
ing the Boer War. The message told how
President Kruger, on the eve of his depar-
ture to Europe, had despatched his private
fortune, or as much of it as was in portable
form, ahead of him to Delagoa Bay. The
editor, as sparing of letters as of words,
made itl “Oom’s Oof Off.”
The latest twinkle upon the Inky Way is
provided by Bill Bowyang’s Magazine, of
Bowen (Q.). Frans Reid, the editor, has
been a resident of Northern Queensland for
many years, and has managed to keep up a
substantial literary output from that quarter
of Australia —“Bill Bow-yang” is only one of
his pen-names. The magazine offers a new
field for the freelance; and, as the freelance
w-ants all the field he can get, the new- arrival
has The Bulletin’s best wishes.
“K.D.S.” : Why is it that Australian news-
papers omit from their notices of new books
the little extra push that would accrue to
writers and publishers by the simple men-
tion at the end of the review of the price
and postage? Many English journals invari-
ably add this pertinent information. I have
been deterred from ordering many a book
which reviewers have made me wish for by
the uncertainty about the cost. It is easy
enough for city folk to run to a bookstall
but the outbacker needs to know r just how-
much he will have to get a postal-note for
when next in the township.
“Geebung”: Comfortably seated at 6WF,
Perth, I listened to the progress of the Demp-
sey-Sharkey fight, heard the excited shouts of
the huge crowd present, their cheers and
hoots, the deep tone of the gong and the
voice of the announcer—in fact, it w r as as
if one were sitting at the ringside at the
other side of the world. We heard every-
thing clearly and well up to the fifth round,
when fading began and put an end to things.
It was over three hours later before any
of the cables drifted into the office.
“Knib”: Old-timers w-ho toiled at the
Argus or Daily Telegraph offices, Collins-
street, feel a pang as they watch wreckers
demolishing their sometime workshops and
removing features of the tony thoroughfare!
Compared with the D.T. building the Argus
structure was modern. For a few r years after
the late Sam Winter regained control of the
Herald the paper occupied the D.T. build-
ing, but was glad to escape to Degraves’s
old bond store, corner of Flinders and Russell
streets, which it subsequently bought. Both
the Argus and Herald have now palatial
new homes—one at the corner of Elizabeth
and Latrobe streets and the other at the
corner of Flinders and Collins-place.
“Strict Veg.”: The ruling passion of one
interviewer on a Melbourne daily is a keen
interest in disease, and he is never happier
than when he can get his victim on to the
subject of his ailments. The other day he
called on a visiting notable who had
apparently suffered from everything in the
dictionary of pathology. For over an hour the
pressman drank in the details of the opera-
tion for appendicitis in N’York. the inventory
of what was found in the great one’s
gall-bladder in London and of the sudden
and inexplicable attack of locomotor ataxia
at Port Said. It was not until the reporter
had got back to the office with only half an
hour to write up his copy that he realised
he had never asked his subject what he had
done in the intervals when he was out of
bed.
“G.Q.”: Capt. C. H. Peters. M.C., who is
the driving force behind the Australian and
Maoriland Authors’ Weeek (12th to 19th
Sept.), has neglected no side of the business.
He has even got Archbishop Lees of Mel-
bourne to frame a prayer for the stimulation
or guidance or regeneration of the men of
ink. Here it is
Almighty God, Who hast made the people of this
Empire members of one great commonwealth for the
service of the world, we pray Thee to bless all who
speak where many listen, and write what many
read; teach them that they - may teach others, in-
spire them that they may inspire their fellows, ami
grant that, thinking little of profit and fame, they
may do their part to make the heart of this people
wise, its mind sound and its will righteous.
It is a well-meant specimen of ecclesiastical
oratory, though it might have said something
about amusement as well as instruction. To
be amusing is to be successful nowadays.
“M.G.S.” : The best blow struck for decen-
tralisation in Vic. lately is the banding of
over 160 country papers to carry on a col-
lective argument with the capital city. A
competent bureau is located in Melbourne,
and whenever any move is made by which
truly rural interests are endangered, within
24 hours or so people in 160 country centres
are reading the case for their side in a well-
written syndicated article. .Hitherto the
countryman’s weakness lias been the lack of
co-ordinated action. Deputations have ambled
into Melbourne and placed the grievances ol
their own small areas before Ministers, who
have listened politely, shown them the door
and rung for morning-tea. They have been
less frightened of a Box Flat deputation, or
of deputations from a hundred Box Flats,
than of two lines in a city daily. With 1W
newsunuprs howling as one dog things will
be.different. ■

♦
“The Road to Rome” introduced a very
attractive and lively lady, Margaret Law-
rence, to an audience at Sydney Criterion
on Saturday night. The playwright, Robert
Sherwood, has had no scruples about out-
raging history to furnish a humorous as
well as pungent satire on war and militarism,
and in dressing it up to suit modern stage
requirements. Fabius Maximus, mentioned in
the course of the proceedings as “one hun-
dred per cent. Roman,” in case anyone should
have forgotten one of his main titles to
fame, is appointed Dictator to meet the
menace of the Carthaginian invasion under
Hannibal; the appointment swells the pride
of his very Roman mother, but appears of
very small moment to his half-Athenian,
childless wife, who believes that wars should
be settled “in a civilised way.” She retorts
to Fabius, when he reproaches her with her
ignorance of the doings of Rome’s enemy,
that, after all, he knows nothing about
Aristotle, which is largely the key to the
argument. The breaking of the news of the
Roman disaster at Cannae and the arrival
of the victorious Hannibal outside the walls
of ill-garrisoned Rome is a bit of admirable
work, and gives a dramatic tension to the
first act.- This is unfortunately lost to a
large extent in the somewhat psycho-ana-
lytical arguments that follow when Amytis,
the wife of Fabius, with two Sicilian slaves
whom Roman law does not permit to marry,
is caught “snooping” about the Carthagin-
ian camp and brought before Hannibal. The
interest is well enough maintained, but all
the clever dialogue with its stunningly-mixed
morality has a sense of unreality; and when
Amytis by argument (helped by her having
spent a night with him) persuades Hannibal
to be something greater than a conqueror,
and to leave Rome to' destroy itself, one is
conscious of having been mildly absorbed
and considerably amused without feeling!
solicitude about the fate of any one of the
puppets who provide the entertainment. The
probability that Amytis may have the child
that she has desired and that Fabius will
be blissfully ignorant that Hannibal is the
parent is the amazing conclusion upon which
the curtain falls, only just in time to prevent
earlier good impressions of brightness from
being completely dispelled.
* * # *
Margaret Lawrence is a lady who can
make involved argument bright and enter-
taining ; her wheedling of the. austere Hanni-
bal is delightfully done. Louis Bennison has
a stiff task to present a military commander
called upon to combine drawing-room man-
ners with the most ruthless ideas of war-
fare ; his dignity and restraint save the
curious compound from being ridiculous.
Wallis Clark is the heavy Roman husband,
Fabius; and Barry Livesey is animated as
Mago, Hannibal’s younger brother, who is
irked by the curtailment of victorious
soldiers’ privileged amongst the inhabitants
of pillaged towns, concerning which Carl
Francis, as a sergeant, and Frank Hatherley
and John Fernside, as soldiers, discourse
joyously. George Baxter, as the fire-eating
Scipio, is too apt to get out his words in
a tangled torrent; Gerald Kay Souper is a
dignified but thin-legged Roman general, and
Frederick Forester, as Hasdrubal, general
of Hannibal’s cavalry, Herbert Leigh and
John Bedouin, as two infantry generals,
fulfil the playwright’s obvious purpose as
satires on the red tabs of all wars. A
minor love interest is provided by the slaves,
Meta (in which part Billie Lockwood was
exceedingly pleasing) and Varius (rather
colorlessly done by Jack Mayer). Nellie
Mortyne capably portrays the virulent
mother-in-law, the proud Fabia.
ss * * >;c
Whilst Hannibal was being held np out-
side Rome by an engaging lady at the
Criterion, Julius Caesar was done to death
within the city at the Conservatorium. The
first performance of Arthur Greenaway’s
production took place on Friday last; but
it was repeated on Saturday Mnd Monday
and will probably have revivals. The play
ran its course before green curtains, with
no more stage furnishings than were actu-
ally needed. Arthur Greenaway’s portrayal
of Antony was given with a restraint which
brought into fine contrast his moment of
genuine grief with Caesar’s dead body; but
William Collins was so weak as Julius that
it seemed fortunate that he was murdered
early. J. Norman. Shield was little better
as Brutus; but Heath Burdock’s lean and
crafty Cassius was a live character, and
Gilbert Howell made a convincing Casca.
Grace Allison was hardly up to the part of
Portia; "Doris Williams was good as Cal-
purnia, and the long cast showed sound
training, especially evident in the case of
Grammar-school boys who filled in as
supers, and proved much more realistic
citizens than producers like Oscar Asche
are able to obtain.
* $ * *
Frances Alda has returned to Australia
while yet the lark’s on the wing in her voice.
It is a voice full of beauty and emotional
power, as was amply shown in “One Fine
Day"’ fi’om “Butterfly,” at the first concert
of her season. The programme ranged over
the whole of the soprano pasturage. The
opening group included “L’ungi dal Caro
Bene” (Seeelii), the artless "Je ne suis
qu’une bergfere” of Philidor, Handel’s “Oh,
Sleep, Why Dost Thou Leave Me?” and
Arnold Bax’s Christmas Carol. The Handel
number was sung with a moving simplicity,
and the outpouring triumph of the soaring
glorias and alleluias in the Bax Carol let
free the voice in all its richness. Spanish,
French and Russian songs were bracketed
for the artist’s second appearance, and of
these the sombre tragedy of Rachmaninoff’s
“Soldier’s Bride” was haunting in its in-
tensity. Something of an anti-climax fol-
lowed in several songs of undistinguished
sentiment, but they were rendered well
enough to ensure them a fresh lease of life
on the local concert platform. Monro’s “My
Lovely Celia,” and Arne’s “The Lass with
the Delicate Air” were earlier gems; Fond-
rain’s "Chanson Norvegienne” gripped the
ear and the imagination, but the Puccini
numbers “In Quelle Trine Morbide,” and
the Minuetto from “Manon Leseaut” dis-
played the voice and the singer in their true
element. A last tribute is compelled by
Alda’s singing of “Home Sweet Home” at
her opening concert, but what Nellie Melba
will say when she hears of it Heaven alone
knows.
* * * *
The Repertory players secured Nellie Fer-
guson’s professional assistance in presenting
Drinkwater’s “Mary Stuart” at the Sydney
Palace on Saturday, and although a little
inclined to confuse heaviness with queenly
dignity she succeeded very well in a part
that kept her on the stage during almost
the entire action. In Drinkwater’s hands
Mary loses a good deal of such romance as
lias been woven about her. Her story is
introduced by a conversation in which an
elderly bachelor of to-day advises a young
married friend not to be impatient with his
wife as she may be only innocently making
tests to find the Great Love —like Mary
Stuart. That switches Mary and her days
on; with Lord Darnley refusing to believe
the best when he discovers his wife in lover-
like proximity to Rizzio, and showing himself
an undesirable character with a penchant
for calling Mary a harlot. John Cox starts
stiffly but loses his head very well as the
ramping Darnley, and Ellis 'froughton is a
convincingly chivalrous Thomas Randolph;
but it is rather difficult to understand what
graces in the Rizzio of Frederick Hughes
attracted the Queen’s fancy, whilst Hubert
Curling, otherwise vigorous, shows an em-
barrassment about accepting his kissing

PALACE.
David the poet (Fred Hughes), is
threatened by Dan..'ey (John Cox).
opportunities which belies his description as
a man who would swashbuckle himself into
the Queen’s affection. Mary Beaton is
moderately done by Shirley Bavin ; and the
prolonged conversational effort of the pro-
logue is maintained by Harold Watch as
the sentimental old bachelor and John Marks
as the jealous husband.
A weakness in “Lady, Be Good!” has
been eliminated by the arrival of Alec Ivella-

Duncan Macdougall is doing well at the
Playbox, Rowe-street, with the present pro-
duction of “Juno and the Paycock”—Sean
O’Casey’s vivid little tragedy which, without
purporting to be anything more than a
character study, provides an incisive state-
ment on Iri.sli affairs. It deserves the full
houses it is getting at each of the Wednesday
night performances, for the play is remark-
ably well presented in spite of all stage dis-
abilities : Kathleen Grinin, as Juno, gives a
fine representation of the good-hearted
woman who is the one solid spiritual rock
amongst the quicksands of Irish tempera-
ment.

Sydney Tivoli management is satisfied
that it holds some good cards in the bright
Edith Clifford and the four dusky gentle-
men the programme christens “Emperors of
Harmony,” and there are no big changes
this week. The Ghezzy Brothers provide an
amusing turn. George Carney is on his
way and due to reappear on Saturday week
(27th}. and the management has registered
its opinion of his drawing power by opening
up the box plan for the first nights of his
season a fortnight in advance.

Student work:—
Amongst the mass of imperfection that goes to
make a student show, there are a few good samples
of achievement as well as promise in the exhibition
of work by the pupils of Julian Ashton at the studio in
Queen Victoria Building (Sydney). Amongst them is a
surprisingly lively nude, with, warm. flesh-tones, by
C. H. Higgins; the same suhject is handled much less
effectively by D. E. Insoll, who, however, shows
some very fair studies, especially in the drawing
section. AY. Dobell, whose nude study of a swords-
man is arresting, despite certain crudities, is one
of the exhibition’s outstanding pillars of strength;
J. N. Kilgour’s best work is a well-done portrait;
H. Badham has a bright harbor scene; and amongst
others meriting mention are F. W. Wentworth
Shields, D. R. Dundas, E. Berndt, B. AVeekes, T.
Porter, Jean Russell, H. AVilkinson and Gwen Ridley.
At Melbourne Royal on Saturday, "Tip
Toes," despite rumors that elsewhere it had
stirred even the daily-paper critics to faint
murmurs of disapproval, provided a packed
and jambed house with an acceptably-saucy
evening. True, it is quite as silly as 100 other
imported song plays, and the imported Gersh-
win music is just as like the anguish of an
imprisoned cat, and the exotic flavor of the
imported dialogue about wows and hicks is
much as usual, but the evening was moder-
ately jolly because of the charm of Thurza
Rogers; the solemn funniness of Cecil Kella-
way, enrolled to replace the laborious imported
Olsen and Johnson ; and the naive and native
humor of Gus Bluett. Of these, Thurza
Rogers, until recently a mime who danced
silently with Pavlova, was the most fetching.
She is no singer, but she has as much tune-
fulness as the Gershwin music deserves, and
the art with which she excused herself was
all-suflieing. Leo Franklyn contributed some
vaudevillean humor and Leyland Hodgson
gave prominence to his teeth in some role
or other. Some sort of conspiracy or plot
was also unrolled, and there was capable
work performed by a numerous chorus, but
nobody mattered much beside the local pro
ducts abovementioned. The Melbourne nre-
sentation was more than a bad adverftse-
ment for the Firm’s inveterate indenting
policy. It was a loud denunciation of it.
* * * *
The virtuoso season crawls wearily on in
Melbourne, and the procession o i inveter-
ate. music-lovers continues to toil up the
stairs of the Auditorium, where the critics
sit in yawning rows wondering where their
next adjective is to come from. The present
Avriter has a muddled recollection of 11
Paderewski concerts, nine Heifetz soirees,
eight Zimbalist events and seven Friedman
parties. He has drunk in 20 Bach fugues,
swallowed 49 Schubert-Liszt serenades and
of Chopin nocturnes has tossed down some
hundreds, including encores. He has gone
home night after night boozed with senti-
ment and tanked up to the ears with pro-
fundity and counterpoint. Having a mind
susceptible to low thoughts as well as high,
he has noted among other things that
Heifetz’s linen was superior to Zimbalist’s.
And now 7 that he lias to deal with Friedman,
the greatest intoxicant of them all, it annoys
him to find that all he craves is a prairie
oyster. The suspicion grows that this
musician-importing business is being over-
done.
$ $ $
The critical faculty of the Melbourne
dailies did what was expected of it and
rushed to arms over “Cradle Snatchers,” and
as a result the King’s has been packed to the
chandeliers ever since, while the thud of
investigating policemen falling out of their
seats with laughter can be heard two blocks
away. On the second night two detectives
attended, and on the third Chief Commis-
sioner Blarney went in person to make
certain for himself. Many other public
and semi-pubiic bodies, charged with the
upholding of private morals, such as the
United Social Questions Committee, are
anxious to know, the worst and are sending
representatives, so a long and prosperous
season is assured.

On August 30 at Melbourne Playhouse
the indomitable Fritz Hart will essay the
vast task of presenting Beethoven’s one and
only great if unequal opera, “Fidelio,” for
a short season. Jessie Brown w T ill sing
Leonora and Stanley J. Allen, who carries
the J.C.W. bouquet, will be her Florestan.
Greta Callow will be Marcellina and J. 11.
King will try his lungs on Roeco. Howard
Andrews, who was the mild Irish king of
the conductor’s own opera “Deirdre” last
year, will be that uninteresting scoundrel
Pizarro. Rather than produce “Fidelio"
most conductors would cheerfully take poison,
so the Hart venture is something of a
feather in Melbourne’s musical cap.

“The Sheik” is still the shriek at Mel-

stiffly behind no longer provokes derision.

Next Saturday’s most important happen-
ing in Melbourne will be the farewell to
“Rose Marie” and her fellow conspirators
at His Majesty’s, after which the theatre
will go into mourning for two days pre-
paratory to an invasion of “members of the
younger set” who will endeavor to amuse
with ;i non-sensational extravaganza en-
titled “The Patchwork Review.” Food will
be thrown or served to the audience after
the show for a moderate fee and there will
be dancing on the stage. As “Outward
Bound” draws to an end, applications for
berths at the Athenaeum tend to increase.

Melbourne art:—
It the next time you are at the theatre you notice
the lady in the row above giving you the glad eye
over two curiously-colored galleons in very sore
straits under some curly cliffs washed by the marcel
waves of a remarkable ocean, or keeping herself
cool with a Chinese mandarin who is pursuing a
singsong girl in a robe crawling with golden dragons,
you will know you are looking at the amazing
fan paintings of Geo. W. Neville. The paintings
are amazing because of the elegant assurance with
which the young man has caught the spirit of an
ancient and aristocratic fashion without ever leaving
Melbourne. ...Fantasy is the essence of the art, and
Mr. Neville, except in a few too realistic landscapes,
is consistently fantastic. His trees never grew any-
where but in his mind, and the pomp of some of his
decorations exceeds a mayoral banquet. If the
craft of fixing such exquisite trifles on to frames is
revived in. Australia, these pictures are certain to find
favor among connoisseurs. They are on offer at the
Fine Art Galleries till the 27tli.
Contrasts:—
There is all the world between the show of A.
E. Newbury at Melbourne Athenaeum and the parade
of Herbert Rose at the Sedon Galleries. Rose has
been hunting overseas and returned with the scalps
of Notre Dame, the canals of Venice, bazaars of
Tunis, and noted tourist resorts of the Cold Land,
delineated with sureness and solidity, sometimes with
poetic charm. Newbury has been browsing exclusively
amongst his familiar gums. A hasty inference would
Ire that Newbury’s is the least interesting and varie-
gated show, but the inference would be wrong. Rose
revels amidst Arab tarbooshes and Italian palaces, and
achieves a uniform picturesqueness that is sometimes
a trifle tiring to the inner eye. Newbury takes that
organ for a tour of inspection of the eucalyptus
architecture of the State, with a side glance at a
cow or a bit of sunlight on grey grass. His strong
sense of the drama of light and shade adds an
excitement to the rural landscape that even a Roman
ruin or a baron’s castle may not have. Incidentally,
his work looks more adventurous than of yore.
Rose’s beautiful pencil drawings are a striking feature
of his show.
The opening of “Rose Marie” at the Adel-
aide Royal has been postponed from August
20 to 28, to the natural annoyance of the
early booking birds, who have now to fit in
where they can. At the Prince of Wales,
Muriel Starr is handing out real Repertory
stuff in “The Eternal Magdalene,” really
a very bad dream of the might-have-been by
an elderly gentleman after a three-course
dinner. It takes a clever actress to make
the part convincing, but. Muriel manages it
with the assistance of the stage mechanist.
At the Majestic, Stud Foley and his Follies
are delighting the public with their Mirth-
quakes. The company includes a troupe of
amazing acrobats, who indulge in Maori
singing for a side-line, and Foley himself
scores both as comedian and dancer. At
West’s, “The Sorrows of Satan” shows the
diabolic figure flitting from English castles
to artists’ garrets in Piccadilly. With a view
presumably to retaining the atmosphere, the
supporting picture is “Hell-bent for
Heaven,” with Patsy Ruth Miller as the
star. Sport supplies the loudest note at the
W'ondergraph; in the star film. “Slide, Kelly,
Slide,” the hero is a baseballer, played by
William Haines, with Sally O’Neill in the
leading feminine role. The second film pre-
sents comedian Field, in “The Potters.” At
the York Theatre, one may gaze on “The
Beloved Rogue,” w r ith John Barrymore as
Francois Villon the fifteenth-century poet
and burglar.

“Hamfat” : The production of “Ruddigore”
is to be the big event of the Gilbert and
Sullivan co.’s return season in Sydney. Even
London has not seen much of this satire on
melodrama. After the original Savoy pro-
duction in 1887, “Ruddigore” was not staged
until 1921 —at the Prince’s Theatre. And
it has had only one revival since—in 1924.
“Flaming Youth’’: Dion Boucicault will
be back in time for the Melbourne Cup with
a bundle of new plays and "The Notorious
Mrs. Ebbsmith,” for Irene Vanbrugh. One
of Boucicault’s passions is for frequent
changes, insisted upon, to the despair of
managements, even when a piece is playing
to capacity—as was “Aren’t We All?” when
it was shelved in Melbourne. Boucicault
knows what he wants and sees that he
gets it.
“Cine Ma”: I don’t wonder that “The
Blue Mountain Mystery” (B. 11/8/’27) has
done w T ell abroad. This Australian picture
is short, sharp and finely dramatic. It sticks
to its point without unnecessary excursions.

“■Caul Boy”: Lunnon will see a curiously
contrasted pair when Dot Brunton and the
de-lib-er-ate G. B. Post open there in “The
Climax.” When Post first played “The
Green Goddess” in this country the prompt-
ers were all of a chilly sweat as to whether
his enormous pauses were part of the busi-
ness or due to his having forgotten his lines.
Impetuous Dot, on the other hand, treads
on the heels of her cues. There were head-
shakings over Dot’s decision to step out of
tune-shows into drama, but at rehearsals of
“The Climax” in Sydney even stagehands
muttered that she wasn’t bad, and that
meant that glory awaited her.

“Caul Boy”: A well-known Australian
actor, now in London, is kicking himself hard.
He signed a two-years’ contract at £2O a
week, and now his owner is farming him
out at twice that figure.
“Goldilocks”: George Palmer, the Surrey
Hills (Vic.) boy who started making pic-
tures when he was 15, is now, only two
years later, well established in the bush'ess.
“The North-bound Limited” was shown by
Hoyt’s in Melbourne, and Sydney is to see it
next month, with the young producer in the
prologue. Palmer nearly killed himself when
lie whisked a car across the front of an
onrushing express in Melbourne to get one
of the scenes. Not the least of his achieve-
ments has been his ability to borrow trains
and such trifles. Thelma Nelson, a daughter
of the N.T.’s voteless member, is his leading
lady.
“C. Oekney”: Henry Ainley and Norman
McKinnel, who have been engaged to appear
in Australia, are in the first class of English
actors. Ainley learned his business in the
legitimate, and was the leading juvenile of
the Benson co. when that Shakspearean
organisation was in its prime. He has de-
veloped into a character-actor of quality;
his performance of the old art-dealer in
“Quinney’s” was largely responsible for the
success of that comedy at London II ay-
market. Similarly, McKinnel’s vivid por-
trait of Old English in Galsworthy’s play
of that name, produced at the same theatre,
was the “draw.”
“Goldilocks”: A Mosman(Sydney)amateur
combination, the Fine Art Film Club, is
doing its bit to put Australian pictures on
the map. Taking “Caste” as the basis of
its story, and working in war details in
approved style, the club collected over a
hundred performers the other day to make
battle scenes at French’s Forest. The scenes
included an attack on marching troops by a
couple of fighting ’planes and an attack on
a military hospital. If the promoters add
skill to their enthusiasm the result should bo
worth seeing.
“J.F.P.”: In the film version of Marie
Corelli’s “Sorrows of Satan.” first published
in 1895, a couple are shown doing the
Charleston. And one of the young bloods
wears Oxford bags.
“Caesar’s Ghost”: When Nellie Stewart
was induced, after reviving “Sweet Nell” for
a charity matinee, to undertake a profes-
sional revival, doubt was expressed as to
whether her health would stand the strain.
However, she sailed through seasons in Mel-
bourne and Sydney with flying colors. Now
she has in mind a tour throughout Australia
with the play that has once again proved a
goldmine.
“Felix”: Bert Levy, Australian vaudeville
cartoonist, is leaving the boards when his
present Orpheum . circuit contract expires.
He tried his hand ac publicity for Metro-
Goldwyn-Mayer Films, and proved such a
success that he has decided to join the "puff"
manufacturers.

MANAGER: “You weren’t too clever last night — didn’t seem to

“Macess”: The Movie Film Commission
was told in Melbourne that a lip-reading
deaf and dumb girl saw a film heroine say
to the love-making hero (with the addition
of an offensive word) “Look out, you are
treading on my corns.”
The lovers, linked in hot embrace,
Now reach the tense, climactic scene,
Strained breast to breast, each glowing face
Alight with passion; on the screen
The caption reads: “My heart....it burns....
Ah, kiss me, love, on lip and brow”....
Voluptuously to him she yearns,
And whispers soft, “You clumsy cow!”
“I. Philps”: Those who remember Jack
Waller, the brisk little Hebrew of the But-
terflies Costume Comedy Co. in Australia
about 15 years ago, would blink if they met
him in London nowadays. Associated with
Herbert Clayton, the juvenile who assailed
the fancy of innumerable flappers when
appearing for the Firm and Meynell and
Gunn, Waller has become a leading manager
with two winners going well —“Prince
Charming,” revealing Delysia and George
Grossmith, at the Palace; and “Abie’s Irish
Rose,” with a complete Amurkan co. nasal-
ising the sugary sentiment of the piece to
big audiences at the Apollo.
“L. de R.”: Belmore is a familiar name
on the Australian stage. Wilson Barrett
brought out Daisy of the clan, also a young
brother, Paul. Another brother, Lionel,
arrived with Guy Bates Post. Alice Belmore
intended visiting Australia, but married and
stayed on the Yankee boards instead. Her-
bert and Bertha B. are now cradle snatch-
ing at Melbourne Kind’s. All are of the
brood of Sir George Belmore, once of Drury-
lane and now T deceased.
“Santander”: Little Beatrice Kay, who
gives her own piquant view of Weenie
Winter in “Sunny” at Melbourne Princess’s,
is making her first excursion into Australian
verse and has found the tour rather agree-
able. For a young New Yorker to find
delight in the swinging lines of "Banjo
Paterson seems rather unusual, but Beatrice
is an unusual young person. She is a
grand-niece of Samuel Gompers, late head
of the American Federation of Labor. Her
husband, J. Harvey Grattan, is collecting
material for a book on Australia’s political
and social problems.
“THE TERM OF HIS NATURAL LIFE.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, thumbed old book that I bought for
a song
Years ago, when the years were long
(So young was I!).
I flung on the counter my eighteen-pence
And ruffled your pages—and Commonsense
(The little sense of a lad of twelve)
Said: "Don’t be an ass, boy—why, you’ll
shelve
The book by night for a mouldy tale —
Now look at that boat you could easily sail.”
But your close-set type and your new-ink
smell,
Oh, stout little book, bewitched me well!
And now I am old, and the years are fleet
(So young was I!),
On the Silver Sheet
Is flung the tale from the thumbed old
book,
And the young ’uns who never will read
may look.
But I shall sit by the hearth alone
And throttle the song in the gramophone,
And turn my back on this “3LO”
(These modern inventions can bore one so!),
Nor ever think twice of a picture show....
Oh, a boy once more. I shall read, and pause,
And sorrow again with Rufus Dawes!
So a man shall keep to the young lad’s
dream
And vision the Thing as It Used to Seem!
S.A.
OLD FOGEY.
