Lolita.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
LOLITA, when the kissing is done
And you fret no more about frocks
and frills,
When the rose of love is a feeble thing
That Time with a rusty dagger kills.
I shall thank you still when we both arc*
grey
For the useless follies of yesterday!
Noons we wasted, that might have been
Vowed to toil that would bring us gold,
Nights we laughed—with a taste of wine—
When the moon broke out of her purple
fold
To make a shield for our reckless play
With the precious counters of yesterday.
Dawns we met with such tired eyes,
Blinking into the floods of light,
When the green earth rose from her dewy
sleep
Yawning out of her mist-coif white,
When carnival had begun to fray
And the grey moths fed on our yesterday.
But our moons came back, and our star shone
on,
And again we jested “There yet is time.”
As down the path of the middle years
We heard the bells of our youth-pact chime ;
It was wearing thin, but it still was gay,
The brijght web woven on yesterday.
Lolita, when the kissing is done,
And we come at last to the Long Good-bye,
I shall not forget how the dimples moved
In the cheek where only the wrinkles lie;
I shall hold you dear, when you’re cold and
grey,
For those madcap moments of yesterday !
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
The Letter.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT is quite apparent from this letter of
October 13, 1809, to Lord Castlereagh,
1 Secretary of State for the Colonies, that
Mr. Thomas Brown w r as very completely dis-
satisfied with things as they were in New
South Wales about that period. Reading
this remarkable communication these many
years afterwards, an impression remains that
either Mr. Brown was a very hopeless crier
of Stinking Fish, or that matters in the
neighborhood of Port Jackson really were,
to put it mildly, unsatisfactory. The letter
must be read in its entirety to be appreciated
properly. Here it is, under date of New
South Wales, 13th October, 1809:
My Lord,
It is high time that some fresh Governor should
have arrived here before this, as such doings was
never known —pardons to the worst of characters,
Croppeys and thieves. Here a man or woman robs,
next day turns King’s evidence, transports those he
sold the things for fourteen years, and saves himself.
Land—three thousand acres given to Mr. Riley, four
thousand acres to another favorite, .as well as eight
hundred Government sheep and 70 bullocks; not a
Government’s hutt (save six or seven) but is given
away to favorites. Forty thousand gallons of spirits
were given away to the civil and military officers
since Bligh has been deposed, and not anything to
the peaceable, industrious individual. The officers and
favorites have been finely enriched by this republican
Government. Johnston and the rest of the military
squad were not satisfied because he (Governor Bligh)
would not let them have as much spirits to trade as
old King did, so that made them determine to depose
him. Mr. Gore, the Provost-Marshall, transported;
Mr. Palmer, the Commissary, imprison’d; Cheese-
man, an old man, 80, hanged, because he had bought
stolen property. Mr. Gore would not plead, so
Judge (Capt. Kemp) said in the Court: “I transport
you, W’m Gore, like a damn’d rascall as you are,
for seven years to the Coal River!” This was a
fine sentence for a man wearing His Majesty’s com-
mission. Paterson gets drunk at Government House
at Parramatta, and Foveaux is left at Sydney to
do as he likes, and he gives pardons, grants and
leases to the whores and greatest thieves, till there
is nothing left for any other Governor (when he
arrives) to give. There is now two Lieutenant-
Governors here—Paterson—but F. is King and
supreme; P. is only a cypher. F. took people out
of church at Norfolk Island and hung them without
a trial, so he and his whore (A S ) were sent
here to inhabit Government House, whilst his Honor,
old Paterson, lived and got drunk in his barrack.
The Orphan House —a bawdy house ever since the
new master (Hoskins), his wife and three stout
boys (his sons), sent out from England to be its
master, and B W (a notorious street-walking
strumpet and a prisoner for life), put in by the
cobbler (Hoskins, the master) as a teacher, and the
former good master and mistress put out. This was
the business of the Rev. Samuel Marsden, the horse-
shoe maker; I mean King Marsden. And here is a
fellow, one Crook, a shopkeeper, a missionary, who
keeps a shop, yet was chaplain and read the absolu-
tion, and had more favor shown him than any
clergyman, for he was miserably poor; but these
two Honors and the two Lieutenant-Governors have
set this fellow (who was a gentleman servant) up
chaplain to the 102 —His Majesty’s loyal corps!
The officers sold their permits for spirits at £2 per
gallon, and let others draw it from the stores. The
Sinclair’s investment [goods by the Sinclair] the
officers and shopkeepers only had, and they sold the
check. Sieves drawn out at Bs. per sieve and sold
at two guineas each.
Oh, it has been charming times! One lady had
forty iron saucepans out of the above investment.
Hang half this worthy set and it will be justice,
for they have been the greatest robbers. -’s
woman, a Jewess, offered to sell, since he went,
1000 weight of kidney-fat—taken out of Government
bullocks —at 2s. 6d. per pound. This she saved during
his government. These are proper peculations, and
I hope such delinquents will be brought to condign
punishment.
1 have, etc,,
T. BROWN.
Such was good Mr. Brown’s pungent
comment upon the Australia of his day and
upon some of its leading lights. He could
hardly have been more sarcastically bitter
had he been a ratepayer of the City of
Sydney referring to the misgovernment of the
municipality by the party he did not belong
to in this year of grace 1927. But it is
doubtful if Viscount Castlereagh’s corres-
pondent would have expressed hmself so
freely had he known what he knew in twelve
months’ time.
* * * *
“Ah.” said the Rev. Samuel Marsden, re-
flectively, as he laid down a letter and its
enclosure upon the table of his study—“Ah,
Air. Thomas Brown? No doubt about it
whatever. ’Tis that damned fellow Tom
Brown, of Sackville Reach. It must be.
Well, 1 will attend to Mr. Brown. He needs
some attention. And he will get it. ‘The
horse-shoe maker/ indeed! The impudent
blackguard—we will show Mr. Brown Jus
place. ‘King Marsden !’ Well, well!
The Principal Chaplain of Ilis Majesty s
Territory of New South Wales was obviously
put out.' His red, pugnacious face expressed
annovance so unmistakably that his guest,
the Rev. Robert Cartwright, Assistant Chap-
lain at the Ilawkesbury, felt it incumbent
upon him to murmur a word or two of sym-
pathy. He was not altogether prepared
for the explosion that ensued.
“Dear me, my dear Mr. Marsden, lie said,
“pray what is the matter? Has Mr. Brown
been - beliaving in an unseemly fashion? Has
lie been presumptuous? I am so sorry, for
I had always considered him to be one of
our most worthy settlers —a really respect-
able and decent man. Pray, may I ask, what
has he done?”
“Damme, sir—yes,-*roared the great man,
taking up the papers again, and flourishing
them in Mr. Cartwright’s face. “Your respect-
able and decent Mr. Brown, I’d have you to
know, sir, is a rogue and a rascal —a con-
founded backbiting varmint, sir. He stands
in need of chastisement, sir. That he does.
And he shall be chastised. I will attend to
Mr. Brown in a way he won’t relish. The
impart’nent dog! I’ll teach him to call me
names—l promise him I will. Read that,
sir. ’Tis a letter from my friend Ned Cooke,
who is Under-Secretary at the Colonial Oflice,
in Downing-street. A very good friend of
mine. So good that lie hath thought it but
friendly to send me a copy of a lette# from
this d—d fellow Brown to my Lord Castle-
reagh himself. Presumptuous hound, that he
is! I’ll show him. I’ll instruct him in
seemly behavior. But read it, sir—read both
the letters. But read Edward Cooke’s first.
The infernal impudence !”
A little awed, Mr. Cartwright took the
letters and looked them over carefully. This
is what he read in the Under-Secretary’s
covering communication.
Downing Street,
December 12, 1809.
Revd. and Dear Sir, .
I take the opportunity of addressing to you a brief
communication concerning a letter recently received
by liis Lordship from a settler in New So. Wales wh.
appears to me to demand your attention. It is needless
to say that neither Viscount C.astlereagh nor your
very humble and obedt. servant attach any import-
ance to the expression of opinion referring to your
worthy self contain’d in the enclosure, but it appears
necessary to me, in view of the terms of friendship
existing between us, that you shd. be advised as to
the insidious machinations against yourself of such
a person as the writer of the enclos’d letter.
Pray convey my respects to Mrs. Marsden, and
Believe me to be,
Your h’ble and obedt. Servant,
EDWARD COOKE.
“There, sir—there, Mr. Cartwright! Wliat
d’ye think of that? What’s your opinion of
this outrageous fellow, now? Monstrous I
call it—damnably monstrous, that such people
should be permitted to hold land in the
colony!”
The Senior Chaplain banged the table with
a gnarled fist, and glared at mild Mr. Cart-
wright, as he handed back the two letters, as
if he held him in some way responsible for
the wickedness of Thomas Brown. Feebly
that gentleman attempted to pour the oil of
conciliation upon troubled waters that
seemed to threaten disaster to everything and
everybody happening to stand a chance of
being wetted by them.
“My dear Mr. Marsden, I can scarce credit
it. I know Mr. Brown well. One of the
most successful farmers at the Hawkesbury
settlement. No doubt, like many others, he
was disgusted lvith the extravagances and
injustices of the late usurping Government of
the Macarthur-Johnston faction. We know
the Major Foveaux has been Somewhat
highhanded—indeed, arbitrary—in many of
his actions, and, doubtless, ’tis his overbear-
ing demeanor which hath called for this pro-
test from Mr. Brown. But I feel sure—from
what I know of him —-that ’twould be Mr.
Brown’s furthest intention to impute to your
justly respected self any but the best of
motives in such steps as you may have taken
with regard to the Orphan School, or to
refer to you disparagingly. A mere slip of
the pen, my dear sir—l am convinced of
that. Pray do not trouble with”
Mr. Marsden bellowed his angry interrup-
tion.
“Fiddlesticks, sir! D —d nonsense! Hark
ye, Mr. Cartwright. I ride to the Green
Hills to-morrow to sit upon the bench, in
order to administer such justice as may be
needed in the distrct, and to arrest the
designs of evildoers. ’Tis in my mind that
your Tom Brown stands in sore need of cor-
rection —and I will, between now and then,
plan and devise means of correcting him. I
will teach him, sir, to allude to me, the
Principal Chaplain of the-colony, as ‘a horse-
shoe maker’- —to refer to me satirically as
‘King Marsden.’ I care not a rap what he
has to say to my Lord Castlereagli concern-
ing that scoundrel Joe Foveaux, or George
Johnston’s discreditable following—who, no
doubt, will be suitably dealt with by the
Home authorities. But he shall not trifle
with me, sir—with me. No, that he shan't.
We will depart hence at daybreak, Mr. Cart-
wright—and, for the love of Heaven, don't
anger me further by seeking to palliate the
grossness of Brown’s offending. I’ll not
stand it, sir—l’ll not stand it. People such
as this infernal agitator must be shown their
place—and Samuel Marsden’s the man to
show it ’em.”
“Indeed, my dear Mr. Principal, I do not
doubt you will take measures lo enforce re-
spect for your sacred oflice. But touching
Mr. Brown, I would most deferentially sug-
gest”——
“Hold your tongue, sir—hold your tongue.
You do not know your place, sir.”
* $ * $
No one who observed Mr. Marsden ride
across South Creek about nine o’clock on the
following morning, accompanied by the Rev.
Robert Cartwright, could fail to realise that
his Worship—the Hawkesbury was to know
him solely in his magisterial capacity to-day
—was in a very ill temper. The obsequious
greetings that he everywhere received from
farmers, soldiers, laboring prisoners and
officials were acknowledged in the most per-
functory fashion by the half-raising of his
hunting-crop to the brim of his broad black
hat. His usual jovial good-fellowship was
conspicuously missing.
“Gawd ’elp ’em all to-day,” muttered
Timothy Driscoll, the </hief Constable, as he

followed the dispenser of justice into the
courthouse. “They’ll surely need it!”
When the reverend gentleman took his seat
upon the bench the charge-book was handed
up to him by Mr. Driscoll, and, as was his
custom, he studied it for a few minutes be-
fore commencing the business of the court,
muttering comment upon the several names
that met his eye, with account of their
transgressions, in a fashion that was audible
enough to those in the front of the barn-like
chamber. Beyond the very slightest amount
of foot-shuffling and an occasional cough, the
silence in the room was fully eloquent of the
fact that all present completely realised that
they were in the presence of a Power —of a
very Jove-like Power —which was not to be
trilled with.
“Humph!” he grunted. “James Con-
cannon—one of those Irish Croppies. I
know all about you, my man. You’ll learn
not to be insubordinate. Mary Peters—ha!
In trouble with your husband again, are
you, Mrs. Peters? Your tongue needs curb-
ing. Joseph Pencarrow, Thomas Bray, Wil-
liam Toogood, Daniel Donovan —illegally at
large. The Coal River’s the place for you,
my gentlemen. Paul Morrison —murder. I
doubt not there’s a gallows awaiting you,
Morrison. Huh! A long list. Can nobody
enforce the law at the Green Hills? I must
do it, I suppose. We’ll see —we’ll see.”
He followed the list down with his stubby
linger, growling his comments as he read
the names of those with whom he was
acquainted, and murmuring caustic criticism
of their lives and characters. Suddenly, lie
stopped and glared at the record before him,
with a frown upon his face.
“Driscoll!” he presently exclaimed gruffly.
“Y’r Rev’rence I do humbly beg y’r Honor’s
pardon, I mean y’r WushUp. What’d please
for to be y’r Wushup’s pleasure?”
“Who are these men charged with cattle-
stealing, and under prosecution by Thomas
Brown. Which Brown’s that? Where does
he live, 1 mean!”
“ ’Tis Tom Brown o’ Sackville, y’r Wush-
up—down th’ river. \A free settler. Tlr fellies
lie’s after —they’re a bad lot, mostly from
roun’ about Cattai Crik. No doubt ’tis them
that did th’ job—no doubt at all. Bad ’uns,
they be, y’r Wusliup. Savin’ y’r Wushup’s
cloth, I’d be sayin’ there wasn’t a one of
’em was any dam’ good but to be transported
—mebbe ’ung. Proper gallus birds—all of
’em.”
“Ha! So they are prosecuted by Mr.
Thomas Brown, of Sackville—are they?
Humph ! Well —I hope the evidence will be
satisfactory. I am not over-disposed to rely
too much on Mr. Thomas Brown. I know
something of him. Is he present here in
court? Point him out to me, Driscoll.”
“If y’r Wushup pleases, Tom Brown’s not
here yet, y’r Wushup. He sent me word las’
night that lie couldn’t get in to the court
so soon’s ’twas opened—him bein’ mighty
busy in tli' early marnin’s a-musterin’ his
stock in tli’ ranges. Would I see to it that
th’ case was put well down th’ list, he
asks—an’ he’d be in court i’ th’ afternoon.
There's plenty o’ business for to fill hup th’
marnin’, y’r Wushup. ’Twould be obligin’
Mr. Brown for to hold tli' matter over till
he could attend hisself.”
“Oh, indeed, Driscoll —it would, would it?
Well, let me inform you, Mr. Chief Con-
stable, that ’tis your duty to consult the
convenience of the Court, rather than to set
up to oblige-witnesses who have been sum-
moned to attend upon a definite day and
have failed to do so. The Court is not to be
trifled with, Mr. Chief Constable —and you
should know that, if anyone should. Was
Brown summoned to attend here to-day?”
“Yes, y’r Wushup.”
"At ten o’clock in the forenoon?”
“He was, y’r Wushup.”
“And lie’s not here—-in contumacious de-
fiance of the law’s majesty. Ida! Very
well, We will take up the case now.”
“But y’r Wushup, th’ witnesses? Tliey’m
all Brown's men—an’s busy with him at
th’ must’riu’. There’s no one for to testify
about th’ cattle, y’r Honor-—not a one. Not
a witness agin th’ haccoosed. Wouldn’t it
be better, y’r Wushup”——
The Magistrate roared at the Chief Con-
stable, and that worthy officer shivered.
“Better — better? You forget yourself, Dris-
coll. Don’t let me hear such insubordinate
talk, my man. Don’t you presume, Mr.
Timothy Driscoll, Chief Constable, to advise
the Chairman of the Bench of Magistrates
of Parramatta and the Ilawkesbury in court
procedure—else ’twill be the worse for you,
my man. D’ye hear me, Driscoll?”
"Oh, indeed yes, y’r Wushup. I do baag
y’r Honor’s pardon. Will y’r Wushup take
the case now?”
“Most certainly I will. Let the charge
against the accused be read, and then call
each witness. If one be not here —then call
the next one. That is all —you may proceed,
Mr. Chief Constable.”
So Messrs. John Twomey, William Tar-
butt, Thomas Nelson and Abraham Golightly
were duly and sonorously informed that it
was charged against them that, on a certain
date mentioned, they did unlawfully conspire
together to feloniously steal the' cattle of
Thomas Brown, of this parish, against the
peace of their Sovereign Lord the King—or
words something like that, and to the same
effect. llow did they plead ? They pleaded
Not Guilty. Call the witnesses. And not
one of the witnesses appeared, although
Avithin and without the court, the welkin
rang with the bawling of their nomenclature.
Very indignantly, his Worship addressed the
prisoners.
“Now lookye here, my men. Ye arc charged
Avith a grave crime—one that, were it proved
against ye, might bring ye to the gallows. 1
make little doubt from your individual and
collective records but that ye are guilty.
But it cannot be proved against you. And
why not, pray-—why not? Because it hath
seemed good to the man whose cattle ye have
lifted to disregard his duty to appear against
ye. There is no evidence.' It irks me exceed-
ingly to do so—but I must discharge ye
Case discharged. Chief Constable, call the
next case.”
, So Messrs Twomey, Tarbutt, Nelson and
Golightly departed from the court, free men
—and made no delay about departing from
the town, which was soon, by order of Gov-
ernor Macquarie, to be known as Windsor.
No men ever had better right, as they
acknowledged gratefully amongst themselves,
to praise God for great good fortune. They
had stood face to face with death, and had
been snatched from the hands of tlie hang-
man by that stout apostle of the Prince of
Peace, the Reverend Samuel Marsden. Gawd
save ’im for a gentleman !
A little after mid-day, Chief-Constable
Driscoll approached the bench, and craved
Mr. Marsden’s indulgence,
“Well, wliat is it, Driscoll?”
His -Worship was in better humor now —
as had been well evidenced bv the leniencv.
not to say benevolence, with'which lie had
treated every malefactor and delinquent haled
before him subsequently to liis dismissal of
the cattle-duffers from Cattai.
“Please y’r Wushup—he’s here now.”
“Who is here? Pray explain yourself,
mv good Driscoll.”
"Tom Brown 0’ Sackville, y’r Wushup.
He's outside.”
Instantly the storm broke once more,
“Ha! Bring him in at once, Driscoll. At
once, d’ye hear me ! Arrest him, and bring
him before the Court. I will teach him a
lesson—one which will, I trust, not he lost
upon the inhabitants of this district. Bring
him in instantly, Air. Chief Constable. Ha!”
( There and then Air. Thomas Brown, of
Sackville Reach, on tlie Hawkesbury River,
found himself committed to the gaol at
Parramatta, for a term of seven days, as a
Punishment for Ins flagrant contempt of
Court.
“That, my man,” said Air. Marsden
severely, “will doubtless teacli you not to
presume too far. Yon have well deserved this
punishment—if all I hear of you from Eng-
may be believed. And I make no doubt
a b°ut that. You will be unlikely again, I
think, Thomas Brown, to treat at least one
magistrate of the Territory with contempt.
Take lnm away, Mr. Driscoll.”
J. H. M. ABBOTT.
All’s Well.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
WE were heading west on a cold winter’s
night. I was for Dubbo. He told
me he was for the other side of
Bofirke.
We talked, this chance acquaintance and
I, as only habitual travellers will and can :
I of shirts, socks, blankets, tweed and
ladies’ “fancies,” he of drought and flood and
sheep and men.
It seems his home was in the city, and
every year he did his round of the sheds;
shearing mostly, but sometimes driving for
the expert. He showed me a pile of cre-
dentials. They were as complimentary as
if he had written them himself. After I had
read a couple and pretended to read the
other thirty or so, he spoke of his family.
A few dozen daughters he had, it seemed, and
just one son. When he mentioned the sou
hi! looked guiltily at a parcel he had been
fondling and which was carefully placed be-
side him on the seat.
“Yes,” he told me, “one son ; and he’ll be
at Munyip to meet me. He is working there,
and this parcel’s a little present I bought
him. It’s cold at Munyip in winter, so I
got him a bottle of rum.”
Now I knew why he had treated the parcel
so reverently, and why occasionally a look
of martyrdom came into his face. He was
tempted, I could see, to tear the paper and
draw the cork. But he kept firm.
Later, however, lie asked if I thought rum
would be injurious to a young chap about
twenty.
I adopted a superior air and said: “Cer-
tainly.” I even quoted cases I had known
of young men, mostly twenty years of age,
being placed in lunatic asylums through
drinking rum. “If it doesn’t send them
mad,” I told him, “it certainly stunts their
growth or something.” '
My answer seemed to be disappointing.
"The boy’s over six foot,” he said, “so
stuntin’ wopldn’t matter much, would it?”
Plainly he wished I could have thought of
something serious.
As we drew nearer to Munyip he grew
more and more excited. The enthusiastic-
way he spoke of his boy made me wonder if
they would kiss when they met. I should
not have been surprised and certainly not
amused had they done so.
Meanwhile I saw by his face and furtive
glances that the war between his will and
the cold still waged.
At last the engine slowed, the brakes
screeched, the train stopped. It was Munyip
all right.
I looked across at my companion, who,
head out of window, was raking the plat-
form. Soon a massive overcoated youth was
standing at the door.
“Goo’ day, Dad,” he said.
“Good day. Son. How’s things?”
“Oh, aw right.’’
There was a long pause. Then: “Good
trip. Dad ?”
“Yes. Son. You all right here?”
“O.K. How’s Mum?”
“Splendid.”
“And the girls?”
“Good.”
After that flood of conversation there was
an embarrassing silence, till doors began to
slam, a bell rang, and a whistle blew. Then
the shearer, with agony in his face, impul-
sively grabbed the parcel and handed it to
the boy.
“A bit of a present, my boy,” he said.
‘And one for you,” said the lad as he
dropped a somewhat similar parcel on the
seat beside his father.
The train was moving now.
‘‘Good-bye, Dad,” said the boy.
‘ Good-bye, Son,” said the man, and then
sank back against the cushions.
“Gosh,” he muttered. “I’m glad I did;
but ain’t it cold!”
He glanced then at the parcel h ( » had been
given and felt it with his hand. His eyes
brightened and he felt it again.
Quickly he fumbled with some string
and undid it. Then he threw back his head,
his mouth ripped open across his face, and
he laughed.
On the seat by his side lay a bottle of rum.
N. E. STEEN.
